THE RED PAGE.
QUEST 10 XS.
By the sea, by the desert mid-
night sea.
Stands a youth.
His heart full of anguish, his head
full of doubt,
And with sullen lips he questions
the waves :—
" Oh, solve me the Riddle of Life,
The painful primordial riddle,
Which already has racked so many
heads,
Heads in hieroglyphic caps,
Heads in turbans and blackberrets,
Heads in wigs, and myriad other
Poor perspiring human heads :
What is the meaning of Man ?
Whence comes he ? Whither goes hef
Who dwells there above in the
golden stars ? ”
The waves murmur their everlast-
ing murmur,
The winds sweep, the clouds scud,
The stars glitter indifferent and
cold.
And a fool awaits an answer.
—Heine, trans. James Thomson.
THE POOR RELATION.
WE. ROTH, Q. Northern Protector of
Aborigines, is named a member of the new
Order of Merit instituted for Australian
• State servants. Roth’s work in guarding
our poor relation is wholly admirable. Q. legislation for
the aborigines is more humane and enlightened than that
of any other State ; and in Roth the responsible Minister
has found an enthusiastic administrator. The Northern
Protector’s Annual Report for 1902 (Govt. Printer, Bris-
bane ; 10 d.) may teach a lesson to Australian Govern-
ments. When will Premier James systematically protect
Westralian aborigines ?
Roth supervises mission stations and land reserva-
tions, but he has not sufficient reservations. It is
clear that the best that can be done for the
aborigines is to treat them as the U.S.A. treat the
remnant .of Indians—segregate them entirely from the
whites oh considerable areas of land devoted to their
use, where an attempt can be made to civilise them
slowly. Mean whites are among Roth’s greatest
obstacles. The Northern aborigine has clearly-defined
white uses. He is often a valuable servant—on a stock
station, on a pearling schooner, or attached to an hotel;
and it is easy to cheat him of his wages. She is useful in
the kitchen or to mind the children, or for prostitution.
The Northern white takes all the profit se can from the
Northern aborigine, and, aided by the Northern J.P.,
bitterly resents the efforts of the Protector to abolish
black slavery. One trusts that justice between blacks
and whites will be done mere faithfully in' 1903 than it
was done in 1902.
BINGHP’S LITTLE JOKES
“ Justice "—no more : our black brother is a jolly
chap, and deserves fair play ; but, under semi-civilised
conditions at least, he is no paragon. He acquires our
vices easily, and easily relinquishes his pristine virtues.
There is a certain humour in his misdeeds —perhaps
Binghi is “ the missing Australian humourist” that The
R. of Reviews is looking for. When he is coddled he
grows cheeky. Last year 1 ‘ one had the impertinence to
offer Rev. Schwarz to do some stealing of other people’s
cattle on the Cape Bedford Reserve for him ’’—Schwarz
being his missionary guide and friend.
And Binghi is not always to be browbeaten or back-
beaten. Twice, last year, he attempted to steal his
employer’s beche-de-mer lugger. “ On 23rd February two
Japanese were thrown overboard from the lugger
Hiroshima, but they managed to swim to the Piper
Island lightship.” “On 13th March, while the ketch
Specie was getting under way near York Island, the
aboriginal crew, six in number, threw overboard the
Japanese master, Tomita, and a Japanese passenger,
Wakamatou.” This shows what you might call a proper
naval spirit. Binghi could go looting in China with the
worst of us. Like ourselves, he is true Australian :he
would thrust forth the Jap.;—and why should he not be-
come “ the nucleus of an Australian navy ” ?
When it is added that at Yarrabah mission station,
Cape Grafton, there is a seventh Australian Government,
consisting of twelve aboriginals elected annually by adult
suffrage, “the only qualification of the candidates being
that they must be church communicants” ; and, that
Cabinet meetings are presided over by the “ King,” who
is by hereditary right chief of the local tribe : “ it is he
who signs the minutes ” I—food is given for deep philo-
sophical meditation.
ABORIGINAL LEGENDS
Then, Roth has another side —the scientific.
No more valuable contributions than his have been made
to aboriginal ethnology. Superstition, Magic, and Medi-
cine (Govt. Printer, Brisbane; 3«.) is the title of
his fifth “ Bulletin.” There the aboriginal is studied
in his downsittings and his uprisings, and is asked
“Why?" He responds with myriad local legends
which, we regret to say, display rather shallow fancy
than high imagination. Yet our brother is quaint
and interesting in his primitive way. He does not bother
much about the Causes of Things : the first explanation
to hand will satisfy him : like a sensible fellow he
troubles himself chiefly about the pleasing or troubling
Effects “Felix qui potuit,” in his notion, is an exploded
notion ; and at the head of this page we quote a verse of
Heine’s for the pleasure of saying that it does not apply
to the Australian aboriginal. His simple faith ends with
the Final Worm, as his simple diet depends upon the fat
one. Roth says that, generally speaking, he does not even
believe that he will “jump up white fellow. What he
explained to “ Durrumboi ” and others was probably not
that after death his actual body would become white,
but that the vital principle or spirit is re-incarnated
in the white man. And Roth adduces seven aboriginal
dialects in which the same word does duty for a Euro-
pean and a deceased aboriginal’s spirit or ghost.
But you do not know why there is a new moon every
month 9 Well, in the Boulia district, a long time ago,
the blackfellow was a turkey and his wife was a cockatoo.
And he hurt his foot. The foot swelled, and poor turkey
was in great trouble. He asked the green parrot for
medical assistance, thinking that the parrot might l&nce
the swelling with his beak and relieve the pam, but the
parrot was no ambitious unlicensed practitioner, and
declined to undertake the operation. Then turkey
applied in turn to the crow, a medicine-man, an eagle-
hawk, the moon (“a whitefellow doctor, said the
humourous aboriginal narrator), and even to the Evil
Spirit; but they all refused.
THEREY'S EXTREMITY
Poor turkey was desperate. As a last chance,
he 'begged the earth-worm to see what he could do ; and
fortunately the earth-worm was Barkis. He bit into tne
swollen flesh, bored his way deep into the diseased part,
sucked out all the putrid matter, and cured the patient.
Hooray! ...
So there was held a great corrobboree of rejoicing ; and
the Governor Carrington of the epoch said, “ Let ’em all
come ! ” There was none of your nonsense with blue and
white tickets. The galahs came, and the storm-birds,
white cockatoos and black, butcher-birds, magpies, and
bower-birds, opossums, bandicoots, and porcupines—
they all came. The clouds and skies came too, for at
that time they lived on the surface of the ground
like everybody else. (Of course, it was a long time ago.)
“ And while the turkey and the earth-worm, together
with the clouds and skies, shifted their position . . . the
whole party began singing, ‘ There goes our brother
up !
(It might have been a modern pantomime, though it
did happen such a long time ago.)
And, of course, when they got up, they stayed up, says
the aboriginal story-teller. “ But so that the people
should always remember what a good medicine-man he
had proved himself to be, the earth-worm sends a new moon
regularly every month to bear him in mind ; for the moon
is a brother of his, and, like him, bores his way out of the
ground, rises up on high, sinks once more and dies. As
he has plenty of brothers he sends along a different one
every month.”
(Naturally these brothers have varying dispositions,
and thus it'is that Russell is enabled to base a drought
theory on the different inclinations of the moon.)
MORE MOON STORIES
But the Australian aboriginal, like the gum-
tree, hates the rut of habit. The great aboriginal moon-
myth includes a whole series of little moon-myths—you
might almost say, one for every district. Round Princess
Charlotte Bay the moon became our moon qhite other-
wise. As at Boulia, he used to live on the ground (he was
a masculine, German kind of fellow). Then... a long
time ago, two brothers—sparrow-hawks, they were—
went out hunting for “ sugar-bag.” They came across a
likely tree and one of them cut a hole, put his arm in—<
and got jammed! The deuce! He pulled, and his
brother pulled, but the arm was stuck as tight as a M.P.
in his seat, and the tree strongly opposed any deduction
of members. The perplexed brother went to look for
help, and fodnd out what rotten reeds friendship and
relationship may be in trouble-time. One after the other
made excuses, and the sparrow-hawk was just about to
express his contempt in primitive gesture when he spied
his mother’s brother, the moon, sitting on the ground
some distance away.
A GOOD NATURED LUNATIC
Ha! the moon vras a good fellow. On the difficulty
being explained, he immediately rose and accompanied
bis sister’s son back to the place where brother was
stuck. He climbed the tree, and reaching the top, bent
bis head well down into the hollow, a.n&—a-TISH-oo!
gave a very violent sneeze ; the pressure on the air from
the inside thus releasing the poor fellow’s arm below,
which was now easily extricated.
The released sparrow-hawk was, however, determined
on revenging himself upon the crowd of people who,
through their callousness, might hare allowed him to
perish. So, having first of all buried the moon under*
ground to keep him out of harm’s way, he set fire to the
grass with the intention of burning the whole camp out,
but some of the occupants managed to make their escape.
He thereupon started another fire, and this time put the
moon on the topmost branches of a high tree ; and again
some of his intended victims got away in safety.
But the sparrow-hawk was not going to be thwarted,
and having placed the moon high up in the sky, out of
all danger, he made such a tremendous conflagration that
on this occasion none of the camp-blacks escaped. And
this is how the moon regularly comes to take up his abode
in the heavens—out of the way of the bush-flres.
’T is the most ingenious aboriginal legend we have seen.
But there are many, many others.
At Cape Bedford the moon is the husband of the sun,
and at new moon he is starving, so he goes Ashing. And
he has luck and no lies : see how his belly is gorged at
full moon ! But his wife is always travelling westward—
she’s after the green ants ! You can see her start; but
at night she circles round by the south. There are really
two suns : in the cold season the frigid elder visits us,
and in the warm season the amorous younger.
Our delightful poor relation!
THE VOLATILE SEX
A story of domestic trouble, from the Normanby river :
“ A long time ago two women—a parrot and a water-
snake—were sitting at the water’s edge in a very discon-
tented state of mind. The fact is, they had got tired of
their husbands, two big eels, and wanted a change. In
response to their solicitations, a big eagle-hawk accepted
all responsibilities, and gratified their wishes. In course
of time each woman bore a boy, which was duly brought
to the hawk’s camp for the father to fondle and caress.
But when he looked at them, and recognised their hybrid
constitution, he could not refrain from exclaiming,
‘ Piccaninny no belong me,’ —though all inquiry amongst
his friends as to whether any of them had been tampering
with his two women proved fruitless.
“ He therefore sent the ladies away on a yam-hunting
expedition, and in the meantime took the two boys with
him for the ostensible purpose of catching opossums.
Coming to a likely tree—a comparatively hollow one—
the hawk sent the llads up and told them to climb into a
certain hole, which he pointed out to them some- con-
siderable way up. As soon as they had disappeared, he
took a tomahawk and made a hole low down in the butt,
and stuffing grass therein, set it alight, and roasted the
poor boys to death. So that suspicion should fall upon
the shoulders of others, he stuffed their bodies with
native onions to give them the appearance of being plump
little opossums ; and then, putting the corpses into dilly-
bags, hung them on a branch well out of sight.
“By this time the mothers had returned, and, in reply
to their inquiries as to where their children were, the
eagle-hawk told them that they were playing behind the
trees with their grass-spears. The parrot and snake
naturally went to see for themselves, and to their grief
and dismay recognised all that was now left of their
offspring. But they got their revenge that night: they
made a big nest for him, composed of sticks and leaves,
and as soon as the hawk fell asleep set it on fire. The
snake then took to the water permanently, only stretch-
ing her nose out occasionally to get the news from the
parrot, who is still poking among the holes in the tree 3
in the hope of getting tidings of her dead children.”
A RECIPE FOR WHISKERS
Roth’s bulletin has scores of similar legends : it
is a mine of golden lore. On every page we see what a
quick eye our aboriginal brother has, how truly he
observes, how just are his epithets. Into his pharma-
copoeia we cannot enter : he has specifics for most of the
ills his flesh is heir to. Yet one remedy from the Pros-
erpine river will be useful:—“Young men can make their
whiskers grow quickly by rubbing over the surface the
teeth from a carpet- or black snake.” There 1
♦
“The Tired Australian” who aaks “Is Aus-
tralian Humour Extinct ? ” in April if. of Reviews shows
no sign of fatigue in his style. The hairy hands of
anonymous Esau do not avert suspicion from the editorial
Jacob. The article reads as if written less in sincerity
than f o’-sensation. “The Tired Australian” professes
not t have read the books included in his sweeping
negative. “He is told” that On Our Selection almost
succeeds in being amusing ; but, of his own knowledge,
he knows nothing. “He has a vague notion ” that Ethel
Turner makes valiant efforts in the direction of humour ;
“ but, then, he has never read one of her books.” Was it
worth while displaying this statue of Condemnation on a
pedestal of Ignorance ? “ There are no snakesin Iceland.’
“ There is no humour in Australia.” Easily said ; but to
what end ? If the writer’s inquiry was made seriously,
why supply an answer admittedly lacking evidence!
If it was made humourously, why was the humour
omitted by this apostle of humour?
“LADY DOSE’S” DULLNESS
In Lady Rose s Daughter Mrs. Ward conjures
up a set of British patricians wedded to high concerns of
literature and art; neglecting court gossip, balls, Hyde
Park, the opera, cricket ; indifferent to house parties,
grouse shooting, fox hunting, betting, whist, billiards,
the play of the season ; thoughtless of the next “ drawing-
room,”' the latest “fad,” the worst divorce case; for-
getting tenants’ rents, bye-elections, Henley, the Derby,
walking tours on the Continent, charitj' concerts, bazaars,
The Morning Post, and Punch. It would be untrue to
say that none of these subjects is alluded to ; but they—
and others germane—are all treated as unvital. To say
that is to say enough.
Besides—a salon in Mayfair under the sceptre of a
“ lady’s companion ” ! A gathering of gifted, clever
people—as the authoress reports them —whose conversa-
tion is dull and mostly confined to family affairs ! ’Tis a
peculiarity of this book : at Lady Henry’s house the talk
is alleged notable for “ brilliance ” ; there is “ a thrill in
the air,” as well there might be if Mrs. Ward’s per-
sonages kept Mrs. Ward’s promises. But they do not.
Their reputed erudition, their knowledge of the insides of
books, their understanding for art, their experiences of
travel, their acquaintance with important events, their
brains, their tastes, their temperaments, their tongues
would presumably demand vent. Yet they keep all their
wisdom and wit locked up in their bosoms. Though Mrs.
Ward would persuade us that her people think like
Chesterfield and Madame de Stael, we discover that they
talk like poor Poll. Montresor’s few bright sallies near
the beginning of the book, Lord Lackington’s brief
observations on pictures at Burlington House, and the
two dozen lines about Shelley in Chapter XII, are
exceptions proving the rule. In fact, the people in
Lady Rose’s Daughter are much more serious and solemn,
given far less to pleasant raillery and playful repartee,
than the real inhabitants of Park Lane, Baton Square, or
“ the demesnes that there adjacent lie.” This novel
wants editing by Anthony Hope.— Lionel Strachey, in
The Lamp.
[Which touches the spot. But Strachey’s misapplication
of Rosaline’s famous “demesnes" (Rom. and Jul., ii, 1
reminds us that if Edward Lloyd had quoted correctly
the point of his mot would have been blunted. Gray, ir
the “ Elegy,” wrote “the noiseles| tenor of their way’
—and one can scarcely conceive a leading tenor noiseless
for thirty years.]
THE AESTHETIC PULSE
There is some quality for which we have no name,
whose essence pervades all true art, whether it takes
the form of music, painting, or poetry, and the ®sthetic
sensations aroused by the contemplation of these works
of art havs this same undefinable quality common to
them all. . . . Perhaps it is not altogether fanciful to say
that through various works of art the same pulse of
esthetic life may be traced, and if this is the pulse which
beats in the contemplator himself, the same quality of
thrill will be felt in the case of each symphony, poem, and
picture. ... .
As an example of what is meant, the same quality of
ecstasy is given to me in reading the final lines of
« Othello,” in hearing the third movement of Beethoven’s
“ Fifth Symphony,” «*d in looking upon lionardo’s “ La
Gioconda" in the Louvre.
What person, knowing thoroughly the work of Swin-
burne, could fail to see that the spirit of his poetry has
been thrown into musical form by Tschaikowsky ? The
Andante Cantabile of his Symphony in E minor is the
very wordless expression of the chapter “ Iseult at
Tintagel ” from “ Tristram of Lyonesse,” while the wailing
downward sweep of the violins in that heart-rending
passage in the opening Allegro is a musical transcription
of the impassioned lines beginning
Dear, dost thou see now, dost thou hear to-night,
Sleeping, my waste wild speech, my face worn white,
Speech once heard soft by thee, face once kissed red !
And the sombre and stately Polish death-march which
appears in every movement bespeaks the love of this
luckless pair—the love which has its fulfilment in death.
None shall say,
Here once, or here, Tristram and Iseult lay :
But peace they have that none may gain who live ,
And rest about them that no love can give,
And over them, while death and life shall be,
The light and sound and darkness of the sea.
There is the same passionate rebellion, the same tropical
luxuriance of phrase in the musician and the poet. . .
I do not insist upon the correlation of musical and
poetical passages ; in the case cited of Swinburne and
Tschaikowsky the resemblance was so palpable that it
would be impossible to forego mention of it. What Ido
insist upon, and feel convinced of, is the fact that similar
“ esthetic pulses ” which give rise to the same quality of
ecstasy in sympathetic observers are frequently to be
found in many works of art; a great proportion of the
art of Swinburne possesses the same quality or aesthetic
pulse as that of Tschaikowsky, much of the art of
Beethoven the same as that of Shakespeare...ln the
feeling wrought upon the reader, in the aesthetic atmo-
sphere created by Keats’s “ Hyperion ” and “ Endy-
mion ” and more by “ The Eve of St. Agnes,” we recognise
that of Schubert’sjjnfinished symphony in B minor, the
colossal work in C major, and the ,l Rosamunde” music.
In the case of Brahms, the first movement, as well as
the scherzo of the I) minor symphony, has the aesthetic
pulse of Tennyson rather than of any other writer, but
the atmosphere of his work as a whole is that of Words-
worth. There are the brilliant flashes of genius shining
out of a dead grey level of monotony ; there are whole
pieces of the highest merit, such as the clarinet quintet
and the Requiem, reaching, and sustaining, a level almost
exactly in harmony with parts of The Excursion and
of the immortal “ Ode.” —E.L.A., in St. Jamess Gazette.
BOOKFELLOW’S MOMORANDA
Booksellers in Cootamundra have fine sport at
the expense of innocent reporters who run round in
search of “the best-selling books.” The interviews
usually happen like this— , T
Mr. Boggs, of the popular firm of Boggs, Coggs &
Doggs, said that they found the three best-selling books
at present were first,' A Seventeenth-Century Sentiment,
which was really a remarkable novel, and was causing
great excitement among local readers ; second, G. W.
Puddock’s Kylie Kailyardie ; and third, The Red
Hair of Queen Elizabeth, by an anonymous author.
(You can get A Seventeenth-Century Sentiment from
Boggs, Coggs & Doggs only. Their agent has pur-

The Mercure de France recently sought opinions
as to Germany’s current influence on thought and art—
and found, even in England and America, little but dis-
paragement. Quote Ugo Ojetti as fairly representing
the mass—
Goethe, Heine, Nietzsche are admirable because they
have ranged themselves in opposition to their own
environment —because they have felt or declared them-
selves anti-German, in an intellectual sense at least.
For the rest, look at Art in Germany. Alas! in
Goethe’s country you findjihat Emperor William is tie
dull arbiter of public taste. What contemporary
German novel has gained the world-renown of any
volume whatever written by Tolstoy, by Anatole
France, by d’Annunzio, even by Kipling—not to men-
tion dead authors such as Flaubert, Maupassant, or
Zola?
On the stage, Sudermann is a mediocre Augier whom
the ostentatious display of a cheap philosophy has
made heavy and presumptuous. Hauptmann, when he
is not a realistic dialectician as in Fuhrmann
Hentschel, is a plagiarist of Ibsen.
In painting, whom can you see to fill the place of
Boecklin and Leibl—dead ; of Mentzel—old ; of Lenbach
—who is sick? In any international exposition the
German galleries are without illumination and without
a public.
We Latins can shake hands in looking to the future.
In another half-century, the Latin genius will still
dominate the world. Work !
“In Ireland,” wrote a Saturday Review-vv in a
flashing apothegm, “ all vitality is vocal.” One might
almost say that in France all vitality is literary. Thanks
to the inheritance of a refined language, the aid of a
thorough system of instruction, the stimulus of the Latin
intelligence, nearly everybody writes, even those who
are not writers. We speak of Paris, naturally, and as if
Paris were France —which it is not. Yet the comment
applies to France, when France has the opportunity.
Even in Brittany, France's most illiterate, most drunken,
most religious province, the alien Celts react to neigh-
bourhood, and grow towards kinship.
This is by way of explaining why a sculptor like Rodin
talks and writes interestingly. Writing is not his busi-
ness ; yet when he writes he says things, and says them
deftly. Recently he was discussing the public ignorance,
the decadence of public taste in matters of art, and advo-
cating the remedy of a return to Nature, and especially
of a return to Work :
We no longer know how to work. Work to-day,
whatever its nature, really tends only to the diminu-
tion of effort. But the Beautiful discovers itself only
at the price of persistent toil.
People no longer love, no longer comprehend the
Beautiful, because they no longer know how to labour.
To remedy the general ignorance of the fine thing,
the thing to he desired and extolled, we must restore
to every individual the sense of Work, the love of
Work. Through labour deliberate as the Earth’s own,
through patience as long as hers, we shall one day
come to a Spring of Art as glowing and spontaneous as
the Spring which her slow toil is always preparing.
Moral: The so-called Australian “artists” never work,
in the sense that they make work their recreation, their
sole occupation. When they begin, we may get an
Australian Rodin. Until they begin, we shall get the
Australian Court ”at N.S.W. Gallery sacred to framed
feetuses of Art!
A goodly number- of Lady Cook’s (Tennessee
Claflin’s) sixpenny books of essays, mostly on sex-
subjects, have been sold in Australia. Before Eng. Chief
Justice last month, the lady’s secretary gained an action
for slander against her, the verdict carrying
damages. Then Lady Cook rose in eruptionl am a
sorrowful woman. I have trusted myself in the hands oi
judge and jury. T have done more for England and
America than anybody else has ever done, and my repu-
tation is ruined. Oh, cruel, cruel men !

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lulu.
Lulu, my queen,
Fairer than waters strewn
With pearls and stars and sea-flowers pale
between—
Child of the Moon.
Rest your tired head upon my shoulder — so,
And let the red fire’s sheen
Make changing glories in your proud eyes glow,
To fade, and light, and blaze, and pass, and go—
Lulu, my queen.
You laugh so low.
With you all sounds sigh “ Peace.”
The sea-winds gather strength to thrash and blow,
Then, sighing, cease.
The world rolls on ; the clock-bells chime and toll
To tell the hours that go.
I hear no bells ; the world may spin and roll.
There are such songs of beauty in my soul —
Sung low—so low.
Yet this I know :
You love in earnest jest;
And you speak low since love-words spoken low
Win kisses best.
Your heart beats true so long as it is true.
Your fancies flit ; and so
I crave no lasting favors as some lovers do.
The struggling truth ; the laughing fickleness in
you—
Ah ! yes, I know.
Your soft, red lips
Make whispering voices sigh.
The sea-storms leap to flog the plunging ships,
Then, moaning, die.
The world rolls groaning ; but I hear it not.
Eacli hour stalks past and slips
In silence to the land of things forgot.
I only know your lips are sweet and hot—
Ah ! cruel lips.
Lulu, my queen,
Sweeter than morning skies;
What jests and tragedies have I not seen
In your dark eyes ? . . .
Outside, the rain and tempest scream and blow-
inside, the red fire’s sheen
Makes love-lights in those proud eyes gleam and
glow.
Oh, God ! that I should come to love you so !
Lulu, my queen.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
Sleep.
Bring blossoms, red and gold,
That I may wreathe her hair;
Also a rose-red shroud,
That I may swathe my Fair.
Bring blossoms, red and gold,
That I may warm my Sweet ;
And fold in rose-red shroud
Her little, clay-cold feet.
Midst blossoms, red and gold,
Lay her warm and soft away
In waves of rippling sunlight—
She hated shade and grey.
Midst blossoms, red and gold,
She sleeps ; she did not die.
One kiss—the last—my Sweet,
My own flower-love, good-bye !
N.B.W.
ELISE ESPINASSE.
Love Repulsed.
Love came knocking at my heart,
Tapping—softly, gently tapping.
But I heeded not the smart
Of the saucy urchin’s rapping ;
Closed the entrance to my heart.
“ Run away 1 ” I said, “ I’m napping.”
But he paused. “ Begone !” I cried,
“ Saucy imp ! Seest not I’m sleeping ?
Cease thy clamor ! Hast no pride,
That a beggar thou comest creeping ? ”
But still he tapped, and I denied,
Till Love turned slowly, sadly weeping.
Then, repenting at his grief,
‘ ‘ Come back,” I cried, ‘ ‘ Grieve not so sadly.
Come fill my empty heart, sweet thief!
Ah, truth to tell, it’s aching badly !
Come back. I’ll hold my heart in fief
For thee, O Love ! I want thee madly ! ”
“ Alas 1 ” he sighed, “ Too late, too late !
Love, once repulsed, comes not again.”
“Yet stay 1” I pleaded. Ah, sad fate !
“ I may not stay !” he so! bed in pain.
“ Farewell, farewell! ” He closed the gate
And vanished. “ Love comes not again ! ”
JILL.
Song.
Such sets of sun, such dawning days
To struggle through ; such weary ways.
Such weary ways 1 The ways are drear
When lovers part. How creeps the year 1 . .
How burns the sun ; how chill’s the dew ;
How cold’s the moon ; how mocks the blue.
How mocks the blue ! The blue mocks me,
It is so dark, apart from thee.
I only know one song, one tune ;
I sing one song from June to June.
From June to June ! Such dawning days
To struggle through—such weary ways !
Such weary ways / The ways are drear
When lovers part —how creeps the year !
V.

Morbid Months of Misery.
“A DESPAIRING WRECK OF HUMANITY."
A Great Friend in Time of Need.
Tf\e Case of l/|r. H. BARNETT,
(bt a special reporter. )
lb is worthy of note that the interviews
which have from time to time appeared in
these columns are singularly free from mere
theorising about the subjects dealt with,
and are entirely confined to practical, though
none the less interesting, details, an addition
to which is made by Henry Barnett, of No.
212 James-street, Leichhardt.
“To show what important events some-'
times arise from trivial causes,” said the
gentleman mentioned, “ I must inform you
that one day I scratched my elbow with a
piece of zinc, which was hanging loosely in-
side a waggon. It was nothing to grow
alarmed about, I thought, and 1 took no
notice of it until the next day, when my
arm appeared to be slightly inflamed, but I
did not take any precaution to stop it get"
ting any worse. Not till a week afterwards
did I bother myself, and by that time my
arm was so much swollen, and the pain was
so intense, that I could not go any longer
without seeking some advice.”
“ Where did you go for it I” ths repor-
ter enquired.
“ To the hospital, and on three successive
visits my arm was operated upon. It was
then that I realised the great disaster which
had overtaken me, as the doctor said I had
just come in time to save losing my life from
blood poisoning, so by that you will know
thkt I was in a dreadful state.”
“ Were you able to attend to your busi-
ness ? ” ■
“ Work of any description was beyond
my powers for nearly four months, for my
constitution was fairly broken up and I was
almost helpless. This being the first illness
I ever had, you may depend that I felt my
misfortune very keenly, and the thought
that I would never be able to use my arm
again nearly drove me frantic. I sat and
brooded over my troubles till my mind
lapsed into a chronic state of despondency,
and although the doctor said I had come
‘ just in time,’ I did not feel so sure about
it, as I felt so generally weak and wretched
that I doubted if I could survive the terrible
calamity which had occurred. For five
days and nights I did not eat a morsel of
food, although I tried hard enough to take
a little nourishment, but I could not swal-
low what I put to my mouth, and this, to-
gether with the agonising pains I was
suffering, made a thorough wreck of me.
My cruel tortures would not allow mo to
sleep, neither did I know which way to lie
for ease, so that when daylight came I was
glad to get up in spite of having scarcely
sufficient strength to walk about, for if I
aid Tysake an attempt my head turned so
giddy that J. st;t>n had to sit down again.
My wife was kept going riyht and day bath-
ing and bandaging my arm, but even the
great care I received and the doctor’s medi-
cines combined could not succeed in elimi-
nating the poison tivhich prevented the
wound from healing. 1 was completely at
the mercy of my original complaint, which in
time made my recovery more improbable,
because of the ruin it wrought on my in-
ternal organs, and as ailment after ailment
was piled up I saw that my prospects were
more gloomy than ever. My nerves were
always on the quiver, which showed that
the strain on my system was too great for
them, and I felt particularly nervous on
hearing any noises, which also grated on my
ears and made me extremely irritable. I
could see that it was no good taking any
more doctor’s medicines, as I was getting no
benefit from them and they made me feel
sick; besides, they did not help me in re-
gard to my appetite, so I decided on pur-
chasing some Clements Tonic.”
“ How did that idea originate ? ”
“Well, I had taken Clements Tonic to
give me an appetite once before when I was
feeling a bit jaded, and it made ms eat like
a, horsa ; and now that I was bo feeble, and
run down a* low as I could be, I thought
Clement* Tonic might do the same again.
And, upon my word, it did ; but for a while
I was sorry that Clements Tonic made me
eat so heartily, as my digestive organs were
in such a shockingly bad state that all food
disagreed with me. My stomach always
got filled with wind, and my chest pains
were so weighty that I could scarcely get
my breath, while the awful pains 1 had
hitherto been maddened with were added
to by something going wrong between my
shoulders. The taste in my mouth and the
furred condition of my tongue testified to
the disordered state of my liver, but the
aches in my limbs and across my loins told
the direful tale that my vitality was per-
fectly exhausted. When I come to look
back upon those weeks of abject torture and
misery, when I could not even read for the
swollen feeling in my eyes, with no anima-
tion in my body, and with no hope left, I
consider it a of
Providence which influenced me to perse-
vere with Clements T,onic, by the aid of
which came a delightful experience that
broke the morbid monotony of all those
months. When the first bottle of Clements
Tonic was finished I was sleeping more
soundly than I had done for some time past,
the reason being that there was a decrease
in the pains which had kept me awake so
consistently, and how thankful I was for
the rest, too ! To find that I could obtain
relief from Clements Tonic gave me a fresh
interest in life, although I did not antici-
pate throwing off my afflictions altogether,
as they had held out so obstinately against
other medicines ; but I was grateful for all
mercies, and when the discomforts following
my meals were lessened you can imagine
my great surprise. Little by little my
strength returned, and the vitalizing pro-
perties of Clements Tonic came in evidence
by the gradual healing of the wound in my
arm, and now there is nothing but a scar to
show where it was opened. I had lost a
considerable amount of flesh during my
illness, but in a few weeks it >vas all re-
stored, so it is needless to say that Clements
Tonic put my digestive organs in thorough
order, besides infusing so much power to my
nervous system that you could not have got
a tremble out of my nerves no matter how
you might have tried. Before long I had
shaken off every pain that had tormented me,
and with the full complement of strength
which Clements Tonic restored to my sys-
tem, I went back to work none the worse
for the terrible trials I had undergone.”
“ Are you sure you were quite cured ?”
“I have not had the least thing wrong
with me since, and I feel as hearty as though
my constitution were made of iron, so
Clements Tonic did its work well. I was
a despairing wreck of humanity when it
was called into requisition, and for saving
me as it did I owe my life—long thanks to
Clements Tonic —an admission which you
can publish in any way that may do good.”

SATURDAY, MAY 9, 1903.
The Victorian Railway Crisis.
The purpose of Thomas Bent —and of the Irvine
Government which for some reason stands at the
back of Bent and endorses his brutal bluster and
blatant bully-ragging—is palpably to force the
Victorian railway employees to a strike. Behind
that purpose there may be some deep motive of
policy, such as a plot against State control of the
railways, or there may be only the reckless resent-
ment of a man whose public career is such as to
make it possible to believe him capable of any folly
and any political crime ; but the purpose is plain
enough. The position is not merely that the con-
temptible Bent, and the equally contemptible
Irvine who countenances him, have taken up a
firm attitude—or even a brutal attitude—on a
matter of principle. If it were so, the action
might be excused if not pardoned. Were Bent—
with Irvine at his back —to declare that trades-
unionism is a pernicious principle which, since it
interferes with the right of the employer to grind
down the worker, must be put down ; and were he
accordingly to set himself to its suppression, such
an attitude, however cruel, however extraordi-
nary in this 20th century, would be at least
capable of some sort of explanation. But
that does not represent the Bent attitude at
all. The Bent attitude is a deliberate effort
to outrage the feelings and sentiments of
the Victorian railway workers; and the demand
that these workers shall cut themselves adrift
from the central trades-union organisation is only
a feature of that attitude. The demand is made
primarily for the purpose of being offensive. It
is flung at the men with savage malevolence and
with all the coarse invective of the enraged cab-
bage-huckster whose continuance in Victorian
public life is such an ironic commentary on demo-
cracy. The men are threatened with brutal down-
rightness that “the sack ’ will follow a refusal
on their part to accede to the demands of Bent.
What better way to insure that they will refuse?
Were men to be asked to go to Heaven in such
terms as Bent uses, their manhood would urge
them to say no.
* • * *
It seems certain that, if the railway men do not
join issue with Bent on this point, he will shortly
find another cause of quarrel. Whether it is from
motives of deep-seated policy (and the backing
given to this disreputable political bully by men
who pretend to a conventional respectability seems
to suggest that he is the lowly instrument of some
noisome scheme), or from mere private malevolence
and the reckless desire of a certain class of upstart
to be arrogant and insolent, certain it is that
Bent wants trouble. If the railway men, with a
consideration for the public good that their
tormentor so conspicuously lacks, give way on
the union point, sacrifice their right of indepen-
dent action in private life, and accede to a demand
which no other civilised country would make, and
which in N.S.W. would be a criminal act if made
by a private employer— Bent, it may be fore-
casted with absolute certainty, will soon find a
fresh humiliation to inflict. Ever since he took
office as Minister for Railways in the Irvine
Government Bent has bellowed “sack” and
| strike ” ; has set himself to pile insult, humilia-
tion, and loss on the railway servants of the
State. Wage reductions, spying surveillance,
sweating, pot-house language—have all been tried,
and, having failed, this latest demand is seem-
ingly relied upon to inflame the men to a point at
which resentment smothers caution. Since it
seems to be evident that there must be a battle,
the railway men had better fight on the high
ground of principle than abandon their union
entrenchments to be forced to a conflict over some
meaner issue.
• • * *
There could be no grander principle for a working
man to fight for than that of trades-unionism, of
the Fraternity of Labor. The right of the worker
to band into a union with his brothers of the same
occupation, for mutual benefit and for mutual
resistance to oppression, the further right of such
unions to federate into more highly-organised
councils of unions, is the very keystone of social
reform. Trades-unionism is the one proved bul-
wark which the worker has against the tyranny,
the selfishness and the folly of capitalism. It has
so vindicated its claims to be considered a power
for good that even such dour and extreme in-
dividualists as Herbert Spencer have been com-
pelled to give it a reluctant benediction. Without
it, the worker in England would have been a
helot until, by some such upheaval as that in
France at the time of the Revolution, he shook his
chains off amid the ruins of society. With it, the
process of industrial and social readjustment, ren-
dered essential by the expansion of human pro-
duction by machinery and the expansion of the
human intellect by education, has been able to
develop on peaceful lines, which promise one day
to lead to a happy issue. Abolish trades-unionism,
or even seriously diminish its power, and the
subsequent oppression of the worker would be
quickly followed by an outburst of anarchy. It
is the safety valve of society. To join issue, then,
with the unspeakable Bent on the question is the
wisest course for the Victorian railway men to
follow. Every worker (in the broadest sense of
the term) in the Commonwealth is vitally in-
terested in the successful maintenance of trades-
unionism, and everyone with an intelligent ap-
preciation of the situation will make all possible
effort to support a firm championship of that
principle, which is as well worth fighting for as
Australia is worth fighting for.
* * » •
And that will be the real issue. There is no
other Sophistry may strive to represent Bent as
an administrator struggling(with much regrettable
but excusable “blanky”) to make the Victorian
railways pay. But, as a matter of fact, the
humiliation of the Victorian railway workers, by
denying to them the right which most private
employees have practically, and all have theoreti-
cally, of combining in unions, can have no
possible effect, direct or indirect, on the financial
results of the railway administration. Bent has
already enforced his reductions of railway wages.
His Government has already disfranchised the
railway men, as far as it says it wants to do, so
that they may. not have a chance of securing
redress by political agitation. Bent has already
successfully inflicted upon them a swindling
system by which they are compelled to work one
day a week for nothing, under a system analogous
to the tribute of a day’s slavery a week for the
State which is imposed in some Oriental countries.
If Bent is now able to force the railway
men to give up their trades-union privileges it
will not put a penny piece into the Treasury.
The humiliation of the men may be grateful and
comforting to him, and more precious than gold
or a share in a land swindle ; but its effect on the
revenue, or its value when brought to Cohen as
security for advances, will be absolutely nothing
The proposal is an entirely unprofitable bit of
Bent malignancy.
* • * *
Still the true reasons why the Victorian rail-
ways don’t pay—though really foreign to the issue
whether the Victorian railway men should be
deprived of their union rights as well as their
votes—should be kept in mind at this crisis.
Bent himself doesn’t argue to any material
extent in defence of the attitude he has
taken up ; with him a curse and an
ugly threat and a blustering insult take the
place of reasoning. But Bent’s backers, the men
who stand by him as he blasts and blithers through
the land, profess to be filled with a patriotic
desire to make the Victorian railways pay, to re-
move from the taxpayers the burden of the rail-
way deficit; and they pretend to find in Bent an
uncouth but efficacious instrument of Providence
to secure that happy end. It is well, therefore,
for the public of the Commonwealth to know why
the Victorian railways don’t pay and be able to
judge from a knowledge of the facts whether the
wild yells of Bent are likely to be a remedy. The
Victorian railways don’t pay mainly because—
They were never assisted in the first instance, as
almost all private railway enterprises in “ new ” countries
were, by free grants of land. The private enterprise
railways of Canada had huge subsidies of land and money
from the Government and now do not pay on their
private capital as much per cent, as the Victorian lines
The lines of the U.S.A. had the assistance of big land
grants, of money grants from the towns which they
touched, and even then, in spite of the great population
of that great country, rarely pay an honest dividend on
their original capital, though they may yield high
profits to “ wreckers ” of the Jay Gould type after much
of their cost of construction has been written off as lost.
The South American private railways were largely
propped up by Government guarantees and Government
cash, in some cases the fool-Government guaranteeing
no less than 7 per cent, dividends. The Victorian rail-
ways do remarkably well, and a reasonable Government
looking forward to their prospective value, would be
fairly well satisfied with the present state of affairs
They are burdened with finding the exorbitant interest
on some of the earlier construction loans ; they have to
find interest on the cost of lines which are now closed
down and on the cost of surveys of lines which were
never built; and they have to carry a load of in-
terest on the ridiculously high price's paid to Tory
landlords and swindling land-sharks for resumptions.
They are used, justifiably in some cases, unjustifiably in
others, as the great State relief department. If there'is a
drought, the pastoralists’ starving stock is carried on the
railways at a loss. When there is not a drought, the
Kvabramapootra crowd, who have already taken the
country down with irrigation-trust swindles, get their
produce taken to market on the railways at unproductive
These are the main reasons why the Victorian
railways do not pay, and they may be expressed
in one word Bentism. It is Bentism which
keeps the Victorian railways off the dividend-list—
the howling ignorance which is typified in Bent
the reckless disregard of facts which is Bent, the
sacrifice of the revenue’s interests to gratify land
swindles, Tory landlords and influential consti-
tuents which is Bent, the extravagance of build-
ing unnecessary lines which is Bent. Those bad
features of Victorian politics, those follies and
crimes that culminated in the disaster of 1893,
which find their lowest expression in Bent, are
the true reasons of failure. And failure is only to
be turned to success by dealing with those causes,
and is not in any way to be affected by further
Bentism—by sweating and swindling the •em-
ployees, by exasperating them to revolt, by
letting the lines go to pieces for want of
repair and the engines to fall in their tracks
for lack of attention, by granting free passes
to people who build houses in or around Bent’s
district, and by cursing generally and making a
railway man feel that to work under such a
master at even a high wage is a humiliation and
a degradation.
* * * •
All this is really foreign to the issue, which is
that Bent demands that the Victorian railway
employees should abandon an integral part of
their civic rights, and demands it in such a way
as to show that obedience to the. ukase —tyranni-
cal as it is—would not end the matter, but
would be the signal for the infliction of fresh
humiliations. But still it is well to bear it in
mind, to remember that the pretexts which
the contemptible Victorian Government advances
as its reasons for supporting Bentism show as lies
on a very little examination, and that the real
causes of any failings of the Victorian railways
are to be found in the political ignorance and reck-
lessness of which Bent himself is the supreme
example to date. If the Victorian railways are
to be made to “pay,” it will be by putting them
under skilful, humane, and reasonable manage-
ment; by eliminating the element of “blanky”
from the control (an employer who treats his
men on principles of “blanky ’’ must in the end
pay for his cursing, either in higher wages
or in getting poorer work); by encouraging a
spirit of cheerful emulation in the employees ;
by shutting down on the exactions of Tory
landlords and prosperous ’pootras, who can well
afford to pay a (reproductive rate for railway
service ; by frankly recognising that some railway
work is charitable relief, and should be charged
to the charity vote ; and, most important of all,
by splitting up, with a progressive land tax, the
big estates which smother land settlement along
some of the trunk lines. The Bent policy, which
is to ruin permanent way and rolling stock by
inattention and to ruin the morale of the men by
too much “blanky” attention, is on utterly
disastrous lines. Incidentally, then, the Victorian
railway men, by taking the firm stand which as
free men they must take on the question of trades-
unionism, are acting in the best possible way
to secure railway reform. Reform can only come
by a negation of all that Bent stands for; and
the fall of Bent would be the probable outcome
of a struggle. To defend a sacred principle and
to kill Bent politically—it is a glowing prospect
which demands any sacrifice.
The Same Old George.
George Reid, the ancient owner of the fatuous
smile and the Dry Dog and the joke that was
borrowed from Comic Cuts, is pervading Tas-
mania on a Foreign-trade mission. According to
his own admiring organ, Sydney Evening News,
he is paid £5O for doing this, a previous offer of
£25 having proved insufficient. It isn’t every
man who can boast that he is paid £5O to damage
the industries of his own adopted country for the
benefit of British, German, Yankee and Jew
importers, and for the profit of the foreign manu-
facturers whose goods they sell, but George
Reid has attained that remarkable, not to say
loathsome, distinction. As the stipendiary hack
of our business rivals —the people who want
the work which is also so badly needed by
Australians George Reid has, so far as
reports go up to date, said nothing very new,
and the old dull things that he said were, most of
them, flatly untrue. The quotations which follow
are all taken from Reid’s other admiring organ,
Sydney Telegraph, which, presumably, wouldn’t
go out of its way to misrepresent its own fatted
joss. Therefore, in taking it as an authority, the
importers’ remunerated hack can hardly complain
of not getting a fair deal.

This is G. H. Reid at Hobart, with his vast
waistcoat overhanging the Derwent and his back
jostling Mount Wellington:
The Ministry began with a magnificent manifesto, and
they were now arraigned before the public to be judged
as to whether they had carried out their promises. The
selection of the capital site was to be the first thing done.
It had progressed through two picnics and into one Royal
Commission. The judicial bill of pre-eminent importance
had not yet been passed. Then there was the Inter-State
Commission Bill, promised but not touched. The old-
age pension scheme which the Barton Government
promised, and which would cost a million to administer,
was chimerical, because it would mean extra taxation of
four millions.
Mr. Edward Braddon : Through the Customs.
Mr. Reid : That was so, and that reminded him that the
Prime Minister had branded the Freetrade party as the
party that was going in for direct taxation. (Hear, hear.)
He did not think Mr. Edmund Barton would have pur-
posely made a misstatement. In living the life of a public
man it was impossible for him to remember everything
and Mr. Barton forgets a good deal. (Anplause.) It was
a clear and sacred bond that the Federal Government
should only tax through the Customs, leaving direct
taxation as much as possible to the States. That was the
deliberate policy of the Opposition as it was of the Minis-
terial party. Regarding the old-age pension scheme,
seeing what would be necessary in the way of taxation’
it was a cruel and wanton act on the part of the Govern-
ment to hold out to the electors such a bribe as that, for
it was nothing else.
That was the beginning of the first speech of the
“campaign,” and it was a beginning of which
Ananias might have been proud. For, in the first
place, it is wholly untrue to say that the Barton
Government promised that the selection of a
Federal capital was to be the first thing done. It
is a gross and shameful untruth to allege that the
Barton Government promised to grant old
age pensions, and though George Reid has
repeated that malevolent lie most persistently, he
most carefully avoids stating when, where, how,
or by whom the alleged promise was made—-
because no such promise having ever existed, he
can’t do so. And it is absolutely untrue to say
that there is any “clear and sacred bond,” or any
other kind of bond, to the effect that the Common-
wealth Government shall tax only through the
Customs. The only bond in existence on the
subject is the Federal Constitution, and it gives
the Commonwealth Government absolute powers
to levy taxes of any kind. Therefore, in say-
ing that the Barton Government, by making
the promise regarding old age pensions which it
never did make, was well aware that it was pro-
mising what couldn’t come to pass because the
“ bond ” which doesn’t exist made it impossible
to raise the money, George Reid did something
extra in the way of misrepresentation.
Further, George Reid lays it to the charge of
the Barton Government as one of its special sins
that if it did provide £1,000,000 a year for old
age pensions it would, under the Braddon clause
have to levy £4,000,000 in customs and excise
taxes to get the said million. If the Braddon
clause is so nefarious Reid should certainly not go
round with its nefarious author as one of his chief
supporters. Also he shouldn’t have refixed the
Braddon clause in the Constitution when the
Premiers’ Conference was called in 1899 for the
express purpose of taking it out and putting in
something better. Premier Barton was not a
member of the Premiers’ Conference which finally
decided to keep the Braddon clause in the Con-
stitution, while Reid and his friend Braddon
were members. It is beautifully characteristic of
Reid to thus blame his own act on the man who
wasnt there. u

When the world was waiting for our grain, metals, and

What a grim and heartless satire ! The world is
waiting for our grain when we have just had a
two-bushel harvest and have failed to grow enough
for our own consumption ; and when all Aus-
tralia has averaged less than seven bushels per acre
annually for the last 10 years, and the farmer
is half the time a beggar asking the State for seed ;
also, the world is waiting for our metals
when our copper, lead and silver mines are
shut up in all directions because the prices won’t
pay for working ; and for our wool when the last
sheep seems as if it was likely soon to perish out

howl for the abolition of the meat duty, so that
cow-beef can be imported. We are not to compete
with the factories in the old world slums—only
with the peasant in the cold Russian bogs, and the
gaunt little nigger in the Indian jungles, and the

pete yet with the Old World. It is a strange
doctrine that because a man iemoves into a new
country, or a new town, or a new street, or a new
house, he must at once lose all capacity for being
a skilled artisan, and degenerate into a hewer of
timber, or a primitive individual running after a
bullock.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Virtuous Jap.
Mr. Bruce Smith is building up a reputation as
the Anacharsis Clootz of Australia. It will bo
remembered that A.C. was known as the
“ Apostle of Mankind ” during the French
Revolution. He held a brief for men of all races
and colors, and wanted them to be looked upon
as brothers by the free and enlightened French
patriots of the period. The grim humorists of the
Convention tolerated him for a time. Then they
chopped his head off. Mr. Smith, lecturing
before a suburban Ladies Political League the
other evening said, amongst other things : “ Our
treatment of the Japanese, as well as of our fellow
subjects of the Empire in India, was an outrage
on our much-vaunted civilisation and enlighten-
ment. We were completely sacrificing our racial
reputation for courage by openly admitting that
what we had to fear in the Japanese were their
great national virtues.” Of course, no such thing
was ever admitted, but it is an old trick in contro-
versy to invent your opponent’s confessions and ad-
missions. A few hours earlier, on the same day
when the member for Niggers and German
Shoddy made these droll statements, a depu-
tation from another Ladies’ League waited on
Minister Kingston at Charters Towers, and urged
him to use his best efforts for “ the abolition of
the system of introducing Japanese women, who
were practically .brought to Queensland for a cer-
tain purpose.” Japanese prostitution, however
much it may Appeal to Bruce Smith as a great
national virtue, evidently has no similar attrac-
tions for the women of the Towers. The latter may,
of course, be prejudiced and take what Mr. Smith
calls ‘‘a narrow view of other races,” but they
are on the spot and are entitled to their opinions,
however illiberal these may seem to Mr. Smith.
And as the class of virtue referred to has
come to be recognised in Queensland, where the
Jappy person is a fact and not merely a
beautiful theory, as a leading Japanese export,
the less said about the virtuous Jap. the
better. Anyhow, Australians are not likely to lose
much by keeping at the end of a four-thousand-
mile pole the national virtues of the Land of the
Geisha and the Yoshiwara. But The Bulletin,
despite its White Australia opinions, doesn’t want
to be too illiberal. As a concession to the enemy,
it is ready to consider the free admission of
Japanese barristers, the same to be allowed to
practise here immediately on arrival.
The Foolishness of Tom- Mann.
The Bulletin has a great respect for Tom Mann,
the English Labor leader, whom the Victorian
workers have engaged as their organiser, but the
above head-line is justified all the same. It re-
gards Mann as miles and miles ahead of the con-
ceited Ben Tillkt— but none the less the foolish-
ness of Tom Mann calls for public remark. Mann
has lately been preaching in Victoria the gospel
?T.. a . six-hours working day as one of the possi-
bilities of the future and one of the remedies for
the evil under which Labor suffers ; and if that
gospel, or anything like it, takes root here in its
raw state, and without any compensating features,
it will be the final ruin of Australia, for these
reasons:—
Australia has already, in a large proportion of its
manufacturing industries, an eight-hour da}'. The
foreign manufacturer mostly gets nine or ten hours* work
out of his men per day, and though they may wear out
earlier and die sooner as a consequence, that makes no
difference in the present advantage which he gains. The
result is that he has a start of us varying from 12J to 25
per cent, in cheapness of production.
The foreign manufacturer pays less wages, which in-
creases his start of us in cheapness of production to
somewhere about 40 or 50 per cent.
If Australia ever gets down to a seven or six hours day
the foreigner will have a still greater start.
And unless the foreigner is handicapped out of the race
by at least a 50 or 60 per cent, protective tariff, the
result will be that, in every’ industry which is exposed to
foreign competition, the six hours per day at present
wages will only’ lead to no hours, no wages, and no
industry at all.
Unusually high wages, unusually short hours (unless
balanced by something quite extraordinary in the way of
skill), and cheap production can’t go together. Protec-
tion itself doesn’t make things dearer, for the cheapest-
producing countries in the world are protected countries.
But paying the maximum wage for the minimum amount
of work does make things dearer. There is no reason
why things shouldn’t be dearer. The producer gets the
advantage in increased wages ; the disadvantage in the
shape of increased prices is shared between him and the
non-producer, and it is folly for the workman to be a
slave in order that the pawnbroker or commission agent
or the landlord may get his goods at .a reduction. Also
if the country, by producing for itself, reduces the tribute
it has to pay to the foreigner the country in the aggregate
is so much the richer. But when the worker starts by
increasing the cost of Australian production through en-
hanced wages and shortened hours, instead of first
handicapping the cheap foreign producer out of the race,
he is only cutting his own throat.
Australia has already done too much in the way
of starting at the wrong end. It has tried to
believe in high wages, short hours, cheap pro-
duction and cheap foreign imports, all at once.
For forty years it has contrived, by finding
artificial employment for its people out of huge
foreign loans, to disguise the fact that there is
a hideous scarcity of real self-supporting local
industries in this country. Now that the loans
imported are barely sufficient to cover the interesb
exported, and the most vigorous borrowing hardly
gives the country any actual cash to play with,
the old ghost can’t be laid any longer. The
manufacturing industries of Australia are larger
now than they ever were before, but even now
they are not more than half sufficient to keep the
people of Australia out of the poorhouse, when
the loan money coming in is all counter-balanced
by the interest going out, and there is no surplus
to make a bogus boom. The tariff has
never been a sufficient set-off against high
wages and short hours, and so the importer is
still, to a great extent, boss of the situation.
Millions and millions of pounds worth of the
goods we should make are being imported,
while our own people are unemployed, because
they can’t, with the present wages and hours,
produce cheaply enough to compete with the im-
porter. The Federal tariff is quite insufficient.
If Australia is going to find work for its multi-
tudinous unemployed, it must either raise its
tariff fence higher or else it must work nine or
ten hours a day for the same, or even less, wages.
If there is going to be any further increase of
wages or reduction of hours, the tariff fence must
go still higher ; if it doesn’t, the inevitable result
is the destruction of many industries and no
wages or hours at all. That is why The Bulletin
advocates the raising of the tariff fence all the
time. But Tom Mann apparently advocates the
short hours and long wages without any serious
attempt to first shut out the competition of long
hours and short wages outside. Henoe the title
of this article.

Stone-Breaking 1 .
Stone-breaking depots are to be opened by the
Government, and the unemployed will be given an
order entitling them to work, according to their
circumstances.— Sydney papers.
Stone-breaking, in England, where most of us,
or our ancestors, come from, has grown to be
synonymous with pauperism. It is the same
thing as stone-broke. It is regarded as the last
possibility in the way of unskilled labor. It is
the job that is done in the gaol-yard and the
workhouse, and it is the kind of work that is
handed out by “the parish,” which is repre-
sented by Bumble the beadle, as poor relief.
And stone-breaking on a large scale is now to be
tried in N.S.W. by way of keeping body and
soul together. The Sand-shifting Government,
and the Scrub-cutting Government, and the
Rail-painting Government we have had, and
the Stone - breaking Government follows them.
New South Wales doesn’t really want any
extraordinary quantity of broken stone just now;
and no country that is heavily in debt to other
countries can pay its way by breaking stone for
itself, any more than it can live by taking in its
own washing. It is a parasitic trade, a mere
makeshift, which helps to partially disguise the
fact that we are borrowing money for purposes of
public charity. New South Wales, with its in-
numerable schemes of “relief work,” is in danger
of becoming, to a greater extent than almost
any other civilised country on earth, a community
of stone-breakers, pick-and-shovel men, rough un-
skilled laborers of the beast-of-burden description
—and enforced paupers. The policy of the State,
as enumerated by its favorite political fool and
clown, George Reid, is to banish skilled indus-
tries, which George denounced recently in Tas-
mania as an insane attempt to compete with
“the slums of the old world’’—and to borrow
money and compete with the poorhouses of
the old world in breaking stones. Every year
N.SAV. imports over £17,000,000 worth of goods
from outside the Commonwealth—goods, the
making of which here would provide work for all
its unemployed; and N.S.W.’s own George
Reid demands that the present low tariff be re-
duced so that we may import still more, and have
still less local employment; while our own people
borrow money from Bull-Cohen and break stones.
The Commonwealth has a population not so very
far short of that of Belgium—Belgium, with its
over 400 iron-works, 148 sugar refineries, 240 dis-
tilleries, its 1200 breweries, its annual output of
£19,000,000 worth of cotton, woollen and silk
goods, and its innumerable other industries ; but
George Reid tells us that we aren’t sturdy
Belgians, and that we are too small and help-
less to think of manufacturing yet—so we break
stones and borrow money in England. The
alleged “great natural industries,” which con-
sisfe of a struggle to make exotic grains and
foreign animals take kindly to an unnatural
soil, have, for the present, collapsed over a
great part of Australia owing to climatic con-
ditions, and when they will recover from that col-
lapse we have not the least idea. The manufactur-
ing industries, which are mostly independent of
climatic conditions, are regarded with derision by
the dominant party in N.S.W. and denounced as
a sinful interference with the profits of the
importer—so N.S.W. sinks into a semi-pauperised
community which borrows money and breaks
stones. And when the supply of loan money,
which holds up the great system of stone-breaking,
relief works, seed-wheat grants, deficits and the
general lack of visible means of support, gives
out, it is difficult to imagine what is ahead.

Horse-hair Sense v. Horse-Sense.
The decision of Victorian Chief-Justice Madden
in the customs oase, Stephens v. Abrahams,
declaring the Act which provides for the collection
of Australian customs void in essential particulars,
should be treated with all the respect which a
Supreme Court ruling calls for. Still Australians
(that is to say patriotic Australians, whose sym-
pathies are not with the criminals who are now
rejoicing in the prospect of immunity from punish-
ment for customs swindles) cannot help recalling,
with satisfaction, the fact that Chief-Justice
Madden has a habit of being mistaken in his law.
There is no Australian Judge whom the writer
can at the moment recall who has had so many
decisions upset on appeal. Justice Madden’s
present decision must stand in Victoria until
set aside; but there cannot be much reasonable
doubt as to its ultimate reversal. The case which
evoked it was not a matter of practice law, but
of constitutional interpretation, and it is singu-
larly unfortunate that it should have come before
a court which was not designed or fitted for its
discussion, and from which the appeal lies only
to the Privy Council —a body which, under the
Federal Constitution, is expressly denied (clause
74) the right to adjudicate on such questions
unless by the request of the Federal Hign Court.
However, that is part of the price Australia has
to pay for its bitterly unpatriotic Freetrade
party, which took up so much of the time of the
first session of the Australian Parliament fighting
the cause of shoddy as to postpone the discussion
of the Judicature Act until the last moment, and
then finally blocked the measure. A Federal High
Court would have almost certainly come to a de-
cision entirely different from Chief-Justice Mad-
den’s. Briefly, the points of the case were:
Defendant Abrahams was convicted of presenting
a false invoice and appealed against the conviction
on the ground that all of the penal and machinery
clauses of the Customs Act (Australia, 1901) were
void, because certain clauses in that Act “im-
posed taxation.” The Federal Constitution
declares (clause 53) that no Act imposing taxation
shall include any other provision, the object of
the clause being to protect the rights of the
Senate. The Senate is forbidden to initiate
taxation Bills and can only suggest amendments
therein, and, as a safeguard to its rights as a
chamber of almost co-ordinate power with the
House of Representatives, clause 53 was included
in the constitution to prevent the House of Repre-
sentatives from “tacking” any matter foreign to
finance on to a taxation bill. “ Tacking-on ”is a
quite constitutional method in Britain, where it
has been used by the House of Commons to force
a measure on the House of Lords; it is also con-
stitutional in any Australian State Parliament.
But the Federal Constitution forbids it in the
Australian Parliament, for the clearly avowed and
only object of safeguarding Senate privileges
against encroachment by the House of Represen-
tatives—not with any idea of interfering with
the necessary work of providing machinery
for the collection of taxes. Chief-Justice
Madden, however, decides that the Customs
Act (which is the machinery Act of the
tariff) is an Act imposing taxation (and there-
fore void as to its provisions in regard to
punishment of offenders, etc.) because certain of
its clauses—such as that which allows the Minister
for Customs to stop leakages by charging duty on
any import which, though not exactly similar to a
dutiable article, is clearly a substitute for that
article— when read in conjunction with the subse-
quent Tariff Act, may be held to impose taxa-
tion. That is, from the point of view of common-
sense, a most strained interpretation. At the
time when the Customs Act (the machinery
Act) was before Parliament it imposed no
taxation; standing by itself it still imposes
no taxation. It is only when it is read
with another Act that it becomes a
machine for the collection of taxes. It certainly
in no way trenches on the privileges of the Senate
—to protect which, and only to protect which,
clause 53 of the Constitution was passed. Chief-
Justice Madden might as well argue that if the
Federation, at some future time, were forced
by a great national emergency to impose a poll-
tax, and were, for purposes of convenience, to
declare that every citizen having a vote should
pay the tax, thereby the Federal franchise and
electoral laws would be made invalid, because,
reading the Act which gives an adult a vote in
conjunction with the poll-tax Act, it would be an
Act imposing taxation. If the Victorian decision
is right, it would seem to be practically
impossible for the Australian Parliament, under
its Constitution, to effectively impose any taxa-
tion. To avoid that absurdity, does it not seem to
be the natural course to interpret the Federal
Constitution as meaning what its framers intended
it to mean, and what to the lay mind it very
clearly does mean ? That section 53 of the Con-
stitution Act can be twisted into a meaning
which gives some plausible ground for the view
that the Australian Parliament cannot pass a
taxation machinery Act, because such an Act
mist provide for pains and penalties, and also
must, when read in conjunction with the actual
Taxing Act, be held to impose taxation, may
possibly be admitted. But when there is another
plain meaning of the section open to be taken,
and when that plain meaning, on the evidence of
the debates, is undoubtedly the plain meaning
which the men who made the Constitution had in
their minds, what crooked wisdom it is to torture
plain English into legalising a hopeless absurdity !
Why abandon plain horse sense for such strained
horse-hair sense ?

The Moaningffl of Fa.
“ If, said Pa Brunker to the Newcastle coal-
miners at their mass meeting on Sunday—“if
there had been in office since 1896 a Minister for
Mines who was not afraid to do his duty, there
would have been no trouble in Newcastle to-day.”
Pa Brunker mentions 1896 because that was the
year in which the Reid Ministry, of which he
was Minister for the Sabbath Banana, passed a
Coal Mines Regulation Bill, which professed to
secure for the coal-miner a great deal more than it
aotually did secure—it professed to secure for
him payment for coal-hewing by weight, and it did
nothing of the sort. It was, in fact, a fair type of
all the political works of George Reid ; it was a
fraud and a sham, and a piece of shoddy. That is
one reason for the trouble in Newcastle at
present. And the other reason is its twin brother.
If there hadn’t been any George Reids and Pa
Brunkers to run N.S.W. on the line 3 of
alleged Freetrade, there wouldn’t have been any
need to have depended upon foreign markets for
coal sales. The Daily Telegraph gave its whole
show away when it let a man in the trade talk in
its columns in this way:—
We have to compete against American coal and Chilian
coal—which is being rapidly put out under labor con-
ditions not nearly so irksome as ours—and if we are to
maintain our position in foreign markets —and three-
fourths of our output is exported—there must be a total
revision of our conditions. We have to compete against
locally-mined coal, English coal, Japanese coal, and
Chinese coal—we are just about driven from the Shanghai
market, and we should have been driven out of Manila
altogether but for the cheap freights by tramp steamers.
Instead of building up our own industries, and
establishing our own mills and factories and works
to consume our own coal while making our own
manufactured goods, we have accepted the
Freetrade heresies of George Reid and Pa
BRtJNKER,_and have compelled our people to scour
the earth in order to find a market for their pro-
duct ; and out there they have to compete with
the cheap yellow miner and with the white miner
who isn’t working under what Australia regards
as white men’s conditions. And when they are
beaten out, as beaten out they must be unless they
consent to be ground down to the odious conditions
underwhich theJapaneseand someotherforeigners
work, Pa Bbunker fatuously tells them that it is
all the fault of the administration of a small Act,
which, at the most, only touches the very surface
of the matter.
; *
Melb. Age, of April 18th, has something to say
about the efficient and intelligent railway service
of the United States, and makes contrast between
it and the Victorian service, with disaster to the
latter. In America, employees all have the way
of promotion open to them, and must move on or
retire. Exams., which must be passed periodi-
cally, compel efficiency, since a certain time only
is allowed in which to pass tests, and, if not suc-
cessful, men must leave. "It does not,” says auld
lang Syme’s paper, “ take an American manager
18 years to get rid of a man who damages stock or
endangers travellers’ lives.” Yet the U.S. has
a villainously bad record for accidents, and
Canada, whence the new Vic. manager is coming,
has about the most murderous railway system on
earth. Even all the facilities these countries
possess for sacking railway employees at the
shortest notice, doesn’t keep down the butchery
bill.
♦
Melbourne Marine Board, at a recent meeting,
discussed an infringement of the Shipping Regu-
lations by a Gippsland steamer. The chairman,
Captain Currie, attempted to close the debate
with the remark, “ never do anything about
nothing.” This remark seems to cover 75 "per
cent, of the Board’s policy. It isn’t anything and
it does nothing. Not so many weeks ago two
cases of deck loading were reported for the
Board’s investigation. One of these concerned a
steamer employed in the Lakes trade. The
shipping inspector reported that early on the
morning of sailing, he had instructed the chief
officer not to stow any more cargo on deck.
Returning just previous to the vessel’s departure
he found that additional cargo had been stowed
on deck, in complete defiance of his instructions.
Reports handed in later by the officers concerned
stated that the additional cargo shipped was for
the Bay ports. What particular bay was not
stated, leaving it to be assumed that Port Phillip
Bay was meant, when, as a matter of fact, it
afterwards turned out that Waratah Bay was the
destination. The final result was that the Board
decided to forward the reports to the owners,
intimating that in case of any infraction of the
inspector’s orders in future, proceedings would be
taken. The same Board has so long been going
to take a firm attitude in the future that it begins
to appear that it hasn’t any future. It certainly
has no attitude worth speaking of in the present,
and it is in the present that vessels are lost and
men are drowned.

Interesting to watch for what is going to happen
to the “Master Coachbuilders’and Wheelwrights’
Association of N.S.W.” That important body,
having been cited by its employees before the
N.S.W. Arbitration Court, decided—
That, in view of the awards and decisions given by the
Industrial Arbitration Court on claims of a similar
nature that have been made on industrial unions of
employers, the Master Coachbuilders and Wheelwrights’
Association of the New South Wales Industrial Union ot
Employers (what a long tail our cat has got I), declines to.
answer any of the 17 claims made upon them by the
claimant union ; and that the secretary be instructed to
file the foregoing in the Industrial Arbitration Office.
As a general rule, it is not safe to refuse to answer
when sued in a court of justice ; the usual result
is a, verdict for the other fellow on all points.
Evidently the true nature of the powers of the
Arbitration Court has not yet penetrated to the
minds of the M.C. and W.A. of the N.S.W. I.U.
of E. Perhaps intelligence takes a long time to
dawn through a name like that.
