Various misrepresentations by Sydney D.T. in
regard to Victorian railway crisis:
A railway man, the Government says, is entitled to
treatment as good as he could get in the best kindred
private employment, but anything more than that is an
injustice to the employing community. In private em-'
ployment loyal obedience is always required from the
employed. There is no other conceivable basis for
friendly relations and profitable business. The railway
workers, when they were beaten last year—not so much
by the Government as by the aroused employing com-
munity-showed that they regarded the event as only
a temporary set-back by at once and defiantly connecting
themselves with the Government’s bitterest opponents—
the Trades Hall party. So they intimated that they were
still full of fight, and would wait for opportunity to renew
the struggle for supremacy. But the alliance comes
under a long standing and necessary prohibition. A
railway servant must not take an active part in political
affairs. There is no organisation in Australia more given
to participation in politics than is the Victorian Trades
Hall Council.
Why can’t the Tory sheets argue their case with a
little regard for the truth ? It is not a fact (as
inferred) that a private employer always requires
implicit obedience from the employed to the
extent of his right to join a trades-union
and affiliate with a trades-hall council. It is
so far from being true that in N.S. Wales
to require that degree of obedience which,
the Irvine Government asks of the railway
workers would be a criminal act on the part of an
employer. It is not a fact that the Vic. railway
unions, “ when they were beaten last vear,” affili-
ated with the Trades Hall Council; some of them
were affiliated before. It is not a fact that there
is no organisation in Australia more political than
the Vic. Trades Hall Council. In Victoria, as in
N.9.W., the industrial and political organisations
of labor are separate. The D.T. speaks now with
as much falsehood on labor as on tariff matters.
White Australia, and its Brown Brother.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Are we not, brothers, subjects of the same great
Empire?”— Press, everywhere.
There are many men who come across the sea,
And some of them are black, and some are brown;
And some of them are moral, but they’re dirty in
a quarrel,
And knife you when you once go down ;
But the queerest of a quaint old band
Is our brother from the coral strand—
Our brother with his dirt and his animated shirt,
And the bundle in bis unclean hand.
He knoweth not the blessedness of Soap,
He careth not a curse for Beer ;
He’s a lanky son of Hades, and he louts before the
ladies
With an oily “ Missee, please look here ! ”
Our brother from the Chutnee land,
Our brother with the greasy brand,
Our brother with his dirt and his animated shirt,
And the bundle in his unclean hand.
Our brother is a man of toil,
Our brother is a Ganges worm,
He was meant to grub for money in a placid land
and sunny,
And give shelter to the homeless germ ;
Our brother of the hawking band,
Our brother with his suave demand,
Our brother with his dirt and his animated shirt,
And the bundle in his unclean hand.
We’re bothered with the brown Afghan,
.We’re smothered by the smooth Chinee,
We’ve always had to bustle and to try a bitter
tussle
With the spawnings of the Heathen Sea ;
But—we shudder in a bright young land,
At our brother with his smile so bland :
Our brother with his dirt and his animated shirt,
And the bundle in his unclean hand !
Q.

Next Saturday the “ Circus Girl ” is to be
staged at Sydney Royal. It is her first appear-
ance in Sydney, and the oity looks forward
to her in a hopeful frame of mind, based on
good—and possibly quite unreliable —reports of
the lady from other parts. It is backing its
opinion with cash to an extent that makes a big
first night a matter of certainty. < The names of
some old stage friends appear in the cast
Misses Rose Musgrove and Lilian Digges, also
Hugh Ward, C. R. Bantock and Arthur Lissant
among them.
« # 0 ft
“Sweet Lavender ” followed “Charley’s
Aunt,” at Sydney Criterion last Saturday, with
some success. It belongs to the. quieter type of
comedy that appeals to the British nation in its
softer moods, as did “ Caste ” before it, but it
hasn’t any particular meaning for the Australian
of to-day. When it came out first, London lady-
hood went into lavender cuffs and lavender collars,
and tried to make itself a sweet young person with
engaging ways, in imitation of the heroine ; but
Sydney merely wonders what there was about this
uninteresting young female to rouse so much en-
thusiasm. Lavender is a child of Nature, with a
mother who is a housekeeper and also a mystery.
The plot, such as it is, hangs on the ancient idea
of her preventing the marriage of her daughter
with the adopted son of the man who is illegally
responsible for said daughter. She doesn’t men-
tion that he is responsible for the daughter till
Act 111., but merely rises with Lavender, when
she hears who is the adopted father, and says
that they must go. Lavender is ill to order, and
leaves her love to order, and doesn’t ask why, or
stamp her feet at ma, and her filial conduct leaves
this paper much depressed. It takes a very
experienced actress to play the child of nature
with success, and by the time she has the experi-
ence she has generally got too fat or acquired
hard, long shoulders, or otherwise put herself out
of court.
• • • *
Of course the person of the play is 'Frank
Thornton, as the gone-to-pieces barrister, Dick
Phenyl. Thornton’s rendering of the battered
horse-hair wreck is clever, and his voice excel-
lently realistic, but The Bulletin does not feel
any desire to burst into tears at the pathetic
parts, and that, it seems, is how The Bulletin
ought to feel. Joseph Wilson acts naturally
as Mr. Wedderburn, Lavender’s fairly sinful
father; and Mrs. Walter Hill makes a good many
humorous points as a fashionable lady with a deep
voice; she is also the mother of Minnie (Miss
Leonie Norbury), who may be in love with the hero
or may not; there is nothing much in her conduct
to determine one way or the other. Vera Fordyce
as the housekeeper-mother acts with what gentle
papers frequently call “ suppressed force.” It
generally leads to a suppressed actress. With a
little more passion and “ go,” however, Vera may
be saved this fate. Miss Pearl Hellmrich’s
Lavender is a quiet, simple girl, and may be set
down generally as a colorless success. Looking
back on the lady with the flaring red wig who did
the character once before in Sydney, Miss Hellm-
rich gains greatly by comparison. If the other
lady hadn’t Worn the red wig the result of the
comparison might have been different. The
“ Private Secretary ” is due on Saturday.
* * * *
Mark Hambourg, the musical phenome-
non, gave Sydney the first taste of his finished
(if music were ever finished) quality as a pianist
at the Town Hall last Saturday. He looked,
through his hair, at a huge audience ; shook his
hair at it in greeting ; then grasped the keyboard
of bis Grand with all his fingers and breathed his
soul into its soul. His programme was supremely
classic —Beethoven, Chopin, Rubenstein. He
brought out their trills like a canary bird,
he arpeggioed like the breakers on Manly
Beach or the dripping of the waters at
Bridal Veil Falls at Katoomba, and, as for
his rapid octavo passages their lightning-
like dexterity caused many a decent player
in .the audience to lose heart and determine
to lock her piano and sell the key to a
marine store. Boris Hambourg, Mark’s brother,
is a budding Gerardy. There are a lot more
Hambourg geniuses, girl and boy, in the bud.
The youngest is two years old, and is already
advanced in composition of major and minor
wails. Miss Mabel Batchelor is vocalist at the
Hambourg concerts. She is a nice little 15-year
old with a pretty, light soprano of some promise.
John Lemmone’s flute-playing was—well, every-
one knows what the flute is at the lips and fingers
of a master, and “Old John,” as he is affection-
ately called (partly because he is young) is a
master.
* * * »
“A Woman of Pleasure,” at Sydney
Lyceum, continues its involved tale concerning the
lady who loves her husband so much that in despair
she nearly goes off with another man, which is
the stage heroine’s usual method of straightening
things out. She likewise continues to rebuke the
bloomer costume of Georgie Leighford, whereat
the audience applauds, forgetting that the rebuker
blackmailed an innocent earl into sorrowful matri-
mony in the prologue. As before, that melancholy
peer is seen outside his baronial halls advising his
small son, aged five, to guard the family escutcheon
and to be careful of a woman’s reputation; and
the precocious infant promises to keep straight.
In tne last act, when the paper snow falls outside
the window and a small fire burns in the baronial
hall, the earl is seen apostrophising the portrait
of his wife in very long yellow locks, so that when
the lady enters with black hair something needs
to be explained.

“ Are You a Mason ” entered on its fourth
week at Sydney Palace with as crowded a house
as on the first night. Going to see this farce has
seemingly become a habit with some people, and
they drift in again and again to give an antici-
patory chuckle as a warning of the joke about to
oome. Even Governor Harry Rawson has found
it necessary to waive etiquette, which is a mighty
thing as regards Governors, and go a second time.

The Raimund Pechotsch concert came
off at the Centenary Hall, Tuesday, April 29th,
and served to introduce Miss Zara Wolinski,
a mezzo-soprano debutante. Herr Pechotsch
played in his usual good style and was warmly
received. Miss Wolinski has a very promising
voice, of considerable range, strength and sweet-
ness. Miss Constance Brandon Usher, Miss
Maud Dalrymple, Dr. Maguire and Mr. Harri-
son also assisted.
** « *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus, camped at Belmore
Park (Sydney), has added new and improved
items this last week or so, so that when the rain
descends the audience merely puts its umbrella up
and stays on. The Zenobia Bros, perform on
the Mysterious High Globe, one on top and
one underneath on an attached trapeze. The
undermost Zenobia prefers to hang head down-
wards. E. Shand balances chairs ; and the cham-
pions of Australia, Newhaven and Stockman,
high jump ; while between times horses called
Napoleon and Silver King, or other striking titles,
come in and look beautiful. Also Herr Pagel’s
lion gets every night a little angrier than he was
the night before. On Saturday next the Diving
Horses resume business in the old tank, which is
being constructed at a cost of £250 or so. It is a
large tank, for a Diving Horse is a commodious
beast, and needs a lot of space.
** * *
The Boissett co. appeared at Sydney
Tivoli for the first time last Saturday, and proved
to be a rather bright and merry group of acrobatic
pantomimists. Pantomime, in its true sense of
dumb-show acting, does not, as a rule, appeal
much to English-speaking people, though the
Latin races cherish its traditions fondly and reach
a high degree of skill in its interpretation. The
Latin is, as a rule, a garrulous person, hence
probably his love for an entertainment which
gives the jaw a rest. When the Martinettis—
really excellent pantomimists—were in Australia,
their success was not great—solely because
people didn’t appreciate that sort of act-
ing. The Boissetts, who are n6t nearly so
good at the panto, game, are likely _to be
far more popular, because they have their acro-
batic business to fall back upon. “The Wild
West”—an extravaganza with much knock-about
comedy and some shooting—was received with
great applause on Saturday. Albert Boissett and
Harry Holton show as the best of the troupe,
which is 11 strong. A subsequent display of
gymnastic feats by the same co. was far too long
drawn out. The first part at the Tivoli continues
to be brighter than was expected, owing to some
good items by Miss Florrie Ranger, Miss Molly
Bentley, Edward Forde, Rodda Perry and H. S.
Buckler. Fanny Powers’ imitations of Marie
Lloyd and Peggy Pryde are still popular features.
They make one sorry that Peggy isn’t here and
glad that Marie is away.
*m * *
“ 0.0.” on a little-known varitey show :
In Manly up till lately [an honorable means of adding
to one’s income has been shark-exhibiting. The happy
finder of the large defunct fish cast up on the shore makes
haste to drag it into a cave, or erect a tent over it, and
then he charges 3d a head for admission. He needn’t be
tied down to his show either, for any juvenile Manlyite is
proud to “mind the shark,” and advances this as a
laudable excuse when late home from school. The son
of a Sydney artist (Manly is crammed with artists
both in pen and pencil) came home one morning spread-
ing around him an odor of deceased fish that broke up
the rose and gold visions of his sire. Asked where he
had spent the night, he replied proudly that he had been
“ sleeping with the shark,” he and Algernon (the son of
another dreamer of great visions) having bought the
privilege of guarding that somewhat venerable object for
2d. a head. The proud child was immediately bathed
and fumigated, but the smell seems ineradicable. One
speculates on the future of a youth who, at thirteen
years of age, can find dissipation in an all night sitting
beside a cold and unresponsive shark. Apropos, a man
who once had a very inferior cuttlefish on exhibition
assured me that his show was under the management of
J. C. Williamson, and that he was J.C.W.’s agent. Even
Dan Barry never beat that bluff by much.
** * *
“On and Off,” coming to Melb. Bijou
from Sydney, with a first-class certificate for
humor and sparkle, strikes the present scribe as
being a farcical comedy which needs a little
human nature in its composition. The dialogue
is mostly crisp, and the situations in the last act
have an unfamiliar humor, but none of the char-
acters are sufficiently lifelike to qxcite interest in
their troubles. One thinks of them as stage
creations supplying an entertainment. An air of
unrealism pervades “ On and Off,” and although a
more natural performance of the same unnatural
play is conceivable, it is none the less obvious
that the person, male or female, who took this
farce from the French, and stripped it of all its
native improprieties, has left the actors very little
chance to be actually convincing. They can only
try to look as though they believed in themselves
for the moment. When the effort is not very
palpable they can raise lots of laughter. On
Saturday night there was too much effort because
the audience didn’t laugh readily enough. A
depressing curtain-raiser had opened the pro-
ceedings with a yawn.
** * *
George Willoughby presents a demon-
stratively comic husband who has shifted his
affections to another girl, and hopes to marry
her when he has divorced his harmless wife and
scattered her awful mother. The character is
gay and French, whereas Willoughby’s method is
of the toddlesome cockney order. Other French
characters, who combine to burst up the sinner’s
dream and reduce him to a state of conjugal
fidelity, are impersonated in an Anglo-Aus-
tralian manner. Miss Watts Phillips, as the
awful mother-in-law, scores more points than
anybody else ; whilst Frank Denton, as the man
that Willoughby pretends to be, scores no points
at all. The comedy seems to demand a good deal
of broad personality and masterful push on Den-
ton’s part, but he remains much the same sort of
angular young man as usual. Miss Ascoli comes
along in a casual way as an uncommunicative
young woman with a misleading wink, and she
might very well change places with Miss Apple-
ton, for then the wink item would acquire more
importance, and the artless spinster in the last act
(now played by Miss Appleton) would look better
to the occasion. '

Six nights of “ The Toreador” are leading
up to the promised novelty at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.). Take it altogether, “The Toreador”
revival is an improvement on last year’s produc-
tion of that tuneful, but unremarkable, musical
comedy. Miss Florence Young, Lauri, Ward,
and even Thorley, are more spry in their old
parts than when those parts were new ; also Miss
Chetwynd, Miss Ghiloni, Needham and Fred
Young appear where they didn’t appear before,
and the changes benefit the show. In Maud
Chetwynd’s case the benefit amounts to some-
thing considerable. On Friday the red raging
Donna who covets the yellow bull-sticker will
sing of her alleged hero for the last time, and on
Saturday the new comedy opera, “My Lady
Molly,” will appeal to Australian taste for the
first time.
## * *
Likewise Bland Holt purposes an impor-
tant event at the Royal on Saturday (9th). “The
Price of Peace,” a vast Drury Lane success by
Cecil Raleigh, is the spectacle which Bland has
prepared for execution on that date. For the past
year or two he has been threatening to place “ The
Price of Peace ’ ’ before his patrons, but a nervous
dread of the possible consequences gave the
humane manager pause. He was afraid their
astonishment might prove fatal. Now that
the public have become hardened to the sight
of motor cars, and the general capacity for
enduring sensations is stronger than of yore,
Bland reckons himself justified in letting his
tremendous drama loose. It will stagger the
guilty and appal the free, but there will be inter-
vals for rest and refreshment between the shocks.
Meanwhile “ The Prodigal Daughter ” has two
more performances to negotiate, and anyone who
is still wallowing in ignorance of this estimable
drama had better seize a final opportunity to wit-
ness it. “T.P.D.” is one of the very few dramas
which deal reasonably with the innocent girl who
goes astray, and allow her to recover her lost self-
respect without returning home in a long, melan-
choly cloak, minus her hat.
** * *
At the Princess’s there is no reduction in
the demand for Shakspeare-up-to-Date. Long
ago, when “The Dream” was very prettily
staged by George Rignold at the same theatre,
Melbourne couldn’t be induced to take much
interest in it. The present arrangement of the
Bard’s light, peculiar comedy is more expensive
and impressive than Rignold’s, richer in the little
things that go to make up a big result, and espe-
cially noted for its beautiful effect of darkness
and dawn, whereby the evening and the morning
are the next day, and the Lord sees that the
Athenians who slept on the grass all through the
soft musical night are preserved from rheumatism
and early dew. Nevertheless, the secret of the
“Dream’s” success as a poetic play (apart from
its low comedy element) is not wholly in the
staging. The picturesque characters are played
in harmony with the picture. The men—
Lysander Demetrius, angry father Egeus, and the
dear Duke —improve on acquaintance. As for
the ladies, they were always good to see and
delightful to listen to.
** * *
When Edward Lloyd returned to the
Town Hall, on Saturday night, he didn’t find
Melbourne waiting for him. Williamson and
Musgrove apparently made a mistake in bringing
a popular Sydney attraction back to the place
where he had not proved much of an attraction
until he was supposed to be leaving it. If the
history of the previous Lloyd season in Melbourne
is repeated, the third concert, which happens to-
night (Thursday), will draw a large audience, and
the tenor’s final appearance on Saturday will crowd
the Town Hall, and make it humanly warm and
comparatively comfortable. Now is the time of
year when a poorly attended concert at the official
residence of the local Lord Mayor would be a cold
dispiriting function, even though an angel warbled
“ Star of Bethlehem,” and raised it far above the
level of a Methodist hymn, or a Paganini played
the violin. Ernest Toy, a very skilled and rather
uninteresting violinist, gave a display in the big
hall on Saturday afternoon, and the function
struck a chill to one’s inmost sacred marrow.
** * *
The Williamson Comedy Co., with its
fine all-round collection of fun-makers, has left
the Adelaide Royal. During the last part of its
season Arthur Crane and Ernest Mozar alter-
nated the juvenile lead ; one of them can sing
and the other can act. Lilian Digges also had a
better chance in “San Toy” than before, and in
the convent scene of “The Runaway Girl” she
put quite a Sign-of-the-Cross fervour into her
talk of the “ life beyond ” —the said life being
that of a street-singer. As for Rose Mus-
grove, she is now very strong in every
branch of comic opera. The Musgrove Co.
has opened—too late for criticism—in * 1 The
Fortune Teller.” At the Tivoli, Winton scores
heavily with various new features (including a
burlesque of the departed Inaudi); and Spry and
Monti have a regrettable joke or two carved from
the immediate neighborhood of the knuckle.
Charles Pope and Irving Sayles, late ornaments
of the Tivoli, are running a small co. of their own
in suburban halls. The Floral Fair for the blind,
at the Jubilee Exhibition, has closed after running
two weeks, taking £4OOO, and clearing £3OOO
profit. The “ Living Whist ’’show crowded the
building to the roof four times.
At a time when the diminishing infant is
in all the papers, a South Australian country-
woman (Mrs. Williss) is worth mentioning. She
died the other day leaving 59 grandchildren and
43 great-grandchildren, with the number increa-
sing now at an average rate of nine a year.

As mentioned a few weeks ago, this bank, having got a
new general manager, is proceeding to have a further
cleaning-up. When disaster struck it 10 years ago it
grew flurried, and, in its anxiety to get from under the
wreckage, it undertook to do some rather ridiculous
things in order to pacify the depositors. This under-
taking has been a heavy handicap ; and as drought and
more hard times have come along, the bank is now writ-
ing off £368,272 from its capital, which has lately stood
at £915,865. It is an altogether commendable proceed-
ing.
The London Bank’s year has not been as profit-
able as shareholders might have hoped, from recent
experience, that it would be. Its business is largely with
the country districts, especially in Victoria, and it has a
few back-country N.S.W. branches also—just the places
where it hasn’t been profitable to be a banker or anything
else during the last 18 months. On the shareholders’ net
funds at the commencement of the_ half-year (capital
£915,915, and reserves £5790) the profits amounted to
£1 9s. Bd. per cent, per annum. This compares with
recent results as follows :
1898 .. £1 311 per cent. 1901 .. £2 11 5 per cent.
1899 .. 112 11 „ 1902 .. 1 9 8 „
1900 .. 1 12 4
If the proposed reduction of capital had appeared in the
balance-sheet, the rate would have been £2 9s. sd. per
cent. For a couple of years before 1898 there was a loss
on the business. Further, comparing'Jhe bank’s position
in 1901 and 1902 respectively these things appear
1901. 1902. Increases.

No idea is given of the nature of the investments held
against the note issue and for ordinary reserve purposes;
possibly some at least of them are as good as “ cash or
liquid securities.” As the latter item stands, however,it
still represents 23 per cent, of the callable liabilities to
the public—that is, the total amount of the liabilities to
the public less the amount of the reconstructed deposits.
In 1901 these reserves represented 26 per cent, of the
callable liabilities, and in 1900 28 per cent.; and, though
the proportion is not yet dangerously small, the move-
ment has, at a time like the present, gone about far
enough. No doubt the London’s position is very diffi-
cult ; for the 4J per cent, that it has to pay on the old
deposits is a heavy burden which it is naturally straining
to get rid of.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed: Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOJin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes,
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to tlie square inch.*

• * • *
The balance-sheet of the Great Southern Con-
sols G.M. Co. (Vic.) for the last half-year shows that the
underground electric haulage plant, which was supplied
and erected about seven months ago by the Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne, sole representatives
of GanzandOo. Ltd., Budapest, Hungary, has effected a
saving of 60 per cent, in manual labor required to operate
the mine. This excellent result, and the great satisfac-
tion the plant has given, has influenced the directors of
the Berry Consols Extended Company in placing an order
for a similar plant with the same firm. The plant will
consist of a generating station, three underground loco-
motives, and a complete electric lighting installation.
This order furnishes further evidence of the high esteem
in which Ganz’s apparatus is held in Australia. *

of the producer part of the State, any way. Its paid-up
capital (£400,000) is represented by about 900 share-

which consists chiefly of a general store and a blacksmith’s
shop, it of ten has a one-day-a-fortnight agency. So, though

even running a branch over the border at Broken Hill, it
is a larger concern than the size of its native village would
lead one to expect. But all this means that, though it
profits in an exceptional degree from the State’s prosper-
ity, it is likely to suffer in an exceptional degree from its
bad times ; wherefore the directors wisely go on building
their reserves, and are not turned aside by a spurt to
jump up the dividend.

• • a •
Nothing has been heard in Adelaide of the
Snow tramway scheme for four solid months. Snow’s
options expired last Xmas, and though he secured an
extension for twelve months, his strongest supporters do
not now believe that he will get the necessary capital.
Meanwhile the two Adelaide dailies, also Cohen’s chief
barrackers, Von Doussa, Tucker, Mayor Cohen, and the
rest of the windy crowd who alleged long ago that Snow
already had the money to commence work are as quiet
as a graveyard. What a pity it is there is not a single
democrat in S.A. Parliament strong enough to seize the
present glorious opportunity of securing national control!

Dear B.—You commit a sacrilege in the wool
trade by even mentioning the producer. There are only
two parties in it, the buyers and the sellers 1 The wool
grows out-back somehow, but it is only intended to pro-
vide rich livings for a horde of buyers, sellers, and drum-
mers. And it is the squabbling over the “pickings”
which prevents Sydney being the wool mart of the world.
Personally I know several large growers who would prefer
selling in Sydney rather than in London, but who refuse
to do so owing to the continual bickerings of the asso-
ciated buyers and sellers.—Yours, etc., A Wool-grower.

With one exception, the net profit for the past half-year

the balance being used in the business. Current bank
balances are a good deal lower than usual, and sundry
debtors owe a good deal m0re—£225,608 as against £193,550
at the end of the previous half-year. This probably
means that the community’s thirst is quite up to the old
standard, but that the community has to ask the beer-
puller to “ put it on the slate ” oftener than it used to.

The Australasian Mortgage and Agency Co.,
which has been staggering through the drought with
several million acres of western desert in its swag, and
last year found it necessary to write off £218,602, is now
reconstructing. Meantime, helped by a trust account,
business is proceeding as usual.

BENDIGO Co-OPERATIVE DISTRIBUTION SOCIETY,
Ltd., managed, it appears, mainly by working people,
doesn’t present its affairs in the most intelligible way;
but apparently its record at present and for the past
half-year is as follows:
Carried to
Profit. Div. Reserve. Reserves.
Feb., 1903 .. £1284 £lOOO £284 £2359
The reserves are used in the Society’s business. The re-
suits look very remarkable on a paid-up capital of £lB6l.
An effort is being made to establish in the
Newcastle district the sweating system which the
friendly societies or Balmain practise on their medical
employees. The coal-miner as a man who likes to get
a fair wage himself, has hitherto been content to pay
» reasonable sum for medical attendance, through his
lodge (sixpence a week per family for advice and medi-
cine whenever needed) That pays the medical man
reasonably well, and Newcastle has in consequence
attracted some very skilful practitioners, and the miner
man can reckon on getting the drugs that, by all the
rules of the game, ought to do him good, even when they
are expensive. But if he tries the medical sweating
syB tem, the miner will find that ultimately he will get
£ be 80r t; of service and the sort of medicine he pays for—
tihat and no better. And it is the worst sort of folly to
have an under-paid man fooling about with your liver ;
it’s the only liver you’ve got and it is apt to take a bitter
revenge on any ill-treatment,
* • • •
The wondrous vitality of the old confidence
trick was evidenced at Bendigo last week by Newton,
“ the great American salesman,” who ran his entertain-
ment for one consecutive night at the Rolling-stock
Buildings. Newton took the platform clothed in naval
un if o rm, and announced himself as a working philan-
thropist of the keenest moral principles. All he wanted
waB confidence of his friends in front. If they
believed in him they would earn his gratitude, and
probably get something more than their money back.
The first item on his programme was a sale of £1 notes at
15s. each. Six notes went off like hot cakes, and New-
ton’s success as a salesman was firmly established. A
number of half-crowns were next disposed of on terms
unfavorable to the vendor ; after which he cleared out a
small stock of penknives at 3s. each, the alleged cost
price being double that sum. Then Newton from
America settled down to the serious business of his brief
Bendigo season. He offered an albert chain for
sale—nob a valuable chain, as he admitted, but an
excellent article for testing the multitude’s faith
in moral principles. He fixed the price of this first great

The business shows a steady and satisfactory increase.
In the past year, for the first time since the capital went
up from £37,500 to £62,500, enough has been earned to
pay a dividend at the old rate of 10 p.c.—though
directors are not rash and foolish enough to recommend
of confidence in the s management,
.** . *
Premier Seddon s latest idea is to boom Maon-
land meat industry by establishing butcher’s shops in
some of the principal centres of England and Scotland ;
placing Govt, experts in charge, and selling at cost price.
When the British public has become accustomed to buy-
ing Maoriland meat—at a cheaper rate, presumably, than
the local article—the shops, Seddon thinks, may be
closed and the trade left to run itself. Which is where
the mistake comes in. The idea should be to make the
shops yield a small profit, and keep them open perma-
nently, and spread the industry till the Maoriland
Government’s sign-board, hanging above the gory fore-
quarter of meat, is familiar throughout the whole United
Kingdom.
* • • •
B.H. Junction North closed its half-year on
March 31. The co. began the term with £2973 in hand ;
calls yielded £3212 ; and the half-year ended with a credit
balance of £3072. Of the £3113 spent, £2681 was for
mining—mainly the sinking of a prospecting winze
between the 937 ft. and 1037 ft. levels. This working was in
ore all the way, averaging 12oz. silver, 16£ p.e. lead, and
B'4 p.c. zinc. The metallic contents are good, but with
sulphide ore that isn’t everything. Right through,
Junction North ore has been intractable, and the stuff
from this deepest working appears to be of the same
character. Anyhow, cheap working while the Junction,
Junction North, and North are separate concerns is out
of the question ; worked independently they are probably
all unpayable under what must be regarded as normal
conditions, and until some striking development occurs
in the processes of extraction.
• • • •
British 8.H., which in its last half-year (to
Dec., 31, 1902) did no productive work, made a loss of
£2679 ; that is to say, that much was spent in keeping
the rust from the idle machinery and the wolf
from the idle office-staff. In the corresponding half of
1901 the “ loss ” was £11,270; but in that term a good
deal of development was in progress.
* • * »
The most important recent development at
Broken Hill has been in the B.H. Prop, at the 800 ft. level.
Other mines are working deeper than this—the neighbor-
ing Block 10 is ore-getting below 1000 ft. —but the big
show has never been pressed for ore, and has kept mostly
above 550 ft., though the bodies have been proved down
to 700 ft. It is the eastern “leg” which has now been
met in boring at the 800 ft. ; and over 20ft. of satisfactory
mill sulphides have been pierced. The class to which the
Broken Hill lode belongs has never been satisfactorily
settled, and it was therefore doubtful whether the eastern
“ leg,” or “ saddle-flap,” persisted downwards. The
proving of this eastern body at this depth, therefore,
adds very greatly to the known ore bodies in the mine.
• • • *
There isn’t much profit in opal-mining just now.
The market is easily overstocked, especially with poor
stuff, and White Cliffs (N.8.W.) has been turning out
little else lately. Buyers are, therefore, very shy. One
result of the dull season has been to revive the request
for £lOOO or so from the prospecting vote to test the
deeper ground. The work really ought to be done by the
J. Bull co. which got hold of most of the best country at
White Cliffs ; but as the co.’s investment has been most
unprofitable (it paid a ridiculous price for the leases) it
will spend nothing. In the circumstances it might be a
payable thing for the State to give a “ tucker allowance ”
to a few deep-sinking parties, working under the direc-
tion of the district mines inspector and a local committee.

The first of the Arltunga co.’s has gone into
liquidation. A very short-lived cat indeed—in fact, only
a kitten.

The record of the Boulder Perseverance, at
present Westralia’s greatest mine, during the past two
years :
Receipts. Profit. Dive.

Depreciation allowance is not separately charged and

* » • •
Great Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) last year
treated 140,642 tons ore at a cost of £340,981, or 48s. 2d.
a ton. This includes £60,500, or Bs. 2d. a ton, spent out
of revenue on development and equipment. The bullion
averaged £3 lls. Bd. per oz., showing that the gold won
from the sulphide zone is about 10s. an ounce inferior to
that won in the oxidised zone. The ore blocked out
above the 700 ft. is estimated at 400,000 tons. Up to date
about 370,000 tons have been treated, making the total
originally contained above 700 ft. 770,000 tons. Taking
14 cubic feet of solid ore to the ton, this means that, on
the average, in every 100 ft. of depth, the Lake View and
Perseverance lodes on this lease each carries 770,000
cubic feet of ore, or is over sft. wide for the full 1450 ft.
of the lease. This is certainly a tall order, and, if
true, stamps the mine as quite unique. The mine
is now being developed at 900 ft., and the shaft is
on the way to 1100 ft. According to the estimates
each of these levels should add 20 months’ supply at the
present rate of extraction without taking into account
Tetley’s lode, which only comes into this mine below
400 ft., and is now being worked at the 500 ft. and 700 ft.
levels. When Christopher Furness joined the board a
year ago he stipulated that divs. were to be limited to
10s. quarterly, the surplus to go into Consols, to build up
a reserve fund. Last year’s surplus profits only panned
out at £2,234, and not a penny was written off for depre-
ciation. This is significant.
« • • •
Associated Northern (W.A.) should shortly
reach the lode cut by diamond drill at 912fc. Co. is still
raising 50 tons of ore daily, and continuing shipments to
Dapto. The first bore on the Iron Monarch lease is down
1000 ft., but results are not available. In the adjoining
Boomerang, on the north, tributors have been
working for years, but, except an occasional rich dab,
nothing much has been found. The delay in ordering
treatment plant is probably due to negotiations for amal-
gamation with adjacent properties.
• « •
Lorna’s Luck, Leonora (W.A.), is apparently
not the wonderful show that manager J. Nuttall would
lead one to believe. He cabled to London that the reef
was 7ft. wide at a depth of 140 ft., and worth 2oz. to lOoz.
to the ton ; yet actual crushings only gave 770 z. from 127
tons, or 12dwt. per ton. On the strength of his asser-
tions a main shaft is being sunk by contract.
* * • •
Queen Margaret South (W.A.) tributors have
had a sensational clean up for their first parcel. Ten
tons of ore, from which 7330 z. had been dollied, yielded
at the battery 7250 z., totting up a gross return of 14580 z.
The gross value of the lot is £5200, of which co. gets
£lO4O, and each tributor receives a similar div. As the
tribute still has some months to run a good many more
may be forthcoming.
* * • •
North Kalgurli (W.A.) has purchased a 20-head
mill of 12001 b. stamps and a complete cyanide plant. Ten
head will be hurried on and will probably get to work in
July. The lodes on this property are disturbed and
patchy, but enough pay ore is now in sight to keep the
plant going for months. Several rich dabs of smelting ore
have also been found. Present manager, Henry Nutman,
deserves praise for bringing this formerly discredited
concern to its present condition.
'« » • »
Golden Horsehoe (W.A.) is bein£ very energe-
tically opened up, the footage now being in the neighbor-
hood of 800 monthly, or a long way in excess of any other
mine. Ten levels are in operation, and all four lodes are
being systematically developed. Development is easily
beating the mill,and ore reserves are gradually mounting
up. 00. has maintained a regular output of about half a
ton of gold monthly for over two years without a break,
and looks like doing so indefinitely. What is known as
No. 4 lode has been the most consistent and richest, and
has helped to sweeten up the returns. This lode was
never located in the Ivanhoe, but recently, while cross-
cutting to the boundary at the 500 ft. level, a lode of 16ft.
wide, and worth 15dwt., was encountered, and is sup-
posed to be the Horseshoe No. 4. If it is, there are in all
six lodes in the Ivanhoe, and probably also in the Horse-
shoe.
* • • *
Bay ley’s, Coolgardie (W. A..), is still exploiting
at the 900 and 1000 ft. levels, but results continue very
disappointing, and it looks as if the mine, like its neigh-
bors, will never be any good at depth. A former
manager, H. H. MacKinnon, is again on the field, and, it
is rumored, is likely to resume charge.
» * • •
Zoroastrian, Bardoo (W.A.), for which Ellis
Parker was going to bring up a 40-head mill from Hope’s
Hill to add to the 10-head on the mine, is a one-horse
show. At the 400 ft level the crosscut had to go nearly
300 ft to reach the reef, which is dipping away, is less
than 2ft wide, and is of poor quality. Even the 10-head
mill continues hung up, and the company is advertising
to crush for the public. The district is a sleepy hollow,
and if the mill has to wait for outside support, it runs a
good chance of being hung up till doomsday.

Total Westralian divs. for April were £b55,000,
against £252,587 10s. for the first three months of the

* • • *
Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), the only
regular producer and div. distributor in that district,
looks as if it were to be mopped up by John Bull. Some
months ago Bull tried to get hold of the property, but
did not offer good enough bait, and the deal didn’t come
off. For some time the mine has been producing fully
2000 oz. monthly and paying Is. divs. Since its flotation
in Adelaide in 1896, the co. has distributed £84,000 on a
working capital of £14,625. As filter presses have now
got to work on the big accumulation of slimes, future
returns should show some increase. Present market
value of the mine is about £160,000.
* * • •
In connection with the fire branch which
Premier Seddon thinks of adding to M.L. Insurance Dept.,
the proposal is to give the divisible profits back to in-
surers in the shape of bonuses. Therein Seddon
seems likely to make a bad mistake. It is all
very well to give back part of the profits to
insurers for purposes of advertisement, and in order
to attract business which will bring in more profits.
But the balance of the profit should be invested in State
securities as a reserve fund and allowed to accumulate.
And the interest earned by that reserve fund should not
be included in the ordinary profit, but should be con-
stantly invested in State securities, and allowed to accu-
mulate. And until the State Fire Insurance Department
has bought up all the Maoriland Government securities
that are available for purchase the process of accumula-
tion should go on.
Having got to the end of the Boer war, Britain,
which has already Daid £67,500,000 of the war bill out of
revenue, starts again to pay off its national debt at the
rate of £6,000,000 a year. If Australia made a similar
effort, it would collect sufficient taxes to have no
deficits, would nay for all unproductive works out of
revenue, and wohld pay £600,000 a year into a public
debt sinking-fu*d. The apologists for our loan disease
are always pointing to Britain as a case that is similar to
ours. It isn’t at all similar. The points of difference
are that Britain’s unproductive debt is rather smaller in
proportion to population than ours, and is being system-
atically paid off; ours is larger than Britain’s, and is
being added to every year.

Premier See’s comparative estimate of
morality and merchandise"is suggestive. He told
a deputation of women the other day that “ the
man .who ruined a woman’s honor had committed
a crime as serious as burglary.” To “ruin a
woman’s honor, ” according to the Christian theory,
is often much the same as to eternally lose her
soul. And that, says the commercial See, is just
as bad as if a man got into his premises by night
and took away a bag of potatoes.

MR. GAYLAD: “ Nobody! t’help me. I didn’t
even mention it to my wife.”
A rather mixed condition ot affairs has
arisen in W. A. in connection with a conviction
for alleged embezzlement. A man was sentenced
to 18 months’ imprisonment, but appealed to the
Full Court against the conviction, and was re
leased on bail pending the hearing of the appeal.
Now the judge who tried him is dead, the Crown
prosecutor at the trial is a judge and a member of
the Full Court, and the lawyer who defended him
has been made Crown Prosecutor. So the man is
at liberty, awaiting further developments.
According to Bendigo (Vic.) Advertiser,
two White Hills persons, with their wives and
families —hitherto ordinary, sober-minded indi-
viduals, with nothing visibly deficient about them
—have taken up the parable of J. A. Dowie
with emendations and expurgations, and have
started out to found a new religion. One
Joshua Jeffrey, or Jeffreys, or Jeffries, an
alleged prophet and pilgrim, with long hair,
flowing black whiskers, and clerical haberdashery,
lately pervaded the district and captured the
emotions of these two White Hills citizens,
Messrs. Watson and Flitterer, who are now
starting a “world-wide mission.” They have nob
as yet adopted a name, but should certainly call
themselves Peripatetics or Flitterers, inasmuch as
they deem it wicked to have the luxuries of a
home, and consider themselves enjoined by the
Book of Daniel and the Revelations of St. John
to only hang out temporarily, and to perpetually
move along like the Perambulating Hebrew.
Anyhow, the two have realised their property,
and started out to convert the world at large.
Nothing definite appears to be known about their
creed, apart from the fact that they propose to
pursue a rheumatic dogma in the open air; and
that their faith—if they can persuade other people
to Flitter along with them —will destroy the value
of real estate. For if the whole community begins
to Flitter, then all the big buildings of Melbourne
will be given over to stray dogs and flying foxes.
* * *
Some curious reports of that recent
episode when the devil came to Cootamundra :
It appears that the few persons who were about heard
a roaring noise, which was immediately followed by a
fire-ball, which seemed to strike White’s Globe Hotel. . .
Constable Manglesdorf, who saw the fire-ball, says he
and Constable Wilson walked on to the middle of the
roadway, and soon after there was a roaring noise. They
then noticed sheets of iron and other building material
scattered about the road. He remarked to his mate
that there had been a cyclone. . . . Another feature of
the occurrence was that Constable Wilson lost his cap,
and afterwards found it on the other side of the street.
As he did not feel any strong wind at the time, the
disappearance of his cap was peculiar. . . . One of the
boarders, named Wright, who occupied one of three beds
in a room, heard a roaring noise, and thinking something
terrible was going to happen, covered his head with a
pillow.
One spectator named Cohen, by the way, saw
either one or three large illuminated balls rushing
down the street. He only spoke of one, but he
probably suppressed the other two lest it should
seem like needless advertisement.*

Queensland detectives still visit the house
of Mrs. Murphy at Gatton, and from time to
time put the sad old woman through a catechism
dealing with the mysterious Christmas tragedy of
years ago.
* • * •
This is a very long ago tale of two Austra-
lian brothers who might have been named Smith,
but weren’t. When one married his choice
fell on a milliner. His brother married a
servant. Mrs. Milliner Smith refused to
know Mrs. Slavey Smith, and ill-feeling
raged. But after a while Mrs. Slavey
Smith’s husband got into Parliament,
whereby his wife became respectable,
and Mrs. Milliner trotted along with
her card. Mrs. Slavey gave a wild
whirroo in her expensive drawing-room,
and replied that she wasn’t in, and didn’t
expect to be. More ill-feeling. Then
Mrs. Milliner Smith’s husband got into
Parliament, and rose much more rapidly
than Mrs. Slavey’s husband. The latter
lady trotted along and sent in her card.
Then Mrs. Milliner did a whirroo in
her still more expensive drawing-
room, and sent out a message that
she was dead. About this time,
however, the husbands, who were
on the same side in politics, and
found these hostilities awkward, in-
terposed, and, after much negotia-
tion, the ladies agreed not to whir-
roo any more.

The election of N.S. W. general
divisional representative to the
Federal Public Service Appeal
Board seems like ending in a
sectarian squabble. The dominant
Postal dept, is putting two candi-
dates in the field, representing dif-
ferent factions, and is fighting with
great vigor. One party alluded to the other as
the “scum of the service,” while the “scum”
accuses the “ cream ” of raising the sectarian issue.
It seems to be a row between the scum at the top
and the sediment at the bottom.

By way of warning the good-natured, fools
who sign and swear things indiscriminately—the
other day a man was allotted six months in Bathurst
(N.S.W.) gaol for wilfully swearing to a false
declaration in connection with an old-age pension
claim. He knew the applicant well, and swore,
probably in a good-natured, irresponsible way, to
his spotless character, and when it transpired that
the applicant had 18 convictions against him
locally, not to mention three prohibition orders,
the State was justly annoyed. The only cliarac-
ters a man who values himself should vouch for
are those of his ancestors who have been dead 25
years at least, and that of his dog. And it is best
to wait till the dog is dead, too.

A neat theft that
happened one morn-
ing lately in one of
Sydney's long, nar-
row grocery stores
sho wed traces of origi-
nality. A well-dressed
woman, the only cus-
tomer in the shop,
sidled up to the pro-
vision counter, and
blushed violently.
Then she leaned
across the counter
and admitted in a
terrified whisper to
the provision hand
that her petticoat was
falling off, so would
he mind excusing
himself, until she tied
things up! Me was
young and bashful, so
he threw down the
ham he had been
deftly pruning the
scraggy ends from,
and excused himself
in haste to the other
end of the counter,
where he began pack-
ing sugar to cool him-
self off. The dis-
tressed visitor seemed
a long while; but at
last she finished,
thanked him, and
passed out with a
decided limp and a
piece of string trail-
ing behind her. Next
minute he noticed
that the ham was
gone. Tearing down
the street he saw his
departed friend dis-
appearing through
the side-door of a dis-
tant pub, and just
arrived in time to
find her in a room
behind the bar,
leisurely untying the
ham from her leg.
A lower court in N.S.W. has given the
eminently - satisfactory decision that premises
rented and used by the Salvation Army are liable
8 for municipal purposes. The verdict
13 to be appealed against, of course, for the sects
nowadays only render unto Caesar what is Caesar’s
when Caesar dynamites them; but it is an emi-
nently satisfactory decision to go on with. If it
isn’t law now, it should be law. The statutes
should empower State and municipality to tax all
religious and semi-religious buildings. The
various exemptions granted to people because
they are supposed to be religious or charitable
cloak many frauds on the revenue. Put all on the
one footing j tax church, parsonage and religious
sweating factory, and let the various sects make it
up in the collection-plate. The Government is
the greatest charitable institution in Australia,
and to rob it in order to fatten “ William Booth,
trading as the Salvation Army,” is sacrilege,
• * « »
A case of alleged assault in a railway car-
riage at Prince’s Bridge station, Melbourne, was
tried a few days ago. The girl’s yarn was that,
whilst sitting alone in the carriage, a man entered,
took a seat opposite, and then straightway
attempted to assault her. She complained to the
occupants of the adjoining carriages, when the
defendant got out and hurriedly walked away,
Defendant stated that he had only stooped to tie
his boot-lace, when the girl of excessive virtue
“ hollered out.” As for walking away when she
began to imagine things—well, any sane person
who reads the papers would endeavor to get out
of the locality in a violent hurry under the same
circumstances. A sensible jury, taking into con-
sideration that the train was at a standstill at one
of Melb.’s busiest platforms, and probably re-
fleeting that he would have to be a hardened and
desperate ruffian who would attempt an “assault”
under such conditions, said plainly that it didn’t
believe the girl’s tale and discharged the accused.
The next sweet young person will probably lay
the scene of the outrage on the top of a ’bus while
it is wending its weary way along George-street,
Sydney, in broad daylight,
.* * * .- *
Maoriland Calvinists have always pointed
to Dunedin as the model city where the suburban
trams didn’t run on the Sawbath ; hut now, alas,
the pagans are prevailing, and there is serious talk
of running the trams and risking the consequences,
What those consequences are likely to be hasn’t
yet been settled. Some think a fireball will fall
on Job Wain’s hotel; others that all the cows in
Otago will run dry and the butter business peter
out.
A RECENT LETTER FROM FIJI.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is trouble in Fiji, where the cocoa-nut trees be,
There is bother in those islands ’twixt the missionary
clans ;
Lo ! the Roman Cath-o-lics are all armed with knotted
sticks,
And'the paths are filled with snorting, aggravated Wes-
ley-ans.
There is prancing on the shore, where the peoples cry
for gore.
And the rival gospel dealers split each other’s creeds in
twain ;
Whilst the Bibles in Fiji blaze and crackle merrilee,
And the native teacher whoops and digs the war-club up
again.
Where Fijian bush is dense the nigger’s soul will hasten
hence
When the warring Methodist has got the Papist by the
ear;
And the Romans in their turn will rejoice ■’mid tree and
fern,
And the land be filled with piety and blood and strife
and beer.
And when the holy books are burned Peace’s hat may be
discerned
Rising like a star of hope on the horizon of the sea ;
But at present there is strife where they sell the Bread
of Life,
And the Bibles on the islands are blazing merrilee.
W.A.
GRANT HERVET.
A MEMORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Love, you remember that glad night in June
When faintest winds were blowing
Rose-scent and musk athwart the dusk
And when the shimmering opal of the moon
Flashed in the river ; how that golden tune,
In perfect measure flowing—
Each note a falling pearl, a fairy gem—
Crowned Love with Music’s mystic diadem
Sing on, sing on, dear Love,
Sing on for evermore !
But, ah ! My Love was borne from me
With cypress wreaths before.
O.C.C.
RESOLUTIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DAINTY damsel said to me,
“ What will your plans for this year be?
As resolutions now are rife,
What are your vows for New Year life ?
I thought awhile, then slowly said :
“ These are my plans till I am dead :
Maiden charms I shall despise ;
My admiration I’ll disguise .”
She said, “ Such plans could never live.
Your folly, though, I shall forgive.
Allow me now to state my own:
They are, fair sir, to break yours down!
GENERAL.

Next Hospital Saturday there should be
some attractive young and old men out with
collecting boxes—especially old men. It is the
rich and wicked old man who is wanted—the
burnt-out volcano with the leer of a sinful past in
his countenanoe —the rake who hasn’t reformed,
and doesn’t recognise that he is too old to rake
any more. He must be rich, because if he
isn’t he can’t have had enough Past—Past
is a very expensive thing if it is to be
the fight kind of Past. Also he should be
a bachelor or a widower as well as rich ; then
there is no knowing when the bright innocent
face of some young thing who drops 3d. into his
box may move him to settle down (matrimonially)
and reform. But he must be old so that he
won’t settle "down too long—in this world, of
oourse. Tasmanian ex-Premier Braddon would
bring in a fortune with a collecting box.
He is old, reasonably affluent, and has a sort
of a title. Also, though lie probably never had a
sinful Past, he looks as if he had had a dozen of
them. Braddon is in appearance the wicked old
earl of the London Journal to a hair, with the
drooping moustache, the aristocratic features, the
crows-feet and all the other furnishings. Standing
on the Block with a collecting box he could lift
the debt off any hospital single handed.

Australia has stood with equanimity
various suggestions that the scales of justice are
not held on a true balance as between rich and
poor. The whole continent, however, is vocifer-
ously aghast concerning the tampering with the
weighing, scales which hold the balance between
horse and horse at Newcastle. That is a crime
which touches the great heart of the nation.

Besides Bent, Leah Abrahams, and other
troubles, Victoria is just now suffering from an
alleged rain-making shindykit, consisting of 39
Srsons, under the guidance of one C. de Lacey
acarthy, M.A. and M.D. Macarthy says he
go# his water-producing secret in Yankeeland,
HOSPITAL SATURDAY.—WHAT IT WILL COME TO SOME DAY.
and he doesn’t need any clouds or vapor strata,
but can fetch a first-class deluge out of the blue
vault of heaven at a few days’ notice. His secret
is understood to be a chemical one, and that’s all
that’s known about it. He tried it at Inglewood
last week, and the rain duly arrived ; but as it
also transpired over nearly the whole of three
States, and as several common or Government
weather prophets duly forecasted it, the experi-
ment was by no means as conclusive as Maoarthy
reckons. If the gentleman with the Irish name
and the Yankee invention will go out to the back
of Bourke, and bring on a heavy downpour once a
month for, say, six months, he may begin to think
he has done something unusual. If he can do
what he claims he may confidently reckon on a
permanent billet from the Commonwealth Govern-
ment at about £1,000,000 a year.

Poor kid, doomed to oow-slavery even from his

“Nil ” on still another aspect of the
case:—
The champion chrysanthemum, the prize sow, the
trapeze girl, and the artist’s model have a chance (when
old age smites them hip and thigh leaving but a wreck)
to recall to the eye the beauties that were. But the
“ respectable ” young woman of any class must be con-
tent with the shadow of her body on the wall or its fleet-
ing reflection in the bath. And why ? She may have a
record of her facial charms (if she have any) and wel-
come. But the girl with the (ah ! so rare) perfect figure
must preserve an inviolable secrecy about it except down
to the upper chest, and as far as it can be suggested and
hinted at by cunning tricks of dress. The old, old
woman who lives again in her handsome granddaughter
may show a miniature of her face and half her bosom and
say, “ Ah ! my dear, you would hardly think it, but that’s
what I looked like at your age." But of the rest a
strange silence ; the hypocrisy of ages of convention.
The present scribe has about as much sentimentality as a
tin tack, but.he confesses to a sense of the pathos of the
(nearly) perfect girl condemned never to be seen ; whose
body is the loveliest work of Nature upon which no eyes
but her own may rest; who is imbued with a natural vanity
not only pardonable but proper to all normal females,
and who may not even have a counterfeit presentment of
her all too fleeting charms wherewith to comfort her
in time of waning. And so, here’s to the Melbourne
swimming girl, and more power to her for a sensible
wench 1
* * • *
The Sunday Railsitter has been running
a “discussion ” about women who are starved by
their husbands; and the number of ladies who
have spread themselves on the subject is simply
astonishing. That there are some women who
are kept on short allowance by their husbands is
true ; but against that many men would be abso-
lutely starved to death by their wives but for the
counter-lunch. The woman who fries the fish
with cold dripping is worse than a murderess;
and the other fiend who regularly cooks the vege-
tables without salt and serves up the beans in a
parboiled state is more nefarious than a baby-
farmer. To fry the steak without first bashing it
on a slab with a hunk of timber is a greater in-
jury than to slay the baker and the butcher, and
to fry the steak at all is an offence compared with
which bigamy is small, harmless and ornamental.

“ Bill ” sends this lie :
Scene on St. Kilda (Vic.) railway station. Well-known
politician arrives with his bicycle. Bicycle crippled.
Well-known politician interviews booking clerk and tries
to borrow screw-wrench for bicycle. Olerk can’t find
one. Gomes out and calls the porter, who tries to fix up
machine with ticket-clips, but is unsuccesful. Politician
waits till train comes in. Asks engine-driver for screw-
wrench. Engine-driver gets off and gets to work screw-
ing up machine. Engine shunts with one man. Train
delayed while politician’s bike is repaired. Engine-driver
walks down station to get back to engine. Politician
puts machine in guard’s van and rides with it, free, of
course.

Creeve Roe writes :
The Mardi Gras procession has been introduced in a
tentative way in Manly. The Boeuf Gras is a bullock
bought by a local butcher at the Agricultural Show. It
is a large and stately animal and has a blue ribbon tied
to its tail. Imagine how the Fattest member of the
House of Fatmen would look if he were compelled to
wear a coral
rattle and
bells
around bis
neck, and
you can pic-
ture the ex-
pression of
that bul-
lock. A man
leads it
along the
Corso and
people pass-
ing by stop
and feel its
ribs. “This
is my
fancy,” says
one, "slap-
ping it on
the rump.
“ This for
me,” says
another,
punching it
in the bris-
ket. The
bullock
knows very
well what
they are
saying.
You can see
that by the
scornful
droop of its
upper lip.
It seems to
be growing
thinner,
too. Which
reminds me
of a goose
that was be-
ing fed up
for Christ-
mas in a
boarding house at which I stayed once upon a time.
They had it in a cage at the bottom of the yard
and the boarders used to feel it every morning to find out
how much heavier it might have grown during the night.
The goose knew and finally refused to take food. It died,
a mere feathered skeleton, a week before Christmas. The
Manly bullock is at intervals halted outside of the shop of
the butcher. You can almost hear it saying “I, who am
about to die, salute thee 1” as it looks at that enter-
prising tradesman wielding his furious cleaver. What
adds to the cynical effect is the fact that it has a cast in
its eye.

Dear Bulletin, —You’re wrong in stating
(B. 11/4/’O3) that the underpaid Australian clerks
are “so revoltingly poor-spirited that they won’t
revolt.” That’s nob where the fish is hooked.
It’s their brainless, empty pride thab prevents
them from bettering their position by forming a
union, and “ haw —lowering themselves to the
level of a—haw—vulgar coal-lumper.” Before
last election asked a haughty three-inch-collar-
wearing-225. 6d.-a-week invoice-clerk in a sweat-
ing Sydney drapery establishment if he intended
to vote for the labor candidate of his electorate.
He stabbed me to the heart with a look of indig-
nation and lisped : “Haw—do you take me for a
common coal-heavah ? ” —The Domainiac.
A conscientious Chinese witness in New-
town (Sydney) police court licked one side of the
Bible all over a few days ago—licked it carefully
and thoroughly, as a cat does its kitten. When he
had got that side pretty clean, he was about to
turn the scripture over and clean the other side,
when the sergeant told him the holy volume was
sufficiently washed and business would now pro-
ceed.

While Bent was raving about the iniquitous
Victorian railway employee who goes “ bung ”
just for the malicious satisfaction of casting dis-
credit on the Bully administration, he counted
without another person indirectly affected. One
of these has just made an appearance in the insol-
vency court, and sets her troubles down to “loss
of boarders through retrenchment in the railway
department ’’—such boarders having, presumably,
to look for oheaper quarters and a thinner variety
of hash.
• • * •
Sydney police department has lately been
making a half-hearted attack on fortune-tellers,
palmists and other small frauds. Several of those
ladies, through being too interested with the dis-
guised policeman’s hands to notice the size of his
feet, have fallen in and been bound over to be of
good behaviour for various periods, and to come
up for sentence when called upon. By the way,
the very respectable S.M. Herald, a paper which
blushes at the word “stocking” and alludes to
that harmless, necessary bag as an article of
woman’s apparel, published the advertisement of
a “clairvoyant” on the very day when that
bowled out woman answered to her name at
Darlinghurst. This is a side issue, but it would
be interesting to know how “Mr. James” would
stand for “ aiding and abetting ” if the law were a
little more vigorously administered.

“L.W.”: Last Saturday saw a mob of
howling lunatics in tip-drays, trollies, sulkies,
donkey-carts (most appropriate vehicle), drags,
carriages, boxes on wheels drawn by ragged and
filthy urchins, and motor cars, procesh through
several of the principal streets of Sydney, some
of them heavily congested with traffic. It was
the University Commemoration, and it howled,
screeched, and blew all manner of wind instru-
ments to the great danger of people who were
driving horses within a quarter of a mile.
It was the sort of enjoyment that might
be reasonably expected to be on view at a
lunatic asylum after six months hard drinking. If
a crowd of wharf-lumpers, grocers’ assistants or
any other class of workers were guilty of one-
tenth of the programme which the University
fools carried out over and over again, the Riot
Aot would be read, and every member of:
the offending party tried for obstructing traffic,
gaoled for being a public nuisance, or committed:
to a lunatic asylum for straight jacket and padded
cell treatment. Why the police authorities per-
mit such disgraceful exhibitions in the public-
streets is only explainable by the fact that there
are different kinds of law for different varieties:
of push.

It is a melancholy reflection on W. C.
Wentworth’s proposal to establish an hereditary-
peerage in N.S.W. that his own old home,
Vaucluse, is in the market. A bag or portmanteau,
peerage, which has no old hereditary residence,
but humps its swag from place to place, Mill,
never look like the real thing, and the Australians
don’t seem able to hang on to the old shanty for
more than two or three generations. An Arabian
nobility may hang out in a tent, but that kind
would never satisfy imported British ideas.
OVERHEARD AT A MELBA CONCERT.
MUCHAIR : “What did you think of Melba’s
rendering of the mad scene from ' Hamlet?’ ”
SINGLETON: “Wonderful, wonderful! My hair
fairly stood on end.”
MUCHAIR : “ That’s singular .”
The Review of Reviews, in a recent
article, complains that Australians have no sense
of humor. But then hitherto no native of this
Commonwealth, other than the kookaburra, lias
had much to laugh at. When the chance comes,
we laugh heartily enough. We laughed wildly at
Brassey’s fallings-off, and Wallington’s nose made
us laugh, and when we saw the little York “ in-
vesting” some knights we laughed something
tremendous, and when we read about the floggings
in the Grenadier Guards Heaven only knows how
we did laugh. Also Wilson Barrett’s Claudian
made us laugh till we nearly died.
• * * *
A jury at Arrnidale (N.S. W.) circuit court,
last week, asked Acting-Chief Justice Stephen for
lunch. It was mid-day and the jury had just re-
tired. His Honor said that the case was so
simple it was not thought necessary to provide a
meal. At 3 o’clock he would order lunch. At
3.15 the jury (victuals not having turned up) found
a verdict. The Judge reminded the jury that in
olden days they would have been starved into a
verdict. Which seemed unnecessary. A fat man
of large appetite (there is always one such on a
jury) begins to starve by 2.30.
HOSPITAL SATURDAY.
(The Plaint of Gayboy Hardup.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To dodge the girls 1 Each smiling fay,
Tricked out to cozen us to pay,
From all Convention’s trammels free 1
How full of spite is Fate to me,
That I, while most old chums are gay,
Must take some unfrequented way,
And hurry on in dire dismay ;
Forced by the Ourse of Poverty
To dodge the girls 1
My clothes are far gone towards decay,
I may not join the sportive play 1
I swear no burning lake, or sea
Of fiery billows, e’er can be
Such hell as this—that I to-day
Must dodge the girls 1
ULOOLA.
THE GIRL WHO STAYED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The greatest problem of the age
Was puzzled o’er by many a sage.
And statesmen even of high degree
Had wondered why such things should be.
Why “ helps ” should seek the factory’s noise
With dearth of sweet domestic joys
When in safe, sheltered homes they still
Might scrub in peace and fear no ill!
Now Mrs. Brown was pale and small;
She was not wise or great, at all.
Yet, like the sages of the land,
She sought this thing to understand.
And with her woman’s heart at last
She solved the social problem vast.
Within her chamber (all unswept)
Sat Jane, their latest help, and wept
Over the woes of blue-eyed Pearl
Pursued by Branksome’s sinful Earl.
Then Mrs. Brown with burning glance
Came to this child of young Romance.
And took that simple girl aside,
And sobbed awhile ; then groaned and cried,
“ My girl, ere in this home you rest,
Where all with innocence seems blest;
“ Oh 1 hear my words and shun all hurt;
My husband is a desperate flirt.”
(This statement —I must write it down—
Was libel black on Mr. Brown ;
But when a “ slavey’s ’’ in the case,
A husband takes a second place.)
“ If you your way should chance to miss
On staircase dark, I fear a kiss
“ He might attempt,” wept Mrs. 8.,
“80, simple girl, I bid you flee.
“ His soul is dark, his heart is bold . . •
•Twas right to tell it, and I’ve told."
A year has passed since first they met,
And hopeful Jane is stopping yet.
C.C.
LIGHT KISSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I played aft love as a strange, sweet game—
Careless, laughing, cruel, maybe—
Wooed eyes of brown, and blue, and grey,
With mocking fervor, fancy free ;
Kissed lips, and other lips as sweet,
With seeming warmth, in careless fun,
JJut without a throb or a quick heart-beat
For e’er a one.
But I’ve done with the lying make-believe,
The mocking, loveless, paltry sham ;
Weighed with regret and useless grief,
Light kisses leave me as I am.
1 learnt the sting of the careless kiss,
With its taunting glimpse of love unwon,
For the girl I love just kissed me once—
Just once—in fun.
via
J. M'CLELLAND.

RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 6, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini,—
After the manner of such annual foolishness,
the Sydney University Commemoration wheel
came full circle last Saturday. Town Hall was
packed. You can’t shut your eyes to the fact
that it’s not because of a dreary reading of a weary
list of benefactors, or a conferring of degrees, or
professorial speeches, that the function is packed,
but because people long to see what new kind of
idiots the students may make of themselves. One
of them got off a gag whioh produced a scream
df joy. You can tickle a Commem. crowd with
the decimal of a straw. A girl grad., coming up
for her degree, gave her hand to Chancellor
Normand MacLaurin in the affected shake-hand-
fashion that was once the vogue, and the Chan-
cellor held the hand quite a long time. There
was a flash of silence in the hall, and out of it
came a request in a tender tone: “Call me
Normand, dear! ”
One women grad, made a correct courtesy.
The others gave masculine nods to the Chan-
cellor, and with the exception of one male grad,
the presence of Governors Tennyson and Rawson
was ignored by the degree-getters. Such a host
of women B’s.A. and M’s.A. in the body
of the hall women who had won their
hoods and gowns long ago. Wonder what use
they make of what a Sydney man once called
their “blotting paper education.” I know a
man who owns several B.A. daughters of whom
he proudly says, “To talk to them you would
never think they knew a thing.”
At the annual great noise of Sydney Uni. the
loudest enthusiasm was evoked by the appear-
ance of Mr. M‘William, who is the first blind
student to receive a degree at our Alma Mater.
Said that his teaching had been entirely viva voce.
Sultan of Johore and his auriferous smile are
expected in Sydney shortly. Some years ago this
potentate, who sultans 9000 square miles at the
southern tip of the Malay Peninsula, lost his own
teeth in an accident, but had them replaced by
others wrought by the goldsmith, the diamond-
setter, and the dentist. His smile has a right to
the adjective dazzling. He must be quite English,
you know, for when he was told that there was a
doubt as to whether he and his teeth would be
allowed to land at Fremantle (W.A.) he replied :
“Very well! But I won’t buy any more horses
in the beastly country.” It was said in the
Oxford bleat, no doubt. “ Beastly ”is so English.
Besides, the Sultan had a breach of promise case
brought against him when he was in London a
short time ago, and which he won on the ground
that the court had no jurisdiction over a sovereign
prince.
Decline in popularity of the sailor hat is notice-
able. Milliners have talked it down and dis-
couraged it all they knew, for there is no place
on a sailor hat for half a mile of tucked chiffon,
or an ostrich plume, or a botanical garden, and
therefore no profit. By the way, American dress-
makers are trying all they know to fight down the
shirt-waist, or what we call “the blouse.” It is
declared bourgeoise, and a menace to the profits of
the hard-working dressmaker. “ How can we
expect to do a successful business, ” asked one of
the speakers at a meeting of the National Dress
Association, “if shirt-waists, which are in the
fashion, can be bought at the stores at from 8 to
12 dollars ? ” Same speaker warned her hearers
against the “dressmaker face/” It is a strained,
agonised expression that settles on the modiste’s
face, especially in Spring time, when the dress
creator is on a hunt after ideas and patterns.
He was the dustman in an up-country town in
N.S.W., and when he fell and fractured an arm
he was taken to the hospital, given a good wash,
put into a clean night-gown and deposited in bed.
After night-fall he arose stealthily, wrapped in a
blanket, and fled back to his shanty, where he told
his mates that he only escaped the coffin by flight.
Everything else—washing, shrouding and laymg-
out—the hospital authorities had done. It is a
painful state of things when an Australian citizen
doesn’t recognise an ordinary wash when it comes
his way.
Lloyd concert co. had a half-hour in Sydney
harbor on the Sunday previous to its departure
that it will remember for a space. The co. had
spent a pleasant fore and afternoon running about
in a little steam launch, and was returning home,
when just off Pinchgut, after dark, a squall
of wind and rain (distinguished enough to merit
special press notices next morning) struck the
craft. The vessel started to climb hills of
seas Olympus high, and the wind blew at
the rate of 45 miles an hour. The owner of
the toy vessel took the wheel, or whatever
he calls the steering-gear, and in pauses of
the “hills of seas” breaking over him, he
entreated Miss Foxcroft and Peterkin not to
stop singing. And they didn’t stop except when
the top of a hill fell down their throats. It was
great nerve. Mrs. Peterkin gave herself up for
lost; tenor Lloyd sat tight and tried to reassure
her. The impressario on board swam about in the
• bottom of the boat looking for a valuable box of
cigars, bub his wife collapsed into a bundle of
clothes with a moan inside it.
After six years’ absence, Mark Hambourg, the
star Russian pianist (does his Czar know he’s
out ?) tore the music out of his Erard grand piano
(which he brought with him) at Sydney Town
Hall last Saturday. Hambourg is magnificent.
He has several different sorts of springs and
hinges in his wrists and elbows and fingers. He
is as charged with electricity as a dynamo, and at
the piano he looks like a new kind of bear. lam
very fond of bears, but I object to men imitating
them. I would as soon listen to Laurence Philip
as to Hambourg. So would scores of others who
heard him, but they’re afraid of the other scores
biting derisive thumbs at them if they confess.
The well-known Chopin study in G. flat, as
played by Mr. Hambourg, was like unto a
splendid storm of thunder, lightning and pelting
rain on our Blue Mountain peaks. Hambourg’s
wonderful hands ought to be watched through
your glasses. One tremulous, slender thread of
sound, no louder than a fairy’s whisper, yet which
oan be heard at the end of the hall, he produces,
looking the while into vacancy ; then swiftly lift-
ing his strong white forepaws, half closed, high
above the keyboard, as if he were conjuring the
music from on high. “Come, my dainty Ariel,”
he seems to say, “lend me your aid; just drop
some jessamine petals on these notes and ” so
finished up the Beethoven group, which began
with the Appassionata Sonata.

The Box and the Woman had possession of
Sydney and its suburbs last Saturday from dawn
till dusk. Over £4300 was collected, inclusive of
tons of copper and a large assortment of buttons,
match-box tops, tram tickets, and pennies covered
with tinfoil. There was also a snapshot photo, of
Mr. Robertson Clarke, Governor Rawson’s P.S.,
and a £5O note. Who is the Pierpont Morgan
who gave that half-hundred ? The collec-
tion was only a few hundreds less than that of
last year, which is pretty good considering the
drought. Also, probably the drought relief street-
collection, which took place some few months
back, may have detracted from the success of the
hospital bail-up. A favorite trick of the badgered
male at this last annual worry was to feign to be
deaf.
Maoriland greenstone is becoming fashionable
in London as a lucky stone. Girls are wearing it
in the form of necklets or pendants, but more
generally as blouse sleeve-links and studs. Any
stone that gets the preface “lucky” is sure of
attention, and is constantly worn for a
time “for luck.” Then the fashion dies, the
wearer not getting the desire of her heart in
everything. In a Sydney jeweller’s, I have just
seen a wonderful bit of opal in the heart of a knob
of ironstone. It shot arrows of violet and ruby
and amber, and seemed full of angry genii trying
to struggle out of the ironstone prison. At Mark
Hambourg’s concert there was a woman wearing
three opal finger-rings of extraordinary size and
fire. She was a kind woman to those behind her,
for she patted her back hair five times a minute.
She carried her gloves in her hands—a boxing-
glove would hardly have gone over the ringed
hand.
Miss Elyda Russell, the Sydney singer who has
just got such good notices in London on her
debut on the concert platform, is a niece of
P. N. Russell, who donated £50,000 to Sydney
University. This millionaire lives in London.
He is childless. Miss Thea. Proctor, the Sydney
artist so well known for her poster work and
book cover designs, is a grand-niece of the same
affluent person. Miss Proctor is now in London,
haunting picture galleries and deepening her
color sense.
.Florence Schmidt, the Queensland soprano,
was married to Sculptor Frank Derwent Wood
in London last March. Signor Steffani gave the
bride away, but the wedding was very quiet, and
there was no honeymoon trip—save to Florence’s
new home in South Kensington. Australasian’s
London correspondent records the engagement of
actor Titheradge's youngest daughter to Kenneth
Lambert, an engineer and manager of a big
South African mine.
In a very dull article in the Review of Reviews
“ A Tired Australian ” denies the gift of humor
to any Australian writer. Well, anyhow, a
section of our people is humorous. Last week in
a oountry centre prayers were offered for the
recovery of a gentleman of 95, who was seriously
ill.
