Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Said that the late Baronet W. Clarke paid
£lBOO for the lace, made to the order of a royal
bride, .whose marriage was postponed owing to
the death of her husband-elect. When the
deferred ceremony was performed later, with
important changes in the cast, the bride wore her
orange-blossom with a difference and changed the
fashion of her wedding-laces along with her bride-
groom. Since the ex-Royal Honiton arrived in
Australia it has been kept in the bank, whither
it returned after the Clarke-Lindsay wedding.
There are still two Misses at Cliveden, so the
‘ May-blossom lace ’ will probably see more service
before its pronounced ‘ivory tone’ grows too
tawny for beauty.
“The Clarke bride, now the Hon. Mrs. Lind
say, is understood to have garnered in £5OO in
wedding-cheques, plus 400 other gifts, mostly
silver things, though the Cliveden widow gave
diamond necklet and pendant, and the bride-
groom a long chain of pearls. Latter is credited
locally with possessing little beyond his pay and,
of course, the allowance, which every British
officah and gentleman must extort from his people
to enable him to dress, polo, etc., up to the tradi-
tions of the service, doncherknow.
“Still, wifie has a paltry £l5OO a year or so.
Papa Crawford, the bridegroom’s paternal Earl,
is reported to be on his way to Melbourne in his
yacht, and he is reputed to spend many thousands
of pounds on rare books, so there ought to be
more than crusts and cold tea in the Lindsay
larder.
“ T’other day, when the George Coppins gave a
party at their dear old house in Richmond to
welcome the Bland Holts back to Melbourne, the
evergreen host seemed quicker in ‘ the uptake ’
than nine out of ten people one meets. Most of
the * Midsummer Night ’ mummers were on view.
Titania (Norah Kerin) has lovely eyes and hair.
Oberon (Miss Rignold) is handsome and pic-
turesque. Hermia (Miss Cynthia Brooke) is a
decorative sort of picture-girl. There is some
wonderful millinery among the ladies of the
‘Dream’ troupe, when seen in private life-
hats all curtained-in, &c. A bed-curtained broad-
brim would answer splendidly for church in
summer. Draw your curtains and snatch 40
winks in sermon-time and no scandal would be
caused, thanks to the sheltering portieres.
“ The winter woman looks like a tea-cosy. The
shoulder slope and the muffled look of things con-
spire to that effect. From the chiffon days of
trail and flutter to these clean-sweep woollen
times is a rare jump. The scanty skirt brings
about fresh attention to hose —an article lost a
good deal to sight and thought in the trailing
days. Then the bustling lady didn’t bother if
her stockings weren’t mates. Even if the petti-
coats were hoisted a bit, only one stocking showed
at a time, and the aesthetic situation was saved.
Also many a hole at the heel went unregarded.
Now all that is changed. Shoes and stockings
shall be flawless, with gaiters to fill the bill where
ankles are not up to form. These gaiters are
being made neat and saucy—two varieties.
‘ ‘ Evening bodices are being scissored straight
across, an inch or two above the bust line, and
suspended from the shoulders by little more than
a hope and an autumn leaf. The barer the top-
storey the smarter the turn-out. The entire arm
is shown in full regalia—vaccination punctures
included. For half-and-half wear—draughty hall
or small party use—long sweeping lace and chiffon
flappers are to flow from the funny-bone. When
the upper decks defy grease, massage and all
fattening treatment, chiffon swathings are to
foam about the bony parts. And this calls to
mind the great labor involved in a toilette of the
day. Hours are given over to beautification and
extensive calisthenics before the reflecting glass
ere the social world is faced. The sex pays in
this way for perpetual kittenism.
“The nuptials of Mr. TTllathorne, celebrated a
month or two ago in the Big Smoke, are of interest.
The gentleman was married some years ago to a
St. Kilda beauty. „ A separation followed immedi-
ately on the knot-tying, and, after years of
negotiation with the R.C. church authorities, the
lovely Mrs. Ullathorne succeeded in securing a
divorce, with the Pope’s permission, last year.
The lady is in Melbourne under her maiden name.
“ The estrangement of Melbourne’s two leading
families is a toothsome morsel for the Toorak
chatterers. The formal dignity maintained
towards one another in public only makes the
gossips grin the more.
“ A motherly ancient associated with the local
Suffrage movement makes a point of addressing
mixed meetings in the form—‘Gentlemenand Gen-
tlewomen.’ A younger, more trenchant fighter
comes forth with a hit-the-nail-on-the-head start
of ‘Fellow-citizens!’ The whole hall stops
blinking then, and gives prompt attention.
Another she-speaker collars the crowd with
an unmincing form, briefly—‘Men ,and Women !’
Miss Sutherland, the grand foster-mother of
hundreds of infants, had her turn at addressing
a Town Hall throng a little while ago. She
shuffled forward—a short, stout woman, in heavy
boots, rough serge, a straw hat strung on with
an elastic band round her back knob and a big
blush covering her fine face. With one word she
took the whole audience in her fold —‘Friends !’
Every time she came gently down with her brolly
in her enthusiasm, the males of the audience —the
unemployed that had just drifted into the hall
with the free tide—yelled their delight. All the
poor, the unemployed, the town stragglers, the
police and the pressmen know Miss Sutherland
and have a large respect for her. ‘Tell me,
straight,’ she said, ‘ what is any home without a
woman at the head ? You know, and I know,
what the motherless home is. Then give woman
the vote, and own up to her worth in the public
weal.’ And that was the best point of the meeting.
“ I was idly ruminating on our bantam Chief-
Justice as he spread himself for the evening strut
home, via Collins-street, the other day, and
wondering if an inward satisfaction mated that
complacency of feature, when a dirty urchin shot,
comet-like, into sight, and all but collided with
the Waxcellency. He roared ‘ ’Erald, sir.’
Justice Madden thrust thumb and forefinger into
an outer waist pocket, gathered a brownie, pre-
sented it with grace to the boy, and resumed his
minuet —the paper being elegantly folded and
clasped by the gloved fist which held the fellow
‘ kid ’ and the brolly. Everything done with a
nice eye to effect. The vigilance of such ‘ star ’
performers never sleeps.
“The saunter of Justice Hartley Williams is
most dandified. The figure is svelte and the
clothes the greyest and the newest of their kind.
The face is the nicest-looking of any fringed with
snow-white whiskers in a community addicted to
much hair-dye. Justice W. is a consummate artist
in ‘ get-up,’ and, Toorak avers, the one frost-
headed type of English hauteur in our midst. His
departure with the Autumn tints in chilly June
will cause much stylish regret.

the Saturday Toy show—the warblings of the
commanding blonde occurring pleasantly between
the brilliant violin flourishes of Ernest Toy and
the grand piano strummings of Herr Scharf.
Miss Davis has advantages in voice and presence.
A picturesque black and blue turn-out framed a
grand figure. The MacEacharns had the most
imposing party at the recital. The long-haired
youth with the old-young air, who wields a bow
with the cheerful confidence of David soothing
Saul, is a Bendigonian and a former pet of
Malcolm’s dame. Students and the teaching
fraternity mostly formed the audience, the world
of frill being engaged in a grand rustle at ‘ Clive-
den,’ where the returned honeymooners—Captain
and Mrs. Lindsay—were at home to a carefully-
leavened three hundred.

“ The crowding along of concerts brings forcibly
to mind a winter horror—the famous Town Hail
draughts. Now that the cold season has arrived
lovers of music have to favor a Polar garb’
Mayor Gillott is not a concert goer, and doesn’t
know the pain of culture in the throes of chill
In fact, his Worship expresses a great admiration
of his fine hall, forgetting that the warm ‘duds’

and that the she-citizen is constrained to sacrifice
her modish demi-dress whenever she is occupying

“Judge Madden was asked in court this week
to allow £7OO per annum for the education, pocket-
money, ‘medical comforts,’ etc., of two misses
aged 17 and 14 respectively, joint heiresses to
£27,000 odd. Mother of said ‘infants’ already
receives £5OO a year for housing and maintenance
of her two golden goslings, and Waxcellency
could not be induced to sanction the spending of
a penny more than another £450 per annum on
schooling, etc. When counsel suggested that the
1 case of the young ladies should be referred to
the Full Court,’ Chief Justice Madden replied
that he didn’t need any telling ‘about the bringing-
up of a couple of girls.’
“ At present, a daily journal is running a ‘ rose-
Eoll’ and all the curators of local public-gardens
ave named their special ‘fancies,’ as well as,
many enthusiastic amateur growers. So far,
‘ Maman Cochet’ is leading with ‘La France,’
only one vote behind. Queen Rose is perfect in,
her own way, but like all queens, she’s expensive.
Toilers’ gardens generally run to sunflowers*
hollyhocks, etc., rather than roses, not because,,
the owners have gaudy tastes, but because said
flaunters keep on making a brave show in spite of
poor soil and comparatively scant culture. The
kiddies, who grow up with certain flowers, become
attached to the floral friends of their youth and if
you have a giant sunflower in your garden, the
children of the proletariat will audibly admire ift
every time they pass. ‘ Look at the big Sunny !:’
they exclaim, with nevet- an eye for roses or
carnations. Cosmos is another blossom that
flourishes on the cheap. Someone should write
up ‘ how to keeD a garden gay at next to no.
expense. ’
“Womenkind’ here are going in largely for
the vapor tub at. one or two .massage retreats*
the luxurious finding the administration of
steam in the altogether as fascinating as up-
to-date face treatment. There is no doubt
that a wash carried out in scented air to the
tune of gossip in soft tones, and the accom-
paniment of some tender fuss and petting and
pats, is a delightful sensation. When Melbourne
girls are lost for something to do they say, ‘Let
us go and get a clean up,’ and hasten to the
haunts of steam and soap just as in Harborville
they say, ‘ Let us boat it to Manly.’
“Marshall-Hall has fallen captive to ‘ A Mid-
summer Night’s Dream.’ He was unable to
appear at his conservatorium the day after wit-
nessing the fair comedy, telling the students (who
never hear cheap praise of Melbourne perform-
ances from his lips) that the ‘ Dream ’ was so
tangled up in his morning thoughts that work—
the usual round —was impossible. Then every
worshipful soul made haste to go and taste of the
raptures. If Marshall-Hall would say ‘ Girls !
go, have a mud bath and a sulphur gargle,’the
whole lot would rush to obey.
“There will never be a heaven on earth till
automatic household-helps are invented. Even in
Toorak, where competent cooks and qualified
parlor-maids abound and the washing goes to
the laundry, there are domestic rubs. A smart
sporting dame, noted in her spinster days
for bouncing ways and command of ‘ language ’
fell foul of the chef re his dirty kitchen. (The
Toorak kitchens, by the way, are not as a rule
kept like new pins). The man of gravies, re-
sented interference, on the ground that, if his
dishes ‘ ate all right,’ there was nothing to growl
about. His employer talked back, but soon found
that, in spite of her girlish vocabulary feats, she
was nowhere in a ‘ jawing-match ’ with her fire-
flushed opponent. She was wearing a tailor-made
at the time, so it came easy to tear off her coat
and attempt to ‘ punch the offender’s head.’
Cook promptly ‘put up his pins,’ but just then
the gardener and groom grabbed him from the
back, while a string of screaming maids tailed on
to their mistress like a tug-of-war team and hauled
her out of danger.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Among the subscribers to Townsville
Hospital is a Montmorency Ah Bow 1
A. D. Austin, who from 1856 downwards
was constructing engineer for more Maoriland
railways than any other man, passed out last
month. He had been a pensioner for nearly 20
years.
Alleged financial swindler Whitaker
Wright had a great and wise objection to being
photographed, and it was from a sketch by Aus-
tralian artist, Percy Spence, in the Sphere that
he was identified for arrest.
The oldest ’Murkan Consul in Australia is
C. A. Murphy, 30 years resident at Adelaide. He
owned old Adelaide Lantern for ten years, and
also held for six years the now defunct Adelaide
Punch. Is a native of Brooklyn.
“Melb.”; Beer-maker Nick Fitzgerald,
M.L.C., was Archbishop Carr’s right-hand man
at the opening of the Cathedral Hall, Sunday
before last. The object of the hall is to keep
young fellow's away from the public-house.
Thorne, the veteran Queensland journalist,
who has just returned to Adelaide, on his way to
Brisbane, after superintending the publication of
his book, “ The Heresy of Teetotalism, viewed in
the Light of the Bible, Science and Legislation,”
wrote his first published contribution to the press
in 1846 Yet he doesn’t look more than 50 now.
Premier Barton’s second son graduated
B.A. this year at Sydney ’Varsity with first class
honors in the two usual decomposed languages,
Latin and Greek, and carried off the blue ribbon
of the year, the University Gold Medal for
Classics. Both of Premier Barton’s sons have had
successful university careers, the elder one finish-
ing up at Oxford.

One man who thoroughly believes in the
Victorian “reform” movement is W. G. S. Dean,
secretary and boss of the ’Pootra League at £BOO
a year. A great many people are capable of
believing in a great many weird fowls for less than
that.
Fell at the last hurdle at Christchurch,
Jubal Fleming, one of the kings of Maori-
land turf speculation. He conducted big
sweeps on the Tattersall principle until Parlia-
ment made racing consultations illegal and in
their place installed the revenue-making tote.
When the late Charles Bright showed
signs of almost immediate dissolution, his. medical
attendant warned him that he could not live more
than a few hours. The invalid said, “I have
communicated with the Unseen World, and shall
not die for two months.” And he lived two
months exactly.
About the only surviving member of S. A.’s
first Legislative Council is John Carr, now close
on 85. During his 40 years’ residence in the State
he has three times been a Minister. But that
happens to almost everybody in S.A. He got out
of the habit of being a Minister 25 years ago, and
has now left politics.
The old S.A. Governor, Baronet Fergusson,
is talking of coming back like a 71-years old ghost
to revisit Australia. He spent four years in
Adelaide, and buried his first wife there. His
second wife, a South Australian girl, died of
cholera in India, but his third is robust and
energetic. Fergusson also took a short spell as
Governor of Maoriland in the ’7o’s.
Senator Smith, on his way to New Guinea,
was blocked at Thursday Island, and was told he
would have to wait six weeks for a steamer. Said
he hadn’t time, and borrowed a little seven-ton
yawl from the Inspector of Fisheries, provisioned
it for a month, and, with two whites and two
niggers, started across Torres Straits for Port
Moresby, a distance of 200 miles. He got there,
and got back, too.
Touching item from a London paper :
The death is announced from Queenstown of John
Duffy, the petty officer who was selected from the Navy
to act as support for the King during his walking exercise
when his Majesty was convalescent on the royal yacm.
Duffy was the most powerful young fellow on the ship s
company, and on his brawny frame the King leaned. The
deceased had to leave the royal yacht owing to a tempo-
rary derangement of his mind, and died from brain-
disease in his twenty-seventh year.
Powerful young fellow—close contact with loyalty
— brain disease — death. What a warning for
K.C.M.G. politicians 1
The Anglican Bishop of New Guinea,
whom Lieut.-Gov. Way . (S.A.) “at homed
the other day, is not quite the usual apostle.
In his diocese his every day attire consists
mainly of a flannel shirt and a pair of dungaree
trousers. He works, manually, as hard as any
one in the place, doing all sorts of odd jobs, from
pick and shovel labor to rough carpentering.
He goes about among the tribes unarmed, and to
this he in a great measure, ascribes his safety
(up till now). Perhaps if the usually unnecessary
missionary were to leave his gun-boat at home
for a while, it might be better for all concerned.
Recently deceased J. F. Smith (Vic.)
arrived in Melb. as a master mariner in ’53,
became shipkeeper to the prison hulks Success
and Sacramento, and went to the assistance of
Supt. Price when the latter was murdered by
convicts at Williamstown in ’5B. Nine years ago
Smith retired from the position of overseer of
works at Pentridge.
William Forrest, who had been right in
the heart of Queensland’s pastoral business for 40
years, and who died the other day, intended to
follow his profession of civil engineer when he
emigrated to Australia in the “fifties”; but the
gold fever took him, and he never handled a staff
again. Sat as an M.L.C. in the cow and grass
interest for 20 years.
Sydney possesses a criminal of regular
habits and mighty bad luck. On 2nd April, 1891,
he was sentenced to five years for being found by
night with house-breaking implements in his pos-
session ; same charge and same sentence on 2nd
A.pril, 1895 ; ditto ditto on 4th April, 1899 ; ditto
ditto on 6th April, 1903. Each time he got a
year and a few months off his sentence for good
conduct, and was “ found by night with house-
breaking implements in hi* possession ” as soon
as he got out. Yet it doesn’t seem certain that
he broke into any house on any of these occasions.
“F.”: Incorrectly stated that the last
two winners of Melb. Art School’s travelling
scholarship were the Altson Bros. Abey Altson
came seoond on the roll of prize winners; he
followed Longstaff. Quinn, Coates and Meldrum
followed the elder Altson, then came his brother,
the latest winner. The triennial scholarship has
been established since 1887, and the quality of the
competitors has undoubtedly deteriorated during
the past 12 years. It remains for young Altson
to prove that he is equal to his brother, who
Sainted “The Golden Age” for Melb. National
•allery.
Reginald Black, M.L.C., director of many
monetary ventures, and just gone to England for
a trip, is son of John Black,
the early manager of the Bank
of New South Wales, and was
born in the bank premises,
then in George-street, where
Bradley and Co.’s auction
rooms now are. Bartley, a
clerk in the old bank over 50
years ago, wrote some “Remi-
niscences.” He reports that
old John Black lived on the
premises, which were rather
primitive, so that things fre-
quently got mixed. Bailey,
the old, unmarried Scotch
secretary, found a partially
nude infant of the Black family
cavorting in his waste-paper
basket one day, and his re-
marks about “a little naked
savage ” interfering in business
were violent. Old manager
Black used to act as teller
himself in times of pressure,
and, being a rather inefficient
teller, was generally short in
his cash at closing time. Those
were primitive times. The
manager’s nude infant doesn’t
crawl through the bank nowa-
days.
A member of the Com-
monwealth Parliament opened
a set of scrap-books the day
after his election, with the view
of preserving all published
pros, and cons, of his political career. So far he
shows three books chock full of cons, with only a

Broughton, M.L.A., has discovered that
George Reid is “the idol of N.5.W.,” and that
the reason is that he is so “humane and kindly.”
Curiously enough the anecdotes writer has heard
about George have mostly represented him as a
coldly selfish cynic, with a hollow laugh and
a heartless jest for other people’s misfortunes.
Did anyone ever hear a story of a single act of
personal generosity or kindliness on Reid’s part ?
This writer hasn’t.
The late Rev. Chas. Clark, who first
arrived in Melbourne by the s.s. Great
Britain, gave some remarkable evidence, before
the Marine Board, in regard to the suicide of a
fellow passenger who, on the way out, jumped
overboard and was drowned. Said His Rever-
ence: “On seeing the man step on to the
bulwarks I immediately proceeded to divest my-
self of my gloves. Before I could get up to him
he had plunged into the sea.”
N.S.W. Govt, has adopted its usual atti-
tude of timorousness in regard to the Inspector-
Generalship of Police. The present occupant,
Mr. Fosbery, wishes to retire; he is too old to
carry out the duties even to his own satisfaction.
There are about 130 hopeless incapables and one
or two good men anxious to take the vacancy,
and, fearful to incur odium by making a choice,
the Govt, intends to allow Mr. _ Fosbery to con-
tinue in office with an “ Assistant Inspector-
General ” —Superintendent Garvin.
Mr. Candler has now completed his 60th
year as a coroner in Victoria. In 1853 he was
appointed coroner for the district lying between
Avoca and the Loddon (Vic.). Two years later
his billet was abolished, and to fill in his time he
offered himself as a candidate for Avoca in Vic-
toria’s first Representative Parliament. He was
third on the poll, Ebenezer Syme and Dr. Owens
(both of the Age) being returned. Mr.
Candler was then appointed coroner for
the metropolitan district of Bourke (in-
cluding Yarra Bend Asylum), which
office he held, with the exception of a
few days at Black Wednesday time,
until the death of Dr. Youl, when he
became city corpse-sitter.
Some time before Bully Bent
became Minister of Vic.’s Great Deficit
Railways, he was travelling in the Wea-
tern district and, by some un-
accountable means, got locked
in the lavatory of a corridor
car, from which the united
efforts of a Melb. press-
man and a western
squatter failed to re-
lease him. It was a
15min. run to the next
station, and all that
time the remarks of
the Hon. Thomas Bent
accumulated in his
prison. Eventually the
guard broke open the
door, and Bent and a
storm of objurgation
poured out together.
Bent now keeps the roll-
ing-stock in a judicious
state of decay which
makes such accidents im-
possible. There is hardly
anything in the Vic.
Railway Department to-
day that wouldn’t double
up instanter if a press-
man and a squatter threw
themselves against it.

H. U. Alcock, of billiard table renown,
recently celebrated the 50th anniversary of his
arrival in Melbourne. An interesting circum-
stance in his career: When acting as commis-
sioner with the late John Zevenboom at the
Paris Exhibition of ’7B, his colleague, while
intently watching the can-can at the Mabille
Gardens, fell backwards into a cactus. Aloock
spent the rest of the night at their hotel picking
the spikes out of Zevenboom’s back.
Be the late Chas. Clark and his Baptist
tabernacle at Albert-street, Melbourne. Some-
body told The Bulletin that Clark’s predecessor,
Rev. Isaac New (superannuated to make room for
the eloquent man .from England), is still in the
land of the living. It seems that old Isaac New,
who was quite played out when Clark took his
pulpit in ’69, lingered on for many years, but has
long ceased to linger. The Isaac New of nowa-
days, if there be one, is presumably a son of the
original Melbourne preacher.

Superintendent Garvin becomes Assistant
to the Inspector-General of N.S.W. Police, after
41 years of police service. Mr. Fosbery was
appointed Assistant, or Deputy, to the Inspector-
General of that day 41 years ago, and Garvin’s
first job, almost, was as police clerk in his office.

which provides food and raiment for the
Laplanders.”
TOMMY: “ The reindeer.”
TEACHER: “And now what is the animal which
provides you with most of your food and
clothes?"
TOMMY! “ Father!"
About a Piebald Australian :
Pa Cole, of Melb. Book Arcade, also his weighty, wor-
shipful spouse and their eldest daughter, sailed the other
Wednesday by the Japanese boat en route to the land of
Britain’s yellow ally. Mr. C.’s little book of special
pleading—“A White Australia Impossible”—seems to
have been taken quite seriously by all the Jap. personages
who have read it. They regard the author as the fore-
runner of public policy, and reckon the Book Arcade the
centre of Australian educational influence. . And Pa Cole
shares these foreign illusions. He worked night and day
for weeks to complete the 2nd part of his prayer for a
variegated Commonwealth. Just before the Arcadian
embassy sailed the booklet was fixed up, and a few
hundred copies for distribution in Japan were, doubtless,
included among the family luggage. The interesting
part of the case is that Cole pleads for the admission of
Asiatics to Australia on the ground that all men are
equal; and the non-understanding Jap. loves Cole for
doing it. Vet if it only penetrated the Jap. mind that
this equality idea means that the Chinaman and the
Hindu are his equals he would be so angry that he would
slay Cole on the spot.
An amazing number of people seem
to have been school teachers in S. Australia
and to have thought better of it. Besides
F. W. Holder, Speaker of the Reps. , there
are—more or less in the front Australian
rank —Gardiner, Treasurer of Westralia;
Propsting, the man who has got the tough
job of running Tasmania without taxing it;
and Batchelor, ex-Labor leader of S. Aus-
tralia, and now in the Reps.
In a solemn sort of way Mr. Spruce
Myth provides a great deal of fun for the
House of Representatives. A Parliamen-
tary party was travelling one day last session
from Melbourne and stopped, as usual, at
Seymour, for refreshments. A burly farmer,
about 6ft. 4in. high and broad in propor-
tion, sat down at the same table as Mr.
Spruce Myth, M.H.R. “ Sausages and
mashed,” said the farmer. “ I shall have
sausages with mashed potatoes,” said Mr.
Myth with the solemnity of an oracle. The
waitress sized up the two appetites, as she
thought correctly, and brought the farmer
three sausages, Mr. Myth two. Mr. Myth,
with a deeply injured air, scanned his neigh-
bor’s plate and then his own and called the
waitress. ‘ ‘ May I ask you to explain this in-
jurious differentiation ? I shall pay the same as
this gentleman here. But whilst he has three
sausages I have been given but two. Now as
Herbert Spencer ” But the waitress had fled;
shortly she returned with a third sausage.

“ Bobrid ” : For some unaccountable
reason, native companions are very plentiful in
the Junee (N.S.W.) district this year. Around
the Erin Yale station-dams I have seen hundreds
of them ; every morning, parties of them fly off to
the cookies’ crops, returning to the dams regularly
every evening. Do not know whether they harm
crops or not, but there’s generally a wild-eyed
cocky with a shot-gun on their trail, which might
lead one to believe that they are not altogether
welcome. It is interesting to watch these birds
“dancing.” They are the personification of
grace when going through what appears to be a
waltz. They bow to one another with such ease
and style that a Darling Point lady would die with
envy if she saw it. I can hardly imagine a more
graceful bird than the companion.
A really vigorous letter from a Western
town:
Wanted : A De la Eey, or De Wet, or something simi-
lar, with a sjambok, to belt energy and sense into
portion of the N.S.W. police-force. Three weeks ago a
low-down skunk, a thing in pants, struck an hotel in the
west. It travelled per sulky and drove tandem. Pub.
was a wayside, and lonely, and kept by a widow who was
grabbing up trouble by the handful, much against her
inclination. Three children in the nearest hospital—
down with fever, and in a serious condition ; and trade
was slack—awfully slack. A friend lent her a sulky, and
’twas the only means she had of getting in to see her
brood in the hospital. Moreover she had five more
chicks of her own at home, and a baby belong-
ing to a married daughter, also sick. The con-
cern in pants drove along, and stayed three days.
Then It went away. Another three days and the widow’s
sulky disappeared. She and her 10-year-old son tracked
the thief some miles on foot, recognising the hoof-marks
of the skunk and the tandem. Tracked to Its camp in
the scrub, where It had fed Its horses while It watched
and waited for the lights at the shanty to go out (It
pulled the sulky by hand to there) and then tracked to
where It had left Its own sulky pro tem. Then they ac-
quainted the police, who started off only eight hours
behind the thief. With a good sprint, two sulkies, tied
one behind the other and driven tandem—a grey and a
black—was easy game. They got within three hours of
their quarry, when their nags knocked up. Then they
found an abandoned sulky—the old one, not the widow’s.
It went on with that. Soon after that they heard news
cf It; which was truly wonderful, seeing how the country
is seamed with telegraph-wires, and they ascertained
that It had got out of their district. Then news came
slowly, and the last was that It had been seen by some
irresponsible person on another track. A week more and
sofneone happened to the township and inquired of the
blaring mass of useless pomposity in a uniform who
bossed the locality “if he had any news, or if the police
were doing anything.” Whereupon the authoritative
heap wobbled with indignation and, spluttering drama-
tically, remarked that it had sent out instructions here,
there, and everywhere, which was a strangely resultless
proceeding, for with telephones and wires in all direc-
tions and a dry, dusty road to track upon, it should have
been a thousand to one against the thief. But the police,
as usual, got tired, and there doesn’t seem to be a Du
Wet or any competent person to sjambok a move into
them ; and now it’s rained and the odds are in favor of
the skunk. Thus the widow loses the sulky that wasn’t
hers and pays £2O for one to replace it. By the way, a
large sociable was stolen from the same township about
three years ago, and the police have not yet recovered it,
let alone discovered the thief. I firmly believe a moun-
tain might be stolen from some of the back-country dis-
tricts and the thief never be traced.
* • * •
The farce of the ordinary trial of the wild
and semi-wild blackfellow. One was charged
lately at Wyndham (Westralia) with murder.
He beamed, and proudly declared he was guilty.
Up there an ordinary magistrate may, with a
jury, take a murder case, or a dozen of them ; and
the magistrate in this case decided to go into the
matter. After that had been done the jury
promptly returned a verdict of not guilty. When
the result was explained to the prisoner it was a
very disgusted black brother indeed who got out
of the dock. He seemed to have an idea that if
he was found guilty he would get a bouquet or
a bottle of rum.

“F.C.” : Many fruit-growing friends
who give me peaches have cautioned me
against drinking milk therewith or immediately
thereafter. Anything in this ? And wherefore ?
Have heard somewhere that an English king or
other ill-regulated personage died of a surfeit of
peaches and new ale, but have never been able to
acquire enough of the latter to threaten any
danger.

to pollution of water courses should be stringently
applied. In this district it is
pitiful to see the way the
river cookies have des-
troyed large
numbers of river
oaks fodder
trees —during
the drought. In-
stead of being
lopped judicious-
ly, the trees have
been felled indis-
criminately, and
the trunks cast
into the river—
thus killing use-
ful trees, and
making the river
bed a tangled
raft of dead tree-
trunks. When
the next big
flood oomes the
bridges and
flood-gates down
stream will suffer
considerably. It
appears to be the
custom of river-
side people to
haul the heavy
logs and butts
from their clear-
ing to the banks
and tumble them
into the river.
“B.”i “Burglar Bill” (B. 25/4/’O3) is
hardly correct in saying that it is hatred of the
farm which makes it so difficult to obtain recruits
for the gaol cabbage-garden. As a matter of fact
the average Australian cocky in freedom despises
vegetable growing. Not one in ten possesses
even an apology for a garden, vegetable or other-
wise. The only semblance of one is when the
missus ” grows a few geraniums or a rose-bush
in a gin-ease or a kerosene-tin ! It isn’t that the
male cocky hasn’t plenty of time at his disposal
to attend to a cabbage-plot, but the work is be-
neath his notice and savors too much of the Chow.
I have attended shows in various parts of the
State and never saw any vegetables exhibited
unless they came from the Govt, model farms.
The cocky isn’t a cabbage enthusiast.

Asserted by Dr. Harris Browne, an old
Adelaide man in England, that the talpose or
pincoo (“ pinkie ”) should be strictly preserved as
an insectivorous animal, fond of locusts and given
to digging up their eggs. Professor Stirling, of
Adelaide, being interviewed on the point, called
the animal pinto or rabbit-bandicoot, but knows
nothing of its alleged egg-digging habits. The
pinkie seems to run about 18 inches long, as a
rule. There is a fine specimen in the Adelaide
Museum, as big as a large buck-rabbit.
In a wayback Maori-
land district of some un-
pronounceable native
name, many sheep were
recently worried to death
by a dog that, for a time,
wisely defied all attempts
to capture it. One cocky,
however, goaded to des-
peration, determined to
have the mongrel’s life-
blood. He disguised him-
self as a sheep, and, per-
fumed with chemicalsand
armed with a Winchester,
took up his abode on
the hillside, where he
passably imitated the
actions of a sheep. For
four long weary days and
nights he waited, till at
last the dog, unable to
resist any longer an
attempt to shake up the
bogus and lonesome
mutton, approached near
enough to receive a charge
of lead. The truth of this
story isn’t guaranteed.
* • *
A marked
survival of simian habits is
the aboriginal Australian’s
command of his toe-action.
He can, even whilst standing
perfectly erect, pick up from
the ground, between the
big and the second toe,
articles as small as a
needle. All young people,
too, display a survival
of ancestral arborealism
in the desire to climb and
to swing by the hands.
Another monkey-like trait
in the aboriginal is the habit
of “planting” or hiding for
future use. The “all-fours” atti-
tude of infants, too, is simian, as is also—to my
thinking—the position most runners assume when
waiting for the pistol-shot to start them.

It does a man good, sometimes, to live a
long way from the Owe’Sullivan. A message
from Wentworth, which is a great way out in the
dust:—
Representatives of the local trading firms leave here on
Monday for Oaddel’s Reef, about 40 miles below Went-
worth, the most dangerous and troublesome spot on the
Lower Murray. It is their intention to take advantage of
the present low state of the river, and clear away all
obstructions, and make a safe channel.
If they lived this side of Dandaloo they wouldn’t
load up with dynamite and go to Caddel’s Reef ;
they would load up with statistics and a large
part of the dictionary, and proceed to Sydney,
and when they had roped in six M’s.P., they
would go to the Works Dept., and the six M’s.P.
would make as many speeches, and all the others
would make speeches, and the Owe-See would
make a speech (or two speeches, if he got the
chance), and, subsequently, they would consume
large quantities of whisky, and otherwise hold up
the end of their township, and the Owe’See would
probably take them for a harbor picnic, and then
they would go back home, where they would later
on “ report progress ” in the local State-subsidised
“School of Arts.” And Caddel’s Reef would go
on remaining just where it was.
• « * *
At Gulgong (N.S. W.) after a shower a few
days back a youngster picked up a 15dwt. gold
nugget in the street. The other papers call him
“a lucky boy,” but this one isn’t sure. The
chances are that this stroke of misfortune will
make a prospector of him, and he will live on
tinned dog and green water all the days of his
manhood, and in his early second childhood he
will be a fossicker and live in a bag hut —with
Chinamen, who never wash themselves, for neigh-
bors. And he won’t wash himself either, and
finally the papers will have a paragraph concern-
ing another old-age pensioner found dead.

A DISCONTENTED BLOKE.
BILL: “ How’ve yer been doin’ since
I last saw yer, ’Entry-been in
quod?”

been doin' too good; don’t yer know I've been

Why, 'Enery, some blokes ain’t satisfied unless
they're hung, and you're one of them."
Mr. Blank, M.P., represents a country
constituency where the train runs through on its
way to the capital at a very early hour a.m. He
was just embarking one morning when a breath-
less elector rushed on the platform
“ How are you, Mr. Blank ? Want to see you particu-
lar ; been looking for you nearly all yistiday.”
Mr. Blank blandly acknowledged the greeting, and the
elector rattled : “ You know my fox terrier—a real good
little dorg he is, mind you ; his mother’s that bitch of
Alf’s what took the prize last Show. You must know
him ; he’s a real good little dorg. He’s got a black spot
on his hack, and a black an’ yellow mark round his left
eye, and his tail’s a long ’un. Nearly all fox terriers got
short tails.”
The train was getting ready to go, and Mr. Blank
hinted that his constituent might come to the point.
“ Well, you see, it’s this way—that dorg’s lost.”
“ I’d advise you to tell the police.”
“What’s the good of telling the police? I did speak
to the sergeant about him, but the police won’t try and
find him.”
“ Well, what can /do?” queried Mr. Blank.
“ You know Mazzini’s circus w r as here not long ago,
and, about the time they went, my dorg went, and I’ve
got a sort of an idea one of the circus coves shook him.
Now, that circus’s travelling north, and—let’s see—this
is Monday, and they ought to show in Brisbane about
next Thursday.”
The legislator was bewildered, and the elector looked
Earned to think that a representative of the people should
e so dense. “ Ain’t you got a free railway pass, and can’t
you travel on the railways for nothing?”
Blank admitted that much.
“ Well, look here, you might oblige a fellow by slipping
up to Brisbane, and having a look round that circus.
That dorg of mine is a real good little fellow, and I don’t
want to lose him.”
Before the paralysed legislator could find breath to say
anything, the train started and saved the situation.

Wangaratta (Vic.) council recently pro-
ceeded against itself by its own officer, instructed
by its own mayor, for allowing the town bull to
wander at large. It was explained that the
council, as managers of the local common, owned
the lugubrious animal, which appeared as an ex-
hibit and created consternation amongst the
other offenders (of the female persuasion) in the
courtyard. The council, however, didn’t carry
its Roman fortitude to the’extent of pressing for
a conviction, but withdrew the case against itself
amid the guffaws of the court.

So far the reported big change in camel-
driving in far west N.9.W. —the substitution of
white for Asiatic drivers —doesn’t amount to
much. Only one firm of storekeepers, on an
intensely democratic mining field, has started to
employ white labor, probably scenting advt.
in the change. It is recognised now to be all a
question of price. The old idea that there was
some special affinity between the camel and the
Afghan has blown out. As a matter of fact,
many of the attendants are Hindus and Sikhs,
who never handled a camel outside Australia—
men raked in from the coastal sugar-growing
district of N.S.W. and from other parts, not
because of any skill or knowledge, but because
they are very cheap and very servile. Indeed in
Westralia, where the still cheaper aboriginal is
available, he is roped in, and is said to call out
“ Hooshta ” just as well as anybody else.
NO HURRY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It appears to be abundantly
And beautifully clear
That there really isn’t any need to hurry !
This opinion we redundantly,
Continually hear
In our politics on either side the Murray.
And it seems to be the fashion with our Parliaments to-
day
To seal up the doors for months and months in quite a
careful way,
While the members go a-fishing, and the angry prawn
they slay.
As for other things—there isn’t any hurry I
Did you hear about economy,
Retrenchment, and the rest?
Don’t be nervous, for they don’t intend to hurry !
Let us talk about astronomy—
Is Wragge or Russell best?
And don’t give way to any needless flurry.
They’re thinking hard, these statesmen, so wise and old
and grey
(I saw eight of them a-thinking in a public-house to-day ;
A waiter fled towards them with refreshment on a tray,
And you should just have seen that waiter hurry).
If our credit’s gone to Jericho,
Of course, that makes it plain,
That there’s really not the slightest need to hurry 1
They’ll meet again.
The merry co.
In Parliament again
To tell us there’s no earthly need to worry.
Never mind about the papers, nor the people, though
they will
Be inclined to ask some questions ; be profoundly silent —
still
Another loan is mooted ; See’s a-drafting of the Bill,
So there’s surely no demand for any hurry 1
It’s highly reprehensible,
This tendency to haste,
And disgraceful when there isn’t any hurry 1
Oh, be silent, and be sensible,
And never mind the waste !
That is only adding cayenne to the curry !
Does it matter to the common folk, to folk like you and
me,
If the revenue is wasted and the country up a tree ?
Please possess your soul in patience; all the Ministers
agree
There’s no need for loss of dignity and skurry.
Kindly lend me your auricular
(That is to say, your ear);
Don’t expect they ’ll face the music in a hurry 1
But they want to be particular;
They want to make it clear.
That they do not want a scramble and a flurry.
Ee generous ; be moderate ; be patient and be calm !
There is not the slightest reason for a feeling of alarm.
There’ll be something smash directly which will do more
good than harm,
And it won’t be re-constructed in a hurry 1
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODAR.

A bit of London
had an exciting ex-
perience the other day
with a wild Australian
kangaroo, which got
loose at Euston Square
railway station and
played ducks and
drakes with traffic re-
gulations and platform
rules for half-an-hour.
Most of the frightened
people thought it was a bear; others reokoned it
to be a tiger, and one lady hazarded the view
that it was the Devil. Finally a bold cabman
gripped it by the neck and restored it to captivity.

“ L.M.D.” writes In a cheerful strain ;
“ J.F.D.” (11/4/’O3) and “ R Q.” (4/2/’O3l both “express
what every bushman with ‘imagination’ feels.” For
“ imagination ” read “ tired feeling," “ specks before the
eyes,” and the hundred and one other symptoms blazed
abroad per acres of advertisement. The ‘ ‘ weird ex-
pectancy ’’ of the bush and the things that inhabit thereof
is m the brain of the observer mostly. That “fear of
something dreadful about to happen,” regarded as a
characteristic of disordered nervous machinery, is bad
enough to contemplate, but when influential writers
strive to prove that not only the bush-imprisoned human
solitary is afflicted therewith, but the whole animal world,
the lizard, kangaroo, wallaby and grub, it is
something prevision of a tired nation with
three specks before its eyes, and visions of the three
golden balls of Lombardy, that awful trinity which em-
bodieth Cohen, waiting in expectancy for that something
dreadful about to happen, is enough to make a man pack
his portmanteau and reach out for the horizon.
Never noticed this expectancy myself, excepting in
rabbits and paddymelons and other things that had been
shot at and dogged so often that their expectancy was
justifiable. I was in the scrub this morning, and the
stringybarks and honeysuckles were teeming with bird
and insect-life. If there was any expectancy there it
was of having a good, solid meal. A rabbit scuttled past
my feet and sat up on his burrow a few yards away. I
raised my hand. He didn’t wait to expect —just made a
dead certainty of it and disappeared. And the pleasant
hum, as of a live nation at work at its own industries,
dispelled the gruesomeness instilled by over-much read-
ing of the loony bush bard and his whisky-conjured
visions.
Please advise any bard writing on “ weird expectancy ”
in future to send “stamped addressed envelope for the
name of a good man."
LIFE IN THE OLD DOG YET.
MR. ISAACS: "Now, Esau, I haf made my will,
and left everything I haf to you.”
ESAU: “ Yes, dad.”
MR. ISAACS : “ And as you get all the benefit I
shall stop the cost of making the vill out of
your next week's salary.”
A correspondent writes :
Through the incapacity, or something worse, of its
well paid officials the Govt, very often falls in badly in its
business transactions with the cute money-grubbing citi-
zen. A glaring instance : In a western (N.S.W.) town-
ship an individual, renowned for his diplomacy in dealing
with State officials, got word that a constable was to be
stationed there. He thereupon hastily fenced in an acre
or two (out of the town), erected thereon a cheap,
shoddy, wooden skillion style of building with a bag-
covered verandah and a flimsy stable, and let the lot to
the Department at a yearly rental of £39 1 This enter-
prising individual then secured an extension of lease of a
large Government farm close by, with about 200 acres
cleared, large grass paddocks, Government tank, pump-
ing machinery, troughs, etc., etc., together with a good
building, at a yearly rental of only £35. The State is
always falling in like this through want of supervision.
MARK HAMBOURG.
MICHAEL HAMBOURG (Father of Mark Hambourg).
Up at wild Wyndham, which is somewhere
between Perth and Darwin, a local black named
Muggeree was lately charged with four lots of
murder. In one case it was alleged he playfully
held up a half-caste child by a leg and knocked its
brains out. . The “Acting-Resident” got as far
as sentencing him to two seven-year terms in
gaol with a flogging, when the jury caved in, and
the other proceedings fell through. The local
medical officer then interposed to prevent the
whipping, on the ground that he strongly doubted
Muggeree’s sanity; but before he could pronounce
definitely the Govt, ordered the whipping to be
abandoned. So it looks now as though this black
brother is too mad to be flogged for a crime, but
not mad enough to escape 14 years’ imprisonment
for it. The idea of finding out his state before
trial doesn’t seem to have occurred to anybody.
• • • •
“Nep.”: Be the patience of fishermen.
Saw a sad-faced man heave a bait towards the
rising sun from Coogee beach the other morning.
After lighting his pipe, he twisted the line
artistically around his fingers and settled himself
comfortably on his haunches to await results.
Passing a couple of hours later, he was still in the
same position. Inquired if he had had any luck.
He replied no, but said he had got an encouraging
bite. By sunset something had taken his hook ;
and shortly after he had landed it. “Yes,
I’m pretty lucky as a rule,” he said, contentedly
unhooking the 21b. whiting, “but some days I’ve
seen me have two or three like this by this time.”
* • * *
Until quite lately no traces of aboriginal
occupation had ever been found on Kangaroo
Island, the generally wild and deso-
late pile which shelters the S. Aus-
tralian gulfs from the ocean. Lately,
however, Walter Howchin, the State’s
most tireless searcher for uncon-
sidered trifles, found numerous things
which look like upsetting the hitherto
accepted theory that the place was
never inhabited by our black brother
Bill.

“L.W.”; Know at least one
snivelling hypocrite who was in town
enjoying himself during show week.
He went out to obtain information
about how to grab a portion of the
free seed grain, and, while engaged
on the task, bought a Erench marble
clock costing about £lO, and a gold
watch and chain as a birthday gift
for his daughter ( and he and his gin
boasted that while in the city no part
of the ox but his two extreme ends
ever passed their lips, and then only
when they got tired of chicken.
* * • •
They tell of a fighting boar in the
Rimutaka district, M.L. :
The boar will bail up cattle and men, and on one
occasion he made a bold attempt to stick up a Fell
engine, which was laboriously ascending the mountain.
He was cast down a gully, two hundred feet in depth,
leaving behind him on the bloodstained cowcatcher a
slab of his hide. Since then the pig has carefully kept
away from the railway line. His chief delicacies are
goats and young lambs. He appears to bear a charmed
life from the gun, and can do a fast mile on any spur in
the Rimutaka.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on ilf S. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

“ H.” : Was yarning with a returned
cohentingenter the other day, who told me that
he believed that most of the comforts sent from
Australia for the different S’African contingents
at various times—clothes, pipes, tobacco, etc. —
never got to the men at all, but were sold to
Capetown storekeepers by certain officers for their
own profit.
MISS MABEL BATCHELOR, SOPRANO.
In a stabbing case heard a week or two
ago in Melbourne, a Chinaman’s wife, living apart
from her husband, stated that the injured person
was her “ boy,” but indignantly denied that he
was her “bloke.” Can anyone accurately define
a bloke, so that he can be recognised at sight and
not confused with a cove ?

The Remarkable Case of Lieut. C. E. Bulmer. Victoria Barracks.
Messrs. Fisher and Co., 337 George-street. _ Victoria Barracks, Sydney, March 30/’O3.
Sirs, Your Phospherine is truly a wonderful and health-giving medicine. Its therapeutic virtues should be widely known. About 6 months ago,
owing to general troubles and constant worry, my health and nervous system gave way. I became very shaky, lost my appetite—in fact, I was in a very run-
down and critical state. For some time I could hardly walk. The doctor by whom I was examined pronounced my case hopeless, that nothing could be done
for me, and said there was no cure for me, and that it was only a matter of time.
It was then that I heard of your Phospherine. After taking two shilling bottles of it I found a great change. It removed the nervousness and
shakiness. My appetite returned. I have taken it ever since. I isher s Phospherine is my doctor for the future. lam now restored to health and strength,
and feel very well. I cannot speak too highly of your Phospherine. I shall be pleased to recommend it to anyone suffering from nervousness, languor, or loss of
appetite. I consider it the greatest blood builder of the age. For the good of others you may publish my case, or refer sufferers to me for further explanation.
Yours truly, G. E. BULMER.

POLITICAL
POINTS
From the Observer’s report of a case
heard in Brisbane:—
He said my father had called him a fraud, a scoundrel
and an importer.
If the Australian defence forces were
called out to meet an enemy to-morrow, it is
declared that 10 per cent, of the men would be
bootless and 20 per cent, without uniforms.
Minister Kingston met magnificent recep-
tions in Mackay and Cairns —particularly at the
latter town. Apparently the more yellow the
town the more they love a white man—when they
can find one.
It is alleged that Maoriland has granted
permission to a charitable organisation in India
to export Eurasians (half-bred Asiatics) to its
territory. Whither is Premier Seddon’s Imperial-
ism leading him?
Alleged Senator Reid says he will not
resign his alleged seat before Parliament meets.
And when Parliament meets and he tries to
sit on that alleged seat he will probably fall
through, on to the floor. That generally happens
to the man who sits on an alleged seat.
Writes a man from Adelaide :
Bounder Bent’s management of Victoria’s railways has
beeD marked in Adelaide b3' the continual late arrival of
the so-called Melbourne express, and now not the slightest
reliance can be placed upon the time table. When the
train comes in up to time the very greatest surprise is
caused among passengers and officials. I forget when it
last arrived up to time.
At Premier Barton’s Town Hall meeting,
according to the daily paper reports, Dr. Nash,
M. L.C. j moved a vote of thanks and confidence,
and Mayor Hughes put it as a vote of thanks
only. The incident calls for some explanation.
Tlte meeting would have carried a vote of confi-
dence ; and Mr. Hughes is on the other side in
politics.
THE FREE(TRADE) METHODIST AND MORALIST.
“Under ordinary circumstances such a verdict would drive its
object from public life, but probably nine persons out of every ten
in Australia hold, at least, that Mr. Reid’s personal character
emerges untouched from the case.”— PARSON FITCHETT in REVIEW
OF REVIEWS.
THE FREE(TRADE) METHODIST: “ Thank the Lord, anyone
with half a Freetrade eye can see that our beloved brother
in the faith has emerged from the ordeal with his halo
untarnished and his robes all unspotted. Hallelujah! ”
In Victoria, State school teachers, after
20 years’ continuous service, are entitled to six
months’ leave on full pay. It is commonly re-
ported that the “Reform” Ministry proposes to
get over this by sacking a multitude of teachers
and then re-engaging them—thus breaking the
continuity of their service. According to all
reports, in fact, the “ Reform ” Ministry is think-
ing of sacking pretty nearly everybody.
Victoria has been looking for that magni-
ficent asset in the shape of the State’s unsold
land which is said to be such indubitable security
for the public creditor. And after raking over
the 17,000,000 acres of leased land and the
15,000,000 acres neither sold nor leased, Minister
Taverner says he can only find 200,000 acres of
State land that looks at all fit for agriculture.
Private grabbers have collared all the good land
long ago. The State has a big area on paper—
mostly mountain tops.
Two consecutive items in a recent issue of
Sydney Telegraph :
Between the Hunter and Tweed Rivers, says Mr. O’Sul-
livan, the country is fertile, and there is room for a popu-
lation of three or four millions.
Stone-breaking depots are to be opened by the Govern-
ment, and the unemployed will be given an order entitling
them to work according to their circumstances.
Bent’s latest “economy” on Vic. railways
is to sack the regular porters at Melbourne
stations, and license a certain number of indivi-
duals, who are to be remunerated by tips from the
public. These persons are to pay 10s. a year
lioense fee, so Thomas’s new idea will bring in a
little revenue as well as save wages.
It is commonly but wrongly supposed that
the “investiture” held the other day was the
Becond fuss-and-feathers of its kind in Australia,
the small York’s being the first. As a matter of
fact there was a similar public joke 41 years ago
when Governor Barkly put a ham-frill on his
father-in-law, Major-General Pratt.
In the pursuit of Seddon’s proposed nigger
empire in the Pacific, 25 members of Maoriland
House of Reps, and 11 M’sL.C. have sailed on a
gaudy Polynesian jamboree. The gentleman who
was doing three months in an inebriates’ retreat
was let out before his time, so that he might
accompany the expedition. It carried 106 cases
of champagne and many other refreshments, but
the press, which might give away its drinkings
and its philanderings with the brown girl, was
rigorously excluded.
According to a Vic. country paper, that
loud economist, Vic. Railways Minister Tommy
Bent, cost the country a little money at Camper-
down, the other day. Being too excited to wait
an hour for the express, he stopped train,
annexed the engine to run a special for himself,
and fled howling into the darkness. Consequently,
it is alleged, the next goods train had to drag a
double load, the engine was badly damaged
through overstrain, and the employees had to
work four hours extra.
The Gippsland mine-owners and the Bent
Government combined to bring their train-load of
blacklegs from Bendigo through Melbourne at the
very time of the Eight Hours procession, and this
coarse and silly insult did more to widen the
breach than anything that had happened pre-
viously. The train load consisted of 57 weedy
youths and ancient battlers—the scum of Bendigo
and Melbourne —so they won’t make much real
difference in the case, but the insult remains.
Meanwhile, there is all the more need for
Kingston’s Arbitration Bill to hasten. The
miners can easily bring their dispute within its
range, and there is no Commonwealth House of
Fat to obstruct its progress.
Maoriland is at present suffer-
ing from a healthy plague of stowa-
ways. Every incoming boat has its
quota of undesirable immigrants. As
the Restriction Act doesn’t apply to
this class of arrivals, Bun Tuck speaks
of devising a law like that in force in
the United States, to compel the ship-
ping co.’s to return their stowaways
to the port of departure. How will the
familiar howl about “ refusal to allow
free British subjeots to land at a
British port ” come in here ?
There’s a lot of guile behind the
Sydney Telegraph’s yell that Minis-
ter Kingston should examine the
merits of every breach of the Customs
Act, and hale before the oourts only
those importers whom he is convinced
are trying to defraud the revenue. If
Kingston were to act upon the sugges-
tion the Telegraph would rise up in
denunciation of “ these Star-chamber
methods.” Kingston would be accused
of shielding his political friends and
prosecuting his enemies. Further, it
would, no doubt, be pointed out that
such a method would prejudice a
man’s fair trial, as it would be under-
stood that the defendant had been
found guilty at a previous depart-
mental private inquiry, and was
merely brought before the court for
the purpose of punishment.
Fossildom in Great Britain
stares aghast at the reports of recent
Parliamentary debates. Honorable
members are beginning to talk plain
English in the House of Commons.
Lord Hugh Cecil has candidly ad-
mitted to Mr. Speaker—all Parlia-
mentary statements are made to Mr.
Speaker—that the headquarters staff
of the British army is “ tainted with
leprosy.” When Mr. Bromley-Some-
body was complaining that social in-
fluence had been brought to bear
against Colonel Thingummy, the Minis-
ter for War turned upon Bromley-
Somebody and accused him of having
brought a weight of social influence to
bear in the spotted Colonel's favor. It
is refreshing to find these polite pillars
of politics losing their tempers and
telling the truth.
Sydney D.T. couldn’t help letting peep
its sympathy for the importing swindlers and
forgers who have been brought to book when dis-
cussing the Madden customs decision.
But on one point there was a fair amount of unanimity
(among the mercantile community) and that was that, if
the Customs Department had to refund all the fines so
far inflicted, it would certainly secure very little sym-
pathy and commiseration.
A correspondent of the Sydney daily
papers states that quarterly payments for the
State’s boarded-out children were lately allowed
to run a month overdue, and that he knew of
a house in which the bailiff was then sitting
waiting till the State children’s (there were three
at this ‘particular place) money came along. If
this story is true the system that permits
children to be placed in such poverty-stricken
homes is a hopelessly bad one.
HE THOUGHT IT WAS JUST A LITTLE
PASSING SHOWER.
S.M. Herald, discussing that horrible
minimum-wage for manual workers:
Only the capable wage-earner can get employment at
the minimum wage-rate. The others are thrown out of
work.
The solicitor has his minimum for certain _ classes
of work, the medical doctor has his minimum
regulated by his profession, and numbers of others
have a wage minimum. Whom do these trade
rules throw out of work ? The man with a repute
above the common simply raises his price.
“L.W.” carefully blows the froth off a
pot of cool, grateful fluid and reports :
Careful observation during a recent trip to Maoriland
convinces me that the days of the prohibition fanaticism
are just about numbered. The recent success of the cold
tea party was not due to the increase in the numbers of
the cold tea drinkers, but to the resentment of the decent
moderate drinkers against the way the bloated person
with a brogue conducted his beer-pulling. The present
class of publican has got to go, there’s no question about
that, but prohibition only seems to create an even worse
class, and the next proposal which will certainly loom
large in Maoriland will be the State liquor monopoly.
The A.W.U. seems to be nearing the end
of its legal troubles. The Keogh case in Equity
against the union is to be abandoned, and the
action for cancelling the registration of the so-
called Machine-Shearers’ Union comes before the
Arbitration Court again shortly. On this occasion,
the A.W.U. having removed from its rules all
objectionable regulations, the application should
certainly succeed, and the long-maintained effort
of the Pastoralists’ Union to avoid a straight issue
between squatters and employees be finally frus-
trated.
Hunk from a letter in the S.M. Herald
by that futile old person, George Pile —the one-
time owner of George Pile’s anti-Federal dog :
Our towns and this great city are full of people who,
through this cruel minimum-wage regulation enforced by
the law, which, in the words of Bumble, is “an ass,” are
deprived of the means to earn their daily bread and the
bread for those dependent upon them. The question now
is, What are these unfortunate people to do?
It takes 500,000 people to fill Sydney ; and Sydney,
to George Pile’s knowledge, is full of people who
have lost their jobs through the Arbitration law.
Yet no one, to look at G. Pile, would suspect that
one small man of his mildly beaming appearance
could hold so much knowledge.
That remarkable man, A. W. Pearse, has
been writing indignantly to the Sydney papers
about the alleged suppression of private enterprise
by a nefarious policy of State Socialism. It seems
to The Bulletin that the great trouble of N.S.W.
has been that the crowd of capitalists for whom
Pearse speaks, never had twopenn’orth of enter-
prise to suppress. Where are the ship-building
yards, iron works, brass works, steel works, wool-
len mills, cotton mills, silk mills, linen factories,
glass and pottery works, distilleries and the other
great industries which these wonderful persons
created by their wonderful private enterprise, and
which State Socialism destroyed, or threatens to
destroy ? This perpetual complaint by an impu-
dent and useless gang of land-jobbers and miscel-
laneous parasites, that the great industries they
never had the enterprise to start are in danger of
being wiped out by State Socialism, is sickening
and bumptious cant.
Queensland’s Philp Gov-
ernment is industriously mak-
ing a dead letter of the only
little bit of democratic
Kanaka - legislation it ever
had. The State law enacts
that Sammy Lolo and Tommy
Tanna must not be employed
in other work than tropical
agriculture, and the late James
Dickson, and several less hair-
denuded people, were once
grievously insulted and fined
10s. a head for employing
Kanakas on their domestic
premises. But the Act under
which those painful steps were
taken then retired into strict
seclusion, and hasn’t ventured
abroad since; so, now, the
thigh-bone push from Guadal-
canal and the ear-carved
brotherhood from the cocoa-
nut paradise in general, engage
in whatever occupations they
please.
It’s a fine thing (particu-
larly in Queensland) to be a
constituency represented by a
Minister. Roma, a fourth-rate country town, got
a Court House over two years ago at a cost of
over .£6OOO to the country. (Member, A. Rut-
ledge.) Stanthorpe, a declining country town of
only 650 inhabitants, possesses a post office that
took -£7OOO of Government cash to erect. Stan-
thorpe is also represented by a Minister.
Sydney Telegraph sticks vigorously to its
lie that it was the Civil Service vote in Victoria
which brought about the downfall of the Patterson
Government in ’94, and quotes this as a proof of
the necessity for treading on the service with the
big flat foot of Bent. As a matter of well-known
fact it was Treasurer Carter’s silly semi-Freetrade
budget that knocked Patterson endways. The
yell of astonished execration that followed that
amazing budget rang from one end of Victoria to
the other. Members fled from Patterson as an
infected being, and Turner carried a vote of no-
confidence against him. Then the election took
place, but Patterson was a lost Premier before the
civil servants had a chance to say anything about
him at the polls.
THE PEACEMAKER AND HIS BANNER.
It has been suggested that " the leading Melbourne
clerics" be called in to mediate and settle the
great railway crisis.
One of Vic. Railway Minister Bent’s early
“improvements” was his much-paraded adoption
of the “ suggestion-box ” system, under which the
men are invited, in return for a shadowy reward,
to give the Department the benefit of practical
ideas on how to improve the management and
working of the lines. Nobody is known to have
got any reward, but one railway man tells his
personal experience with much disgustedness. He
plainly demonstrated, he affirms, that a certain
appliance used, with the object of ensuring the
safe working of trains, was not, as fondly believed
by the official heads, automatically infallible, and
lost no time in informing his superiors, under the
suggestion-box scheme, of his discovery. He
claims to be the only man whose suggestion
brought an almost instantaneous reply ; the letter
telling him he was fined £5 reached him with a
speed of which he didn’t think the Department
capable.
THE PRINCESS IN THE TOWER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She had a golden dower,
A noble heritage.;
They kept her in a tower,
They cooped her in a cage.
The wicked uncles gaily
Spent all her income fair—
Some thirty thousand daily—
And never turned a hair.
One uncle was O’See, and
He was a stupid man,
And always gave a free hand
To uncle Sullivan
“ O maiden, maiden dearest,
Why do you look so sad ?
Gaze down on the sincerest
Of friends you ever had.
“ We’ve travelled long : the thud of
Our hoofs the watchmen knew,
And when we’ve scraped the mud off
Our clothes, we’ll rescue you.”
The maiden looked down shyly
Upon that squalid band,
And then she chuckled slyly,
And coughed behind her hand.
For with his little brothers,
The fatuous and forlorn,
She saw the knight Carruthers
Blowing his little horn.

A MARTYR TO INDIGESTION AND
BILIOUSNESS CURED BY
VITADATIO
144 Drummond-street, Ballarat,
18th August, 1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Agent-General for Vitadatio.
Dear Sir, —I have much pleasure in sending this testi-
mony of the great benefit I have received from using
WEBBER’S VITADATIO. I was a sufferer for nine years
with indigestion and biliousness, also dry retching ; in
fact, so ill that I was given up by the doctors. For three
years I was hardly out of bed. I became so weak that I
could not attend to my household. I have been a
MARTYR to PAINS IN MY HEAD, and words fail to
express how intense my sufferings were. I have tried
doctors and patent medicines, but found no relief.
Finally I was persuaded to try WEBBER’S VITADATIO.
The first bottle made me so ill that I was doubtful if the
medicine would do me any good. However, I decided to
give VITADATIO a fair trial, so, I purchased a second
bottle. I felt hopeful, for I began to feel stronger every
day. I took four bottles altogether, and can truthfully
say I FEEL LIKE A NEW WOMAN, and was never better
in my life than at the present time. I can now attend to
my household duties without feeling them a burden. E
AM THANKFUL TO GOD and GRATEFUL TO VITA-
DATIO for the renewed health and strength which I
enjoy. I CANNOT SPEAK TOO HIGHLY in its PRAISE,,
and will only be too-happy to give information to ahyone
who may be suffering, if they will kindly call at the above
address.
Yours faithfully,
JESSIE COLEMAN.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Correspondence Invited. Write for Testimonials.
The price of medicine is 5/6 and 3/6 per bottle.

Members of the Outtrim (Vic.) brass band*
mostly composed of working miners, being
desirous of leading the Coalminers’ Association in
the eight-hour procession in Melbourne, but,
owing to the local prolonged strike, having no
funds with which to cover railway fares, walked
the whole distance between the two places, a
matter of over 75 miles, to attain their object.
This would have been a remarkable feat enough
in ordinary weather, but for the whole of that two
days march it was particularly wet, and anyone
who has sampled Gippsland roads in rainy times
will not begrudge the coaly ones their honest pride
at the exploit. As a counter picture imagine a
body of Calico-Jimmies walking even ten miles to
attend a banquet of sympathy to alleged Senator
Reid.

carriage paid) and Self - Measurement Forms.
Then compare the values. You will think
differently.

Dear Bulletin.—The obituary notice of
Charles Bright has one or two inaccuracies.
Bright arrived in Melbourne 48 years ago, and
joined the staff of the local Morning Herald in
1855, some years before he became connected with
the Argus. It may be true that when he was
invited to manage the advertising for the Mutual
of New York he described himself as “one of the
worst business men in Melbourne,” but, all the
same, Charles Bright belonged to the Chosen
businesspeople. He was a Jew. —K.K.
The death of fine old Charles Bright leads
to talk about the Rev. James Pillars, B. A., whose
widow Bright married. The body of Pillars was
never recovered from the waters at Watson’s Bay,
but in Rookwood cemetery is a monument to him,
with a tablet designed by sculptor Lorando Jones.
Years before, Lorando was tried for Freethought
lecturing in Parramatta Park and sentenced to
hard labor in Ninny Melville, afterwards
Chairman of Committees in N.S.W. Assembly,
challenged the sculptor to repeat his heterodoxies
about King David, and was present in the park
with Police-inspector Bremner and other wit-
nesses to take the words down. Lorando gave
David what-for in the hottest language, and
Ninny had him duly gaoled. Then the Rev.
Pillars stumped Sydney. He wrote to the papers,
called public meetings, headed deputations, and
otherwise excited the mind of the public to such
an extent that the Government quashed the con-
viction and set Lorando free. It is Lorando’s
tablet that is now on the Rev. Pillars’ monument.
The motto around it is “More Light,” a phrase
used on the title-page of a little religious journal
published by Pillars.
Severe Trials Weathered Through.
Sailors at sea have many a severe trial to
weather through. But they stick to their ship,
laugh at the storms and manfully pull her
through. Then the weary and trying times of
the past are looked back upon light-heartedly.
Many a man’s existence can be likened to a ship
struggling against adversity upon the ocean of
life. Trials come, but he who manfully puts
his shoulder to the wheel and determines to push
through them, succeeds where others fail. Mr.
William Huston, of Ashburton, New Zealand,
was well nigh shipwrecked upon the ocean of life.
Before he became utterly wrecked, however,
he miraculously saved himself. “For some
years,” he says, “I had a sluggish liver, which
ultimately ended in a long and painful illness.
On the top of this I had influenza, which further
deranged my liver and brought on a complica-
tion of the kidneys. I underwent treatment by
two doctors, but their medicine and advice were
alike useless. Worn to but a mere shadow of my
former self, I was day and night the victim ot
the many ailments that ceaselessly troubled me.
In this state I should have remained had it not
been for Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, which
did for me what doctors could not effect, l
past is but a mere dark shadow through which
I have passed, the present is full of happiness.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are the best and
mildest medicine I have ever taken, and tnroug
their agency I have been permanently an co
pletely cured.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Roo
are a perfect blood purifier, and a positive
for biliousness, indigestion, constipation, n _
aches, sallow complexion, liver and ki j
troubles, piles, pimples and blotches and
female ailments. Put up in amber bottles ana
the full name blown thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Since the day that Silvermine fell at Randwick,
and thereby caused the death of jockey Alec. Robertson,
the racing community has been considering that “ some-
thing ought to be done ” to put down foul riding and
riding that is made dangerous by ignorance. The child
jockey who has not strength to control the brute he is
perched on should be ruthlessly abolished. Apropos
which, crack English jockey Grimshaw was very short-
sighted, and, on one occasion, he finished an easy second
without making the slightest effort to pass the horse that
was straining every nerve to keep just in front, because
the kid on its back was so small Grimshaw couldn’t
discern him, and thought the horse had thrown his
rider, as Simile did recently at Randwick.
• * * *
Eighteen-year-old jockey W. Aspden died in
hospital last week from fractured spine, brought about
through a fall with nag Gimmaron at Randwick, April
15. Coroner’s jury verdict was accidental death. Un-
usually large crop of falls at A.J.C. autumn gathering
should be inquired into. A.J.O. secretary Clibborn
asserts that the track was much cut up at one part
through horses crowding on the fence near the home
turn and it is told that there wasn’t a hoof-mark outside
10ft.' from the inner rail. Giving mounts to. inex-
perienced boys and the craze for riding Sloan fashion are
other explanations advanced by Mr. Olibborm
F. A. Tennant, ruled out of S. Australian racing
for 10 years, is a young owner, and reputed to be pretty
heavily gilded. Two alleged Maoriland nags were entered
for the Adelaide Autumn meetings—Evening Wonder and
Maori Chief. Maori Chief didn’t turn up, but the Wonder
did • and after it had been nearly a month on the training
tracks, with betting proceeding all the while, it was
announced bv Tennant that it wasn’t the Wonder at all
that had com**but the Chief. Then tb« kicking began.
* * • *
“Le Chasfee” puts forward a sordid view of
things: Why parents persist in rushing their kids into
the Civil Service, and spending hundreds of pounds to
qualify them to earn 30s. per week in the Signal Failure
Department when there are plenty of good training
stables to turn the same boys into capable jockeys, is a
mystery to me. Australia is not the only country where
jockeys ride horses, but it is about the only land where a
man trained under the Owe’See Govt, has any chance of a
billet. In ’Murka, jockey-boy Arthur Redfern expects to
earn £lO,OOO before the year is over, and Danny Maher
will follow him with about £BOOO. W. C. Whitney paid
001. Pepper £3500 for young Redfern’s release and made
a contract with that fortunate kid for a yearly salary of
£3OOO (in dollars, of course) and Frank Hitchcock pays
him £2500 for second call. Danny Maher will ride in
England and his retaining ’fee is £5000.1 In addition to
these small but regular screws both cubs will receive
“outside fees’’which will swell their takings (one cant
Call them earnings, by any stretch of imagination) to
about ten or twelve thousand almightiest pounds for the
vear What’s the use of being a Premier, or a Federal
Treasurer at a paltry £lOOO per year for not more
than three or six years, when in three years as a
jockey one can make a snug fortune to keep one s self in
comfort during middle age and decay ?
* * * *
London Sportsman recently appointed an inter-
national committee, members of which were located in
England, Ireland, France, Russia, Germany, Italy, Aus-
tralia U.S.A., Maoriland, South Africa, Austria, Den-
mark’Spain, Belgium, Roumania, and British Columbia
to determine which of all the stallions would most
suitably mate with the speedy Sceptre. Isinglass was
duly appointed husband, having 54 votes recorded in his
favor Merman was second with 36, and old Jack.
Carbine a good third with 31. Up to the present there is
no word of what Sceptre thinks of the matter, and
whether she will accept the committee’s decision as final
remains to be seen. Isinglass was certainly a fine per-
former on the turf, but that does not always mean
success at the stud ; and some horses who could never
win even a donkey gallop have been highly successful at
siring winners of great races .and many shekels.
The Fritz-Ribbon wood match produced a fine
interchange of skite between Maoriland and visiting Aus-
tralian sports, which resulted in a straight-out offer to pit
Achilles and Cruciform against the best on the mainland.
Wakeful, Abundance or Patronage, w.f.a., over a mile and
a half Suggested that each owner should put up s
sweepstake of 500sovs. on the forfeit system, and run the
race at Riccarton at N.Z. Cup time (Nov.), the Canter-
bury Jockey Club hanging up a purse of a similar amount.
The winner if a Maorilander, is to allow visitors a sum to
be agreed upon for exes. Of course, competitors could
race in the rich N. Z. Cup on first day, run their match on
the second, compete for the Canterbury Cup (w.f.a.) and
Jubilee Cup (also w.f.a.) third and fourth days. It
remains for one of the Australians to take up the
challenge.
* • • m
D. J. Price, the owner of champion pacer
Ribb’onw’ood, was cheered vociferously every time he
beat Fritz, but on the third day of the metropolitan
meeting things suddenly changed. Price had steered
two winners home, and then, as the club declined to give
a purse he gave an exhibition mile “for love, and
broke his own Australian record of 2.10, made on the
first day, reducing it by one second easily. Another
ovation; and Price went out to drive the second favorite
in a handicap event. But he shaped so suspiciously that
the stewards promptly passed him out with the horse
for a six months spell. Curiously enough the moke s
owner is a Mr. Felony. Then the crowd made another
noise, and it had such an ugly sound that the fallen
favorite is understood to have gone home the back way.

whole of the big trotting string which A. J. Buckland
