* * • *
“ Ne’er-do-weel ” : It was at a big meeting at
an inland town, and the biggest bookmaker confided to
me that he was disappointed with the business doing,
and intended soon to “ shake ’em up ” a bit. It wasn t
till the second last race, when everybody was pretty
drunk, that the shake-up commenced. Then he shook
things right enough. Listening to the yells of the
bookies, I was struck by an occasional offer of longer
odds than the threes and fours usual at these meetings.
This came from my burly friend—“ Twenty to one Black-
bird ! Twenty to one Blackbird! 11l lay Blackbird.
I saw one or two men, mostly tight, book wagers with
him, and considering the horses and the class of horse-
men, thought it was worth while to put a quid on any-
thing with four legs at these odds. I was just about to
make the bet when I looked at my card. Then I spoke :
“Here! what price Blackbird?" “Lay you twenty-
five! Blackbird!-I’ll lay” “Why, dash your
eves ” I said, “ there’s no such horse on the card. What-
chergivin’us?” “ Don’t give a hang ! Laid him a dozen
times already. Thirty to one Blackbird. I’ll lay Black-
bird !"
• • * •
The imported St. Ambrose, a stable companion
of The Victory, looks a very likely winner of the Good-
wood Handicap at Adelaide. On his dandy appearance
St Ambrose would be picked to sprint and stay, and on
his form in last Champion Stakes, when he chased Great
Scot for a mile and a half, the Britisher should prove
dangerous when wanted.
S T.C. meet. Saturday, for an off-day, was well
patronised—muster totalled 6000; but small fields and
unaccountable reversals of form marred the programme
much, and the stipendiary stewards remained same old
wooden images. In face of anything but prominent
running of a week before, Oarwee and hai Viti (well
backed by someone in each instance) appropriated S.T.O.
and Farewell Handicaps easily, giving nothing else a
hope. Yet no explanation was sought. Other things
were “ strong 99 enough to knock one down, and still those
responsible remained inert.

“Point ” : Anent Constable M‘Quaker’s 11751bs
punch, and Hickey’s and Aulsebrook’s improvement
thereon. Writer knows a little man who can always
send the indicator twice round the dial, which is anything
that might be named more than 11751b5, for its full
extent isn’t ascertainable. Know numbers of bruisers
who couldn’t thump within coo-ee of 11751bs on the
machine, but, would sooner be punched twice by
M‘Quaker and Co. than once by any man of them.
People practised at the game bring the weight of the
body to bear, and that’s the trick of it.
* * • ».
A couple of cunning visitors at Tamworth the
other day conspired to take down the local talent at a
shooting gallery. While one competed with the rifle the
other hid behind the target and rang the bell when his
mate fired. The dodge worked all right until an unfor-
tunate wide shot put a bullet through the bellringer s
hand. Then the game was up.
* • • *
Thanks to a further subsidy of -£5OO from the
N.S.W. Government for improvements, the new sports’
ground at Moore Park, Sydney, will have a first-class
cycle track laid down—something which Australia
hasn’t got at present. Speaking of cycle-tracks, Major
Taylor denounced very bitterly the Melbourne track, and
besides declaring it the worst in Australia, hoped that no
races on such a track would be arranged for him on a
future [visit. And the Melbourne authorities simply
smiled.

Mr. O. H. Kerry, hon. sec. N.S.W. Gun Club,
writes: A par in your sporting columns, 25/3/’O3 inst.,
refers to quail shooting at the N.S.W. Gun Club grounds
some time before the season opened. Writer is evidently
unaware of the fact that during the N.S.W. close season
large shipments of live quail reached Sydney by the
China mailboats. Those referred to in your par. formed
part of such a shipment landed just at that time.
[This explanation makes things look more legal and
quite as brutal. As the Scripture might put it, “ I was
a stranger, and ye blew me to pieces with a gun.’’—
Ed. B.]

• * • *
“ Nym.” : T’other Sunday witnessed a splendid
coursing match —the first of the season. Was driving
along the road to Toolleen when I noticed two
eagles pounce down on some object in the middle of a
paddock. Then the fun commenced. First a dart this
way, then a dart that way, the immense birds often col-
liding against one another in their haste. It was some
moments before I could make out what was up, then they
got closer and I saw the trouble. The birds had surprised
a buck hare asleep, and he was racing and dodging for
his life. The dash of the birds was becoming more
malicious every time, and I thought it must end in the
death of the hare, but the plucky long-eared animal
twisted and turned through a good ten minutes go till
within a few yards of a log fence, when he streaked
like greased lightning to shelter the first time I
ever knew, a hare to take to dead timber. Then the
eagles, thoroughly “ done,” perched on the fence to wait
till he would come out, but when my whip cracked they
fluttered wearily away\
The degeneracy of the Australian man of thew.
Thursday night, last week, turbanned heathens Buttan
Singh and Gunga Brahm’rastled for Australian supremacy
before 2000 people at Wirth’s Circus, Melbourne. Falls
occupied 20, 6£, and 4min. Only one (second) went to
Singh, who has lorded it over all Australian catch-as-
catch-can comers for past few years, because Australia
hasn’t produced a ’rastler—European or otherwise—of
better than mediocre capacity since 15 or 20 years ago.
When ’rastling boomed, and world’s champions Cannon,
Whistler and co. found it profitable to come here, but
crooked work —always in evidence with athletics under
similar conditions —completely choked public zest for
the game. Convincing proof of the decadence of ’rastling
in Australia were the Dunn-Lucifer, Hajek-Lucifei, and
other matches in Sydney, a year or two since.
• * • •
The “colored” persons gradually rake in the
championships of Australia. Major Taylor hung a string
of them to his bike-wheel, and now an Afghan secures
the wrestling championship. The casual and tired Aus-
tralian evidently thinks that wrestling isn’t within his
ability ; and it’s hardly a policy for athletic bodies to re-
cognise championships which can only be won by aliens
to their country.
* • • •
Recently E. L. Armytage rowed on the Tamar
(Tas.) from Launceston to Georgetown and back (74 miles)
in little over 13 hours. Armytage (as breathers for the
big trip) covered 46 miles five weeks before and 23 miles
three times with intervals of a week between each row.
He is same man who, two years ago, rowed from Geelong
to Melbourne.
• * * *
Defeat of Sydney champion amateur sculler,
Joe Daley, in contest for Australian supremacy on the
Yarra, Saturday, was biggest set-back Cornstalk rowers
have known for years. Though doubtful about the Oar
event they looked upon the Sculls as a “ moral.” How
little expert judgment is to be relied upon was never
better exemplified. Authorities generally voted Daley
sure. While Victorians, Ivens and Brasch, were looked
to to make a nose and nose finish for second, Tasmanian
Bourke wasn’t considered “in it,” beyond being among
the starters, yet he won, and easily—26 seconds ahead
of Ivens, who beat Daley two lengths. Course was
2j measured miles. This was Bourke’s first success, but
Daley has won three times—last two years in succession.
’Twas known before Daley left Sydney that he wasn’t at
his best, but he had proved so much ahead of the general
run that it still looked odds on him scoring.
• • • •
Result of latest inter-State 8-oar race should go
far towards forcing N.S.W. R.A. to make room for the
manual labor man, hitherto religiously shut out because
of the fear that, through living by the use of his arms, he
would have a considerable advantage over the clerk, the
counter-jumper, and such. Every other branch of
athletics admits the manual labor man open-armed—
swimming particularly. Until N.S.W. rowing autocrats
make up their minds to follow suit with Victoria in this
respect, they should refrain from sending crews to
compete in inter-State 8-oar races, as they do so
at a disadvantage, which renders the result a fore-
gone conclusion. Evidence of what the manual labor
man can do at rowing was ample in the time of the
“Footscray mud-punters,” who’d leave off their work,
get into their “ eight,” s„o.v their pipes alongside of them
for the time being, and beat any crew that came along.
“ Thanks particularly to the ‘blawsted kaw-
linies ’ ” writes Thb Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ Scotland well defeated England in the ‘ International ’
Rugby by 10 to 6. Having beaten Ireland and Wales,
the triple crown was the victors’ for this season. The
Scottish (?) three-quarter-back line was made up of two
Australians (A. B. Timms and A. N. Fell), one Maori -
lander (J. S. MacDonald), and one S’African, while another
‘ kerlonyel ’ played forward. Thus a third of the team
were Greater Britons, and, it is admitted, did more than
their share in winning the game. Timms, 30yds. from
the posts, snapped up the ball outside the scrum, and
< repeated his performance in the Welsh game by dropping
a magnificent goal, which fairly aroused the enthusiasm
of all the Scots on the ground ’ (Edinburgh Dispatch). A
writer to the paper above quoted says : ‘lt is a sorry
plight when Scotland takes the field with one-third of
her side composed of rank outsiders.’ ‘Rank’ seems
particularly ungrateful."
* * • *
London Truth has a long article on batsmen
who play leg-breaks with their pads, and argues very
fairly that pads are only intended as a protection, and a
batsman who intentionally plays the ball with them
ought to be given out on the spot. But the article very
•unfairly quotes as its only definite instance the Austra-
lians in their big score at Brighton. It is quite possible
that one or two of them picked up the trick when in
England, but it is distinctly an English trick, and hardly
known in Australia —as a regular method of defence.
* * * *
Jack Reedman, the hardened veteran who still
plays cricket and football for S.A., is to have a benefit
from footballers shortly. Reedman and Giffen are the
two “ aged ” athletes who never knock under. Reedman
has now been playing football for twenty years. He is a
letter carrier, and avers that the 15 miles travelled daily
in doing his work keeps him in good fettle for sport on
Saturday*.
* • • •
It seems “fashionable” for every successful
athlete to blossom as a compiler of reminiscences, and
cricketers take the disease very badly. Giffen, Grace,
Fry, Ranjitsinghji, and hosts of others have been
afflicted with the desire to become famous by resurrecting
mouldy old stories and averages, and jamming into the
heads of aspirants how to play the game by theory.
“ Billy ” Murdoch is the latest to fall into the error, and
he will reminisce with much gloom and weight.
• • * *
The point-blank refusal to send a rifle team to
Bisley from Wellington (M.L.) contrasts very vividly with
the scraping and bowing which is being indulged in by
the different Australian States to raise sufficient boodle
to despatch a team of shootists to the same destination.
Three months ago the trip was considered a certainty,
yet there isn’t an Australian State with its guarantee
ready ; evidently the public regards this bauble- hunting
with feelings only to be expressed by snorts.
♦ m • •
The projected “ benefit ” to cyclist “ Bobby ”
Walne seems to have eaten itself out of existence. When
the accident happened, there was a rush of people to
sympathise with him, but, since then—with the exception
of his trainer and a few friends —he isn’t thought as much
of as yesterday’s meat-pie.
* * • •
Certain now that top-notch Bananaland bikist,
Bob Walne, won’t race again. Whether his leg gets
thoroughly right or not his mind is made up to give the
game best. Few men have had such a run of accidents.
Since latest trouble happened over a month ago he hasn’t
shifted an inch in his bed at Adelaide Hospital. During
eight years Walne has done some big things cycling, and
perhaps scooped more premierships than any other bikist
on this continent.
* • • *
Bendigo (Vie.) bicycle circuit (which is adver-
tised as first-class) is considered by many racing men as
absolutely the worst and most dangerous in Australia. A
recent meeting there teemed with accidents. The track
is so uneven that it invariably causes a sensation akin to
sea-sickness. The path, which is of good size and shape,
has two straights with a curve at each end, and, as the
rider leaves one straight, he ascends a stiff incline, down
which he comes at a frightful pace. The turns are sharp,
with scarcely any banking, and it’s as exhilarating as
a bull-fight to watch the hair-raising way in which riders
negotiate the curves. There are scores of such tracks in
Australia, and the chief end of cycling councils should be
to investigate these death-traps and obliterate them.
• * * *
May not be generally known that bikist Brown,
who was placed second to Don Walker in recent Druids’
Wheel race, and whom many onlookers thought won by
inches, is in the forties and a veteran rider. Remarkable
feature of Brown’s latest effort was that Walker passed
him, but he came again and stayed with the continent’s
top-notcher ever after.

» * * *
Unlike Australia, Maoriland doesn’t take seri-
ously to the “ harmless ” pastime of boxing, and so it has
taken a considerable amount of persuasion to induce the
M.L. Boxing Association to add a bantam-weight
championship to the list. Hitherto an under 8-stone
pug., if he wanted to have a smack at a 10-stone bully,
had to do so with all odds thrown in. The newly-added
championship will be fought for about the first week in
June, and promises to launch forth with an unusual num-
ber of entries.

ally chopped one-time top-notcher Mick Dooley out in less
than a round at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday night. It

assertion. Those who anticipated Dooley would show
some of his brilliance of 15 years ago were bitterly disap-
pointed, for he simply wasn't in it a moment with the
giant woodchopper, who went straight in, using both
hands, and never left the issue in doubt. Dooley should
not have been resurrected; his showings in Melbourne and
Sydney, as far back as a couple of years since indira»»H

of his stamp don’t improve with age ’ men

Much was expected of Hiscutt and Truscott b,
their fight at Sydney National, Tuesday night last week
but it proved a huge disappointment from end to end
Truscott, with a man jumping away from him and duck
ing and dodging round the ring, didn’t show to near si,eh
purpose as with Lenton, who came for his gruel eve™
time. They cuddled, looked fierce, and then scrambled
hitting any and every way, but mostly missing Trim’
cott poked his left in often, and on that account the
blow was harmless, but when he punched properlv it
made Hiscutt wince. They got into all manner of
entanglements, and tied themselves into weird knots it
was nothing but scuffle from end to end. The onlv clean
hitting was done by Truscott in the eighth round and
then he gave the one-time silver digger a hot couple of
minutes, sending him to the boards repeatedly At
“ corners ” Hiscutt had enough and threw the towel in
Most onlookers sized the show a “schlenter.” Their
methods were too much alike to make the match interest
ing.

There is money in cycle racing. Don Walker
the Red Bird champion during the season just ended’
won £7BB on his Red Bird. The new type of this famous
mount for 1903, which has been specially built for Aus-
tralian track and road work, embodies several new and
practical features which should make it a favorite with
lovers of a speedy, reliable bicycle.*
* * * *
Road riders will be interested to learn that the
Dunlop Tyre Co. has fixed on Saturday, August 22nd
for its great Commonwealth road Tace from Warrnam-
bool to Melbourne— a distance of 165 miles. Application
has been made to the League of Victorian Wheelmen for
the above date, which is a week earlier than last year’s
contest, when over 50,000 people witnessed the progress
of the 100 competitors along the course. Upon the same
day, and over the Warrnambool course, the Dunlop Co
is also organising the first motor cycle race held in Aus-
tralia, so that the 22nd August will be a red letter day in
cycling and motor circles.

After all Tommy Bent’s boasting about
the complete readiness of the Irvine Govern-
ment to face a railway strike it showed its pitiable
unreadiness by serving notices on the old worn-
out pensioners of the Department to prepare to
resume duty. Of course, these 70 and 80-year-old
ancients simply replied by sending in medical
certificates that they were too blind, deaf and
rheumatic to work, and that prop collapsed. But
fanoy the joy of rushing along in an express train
the safety of which depended on a spectacled
Satriarch, who was retired, owing to old age, a
ozen years ago, and who has practically forgotten
the business and the road.
The Argus reports quite cheerfully :
The majority of the settlers on Eurack estate have not
had a good season, and 25 have sent a petition to the
Minister of Lands asking him to postpone their payments
till they have had a good season.
In the same issue which relates this further beg-
ging for State support by the “great natural
industries ” the Abgus denounces the Labor party
as per usual for causing Victoria’s financial
troubles. It looks as if Victoria would have very

N.S.W.’s Foreign-trade Joey Carruthers
says that “the best way to relieve labor is not to
offer it State charity, but to offer it better chances
to work for itself.” Just so ! And to work for
itself at what ? In iron foundries, steel mills,
cotton, silk or woollen factories, distilleries, etc. ?
Where are they ? To go on the land in the west
and centre where a beaten population is already
leaving the land wholesale ? To shear the dead
sheep or drive the dead cow ? To hump ashore
imported goods that the people haven’t the means
to buy? Where does Joey’s fiscal creed present
the opening that Joey talks about so glibly ?

Maoriland Supreme Court has been con-
siderably ruffled over the violent language used
about one of its recent decisions by the British
Privy Council. Chief-Justice Stout and two of
his colleagues, speaking from the bench, have
stated, in effect, that the Privy Council is an old,
ignorant, bumptious, ill-mannered ass. Consider-
ing that Premier Seddon went out of his way to
persuade the British Government to veto those
provisions in the Commonwealth Constitution
which abolished the appeal to the Privy Council,
it is only fitting that that effete body should kick
Seddons grovelsome State first. And it is beauti-
ful irony that, while Seddon was the one Premier
whose reverence for the P.C. was simply abject,
Seddon’s Chief Justice should be the first man to
tell the P. C. what a meddlesome old Shallow it
really is.
A Scottish Charm for Cramp.
Notwithstanding their intense materialism and
shrewd common sense, qualities in which they are
second to none in the world, the Scottish people
have always had a full share of superstition, par-
ticularly Highlanders. Sir Walter Scott had in
his service at Abbottsford a Highland piper
named John Bruce, who, observing that his
master was often afflicted with cramp, ad vised the
distinguished author to sleep upon twelve stones
taken from twelve south-running streams, as that
would infallibly cure him of his malady. Sir
Walter bade the piper obtain twelve such Stones,
a task which cost the piper much time and infinite
trouble. When at last they were obtained, Sir
Walter told the piper that he had looked further
into the matter, and discovered that the charm
was quite worthless unless the stones were
wrapped in a petticoat belonging to a widow who
had never wished to marry again. The task of
obtaining such a garment was too formidable for
the Highland piper to undertake, so the charm
was never tried, and Sir Walter Scott continued
to suffer cramp till the end of his days.
So much for Scottish superstition three genera-
tions ago. To-day when anything ails a native of
“Caledonia, stern and wild,” he or she wastes no
time in hunting for charms, but straightway
resorts to Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. “ For
several years,” writes Mrs. Hutchison, of New-
castle Road, Jesmond, N.S.W., “I suffered
agonies from indigestion and liver complaint. I
could neither eat, sleep, nor work—in fact, did
not know what it was to enjoy a single hour of
freedom from pain. I was attended by two of
the cleverest medical men in the Newcastle dis-
trict, but their treatment failed to bring me any
relief. Indeed, I went steadily downhill, and
begun to fear that my case was beyond the aid of
medicine. I grew weakly and thin, and b< came
dejected, when, two years ago, I was advised to
try what Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup could do
for me. It was a happy decision, for before I had
taken a quarter of the first bottle my health was
much improved. I continued to take the medicine
according to the directions for five weeks, by the
end of which time I was cured. The cure is
evidently a permanent one, for I have remained
well and sound to the present day. I regard
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup as a never-failing
family remedy, and always keep a bottle of it in
the house.”
Mrs. Hutchison is a native of Scotland, and
came to this country about thirty years ago. She
has resided at and near her present address for
twenty years.
Minister Murray of Queensland, fn his
latest breaking out, says it is “ an insult to white
men to ask them to engage in such degrading toil
as the cutting of cane.” Will Murray answer
these questions: (1) Why is cane cutting more
naturally degrading than cutting wheat? (2)
Isn’t it a fact that every industry comes to be
regarded as degrading when Murray’s colored
friends have been long engaged on it, just as
vegetable-growing is now held accursed in some
regions as “Chinaman’s work,” and isn’t that a
good reason for not having niggers, seeing that
they turn honest toil into degradation ? (3) How
dare Murray and his friends advocate bringing
into the country races so depraved that they are
fit for “degrading work’’ that a white man
mustn’t touch ?

This is a story only for biblical students.
He was an eloquent preacher in the suburbs, and
he was harrowing his congregation with a pathetic
amplification of the story of the Prodigal Son.
He pictured him packing his portmanteau ; he
followed him from room to room on his last look
round the old home —
Then he made his way to the open door, and passed
along the path to the gate. And as he went his precious
mother —oh, the influence of a good woman ! —came
beside him, and as she tenderly whispered to him her
last farewells, she slipped into his pocket a little copy of
the New Testament, that hope and joy of so many lives
cast adrift upon this uncertain ocean we call life.
He a defunct bookseller :
Apropos bookseller James Caple and his snuff-box (8.,
18/4/*O3). Caple’s shop was in Collins-street West, Mel-
bourne, opposite the Criterion Hotel. He was a political
bookseller, in the sense that all the politicians on the
fringe frequented the shop. He was the originator of the
“ late fee ”on letters to England. The mail steamers in
the late fifties lay out in the bay. There was no “ late
post ” at G.P.0., the mails closing some hours before the
ship sailed. To accommodate the mercantile community
Mr. Caple established a “ late bag ” in his shop, where,
up to within an hour of the advertised time of sailing,
letters could be left. The usual postage was paid in
stamps, but Caple charged a late fee of one shilling. The
bag he despatched by special messenger to Sandridge, a
waterman’s boat was in readiness, and the bag was
handed to the postal officer on board while the anchor
was being hauled up. The idea caught on ; from a few
letters the number grew to hundreds. For'some months
Caple made a good thing. When the Government stepped
in Caple’s receipts for the day amounted to over ±5O.
That did not represent single letters, as he made bargains
with large firms to take all the letters on hoard at a fixed
fee. The Government saw that legitimate revenue was
going into private hands, and established its own late
bag and fee. Caple put up for North Melbourne at the
general election in 1859, when the second O’Shannassy
Ministry was thrown out, and polled 29 votes. • *

“ Polstan ” : A recent Bulletin par. stated that Pagel’s
horse is supposed to weigh nearly a ton. Writer has
seen the beast, and reckons it to be of the delivery van
class, it being about 15 hands high, and rather lightly
built. It is a tremendous horse—the biggest draught
stallion sort—that comes anywhere near being a ton in
weight, and Pagel doesn’t take on draughts.

“ T.” : First the Pat Mackys and the Dill
Morans will split the Labor crowd. Then the Labor
crowd will recover its senses and be mad. And
when that happens the Pat Mackys and Dill
Morans will start asking again why the working
people don’t go to church.
Sydney Telegraph, last Monday, ex-
plained that Tasmania was prospering splendidly
under the Federal tariff.
Devortport is one of the main outlets on the north-
west of the island for an enormous quantity of produce,
which finds its way principally to Sydney. Since Federa-
tion, too, the trade with Victoria has enormously in-
creased, and everybody on the north-west coast
appears now to be resting in substantial prosperity and
prospective affluence.
Then it went on to say that George Reid was
making magnificent progress _at persuading the
Tasmanians that the Protective features of the
tariff should be abolished, and the N.9.W. and
Victorian markets be thrown open to the compe-
tition of Maoriland and other places, so as to
alter all this obnoxious affluence.
As The Bulletin went to press, the
Victorian railway position was this : The railway
unions had firmly refused the degrading abandon-
ment of their civic rights proposed—with much
cabbage language —by Bent, and the Victorian
Government had made the first move to a back-
down by kicking Bent into the background.
Caliban is no longer allowed to adorn degrading
proposals with degrading language, and Premier
Irvine has taken up the task of trying to compro-
mise with the men. Irvine is a contemptible poli-
tician—in this regard especially for allowing Cali-
ban to rage at large, so long as he thought that that
would succeed in frightening the railway men
to submission —but he is, at any rate, decent
and dignified in his language, and from the Tory,
capitalistic view he is more likely to score points
from the men than Bent. Therefore the men
should be especially watchful. Irvine’s suave
assurance is that if the men throw away the
defence that their affiliation with the Trades Hall
gives them, then their grievances will be inquired
into. This is better than Bent’s language—
the howl of Bent that the men had no
grievances. But the chief grievance, after all, is
that the promises of Irvine’s Government have
been tried, and have proved not worth a rush. It
was that Government, for instance, that cut the
men down to five days’ work and five days’ wages
per week—and then made a large proportion of
them work six or seven days for the five days’
wages. The railway men will be fools if they
give up their rights as unionists under any cir-
cumstances. They will be extra-special fools if
they listen to any more promises by the Irvine
Government until it has redeemed the last lot.

LADIES AND GENTLEMEN.—I desire to intimate that
I am a candidate for election to the office of Director,
and in soliciting your votes and support venture to say
that I possess the necessary qualifications for the position.
My long experience in Mercantile life in connection
with the pastoral industry—admittedly the principal
industry of Australasia, and in which a large portion of
the, funds .of the , society is. invested—warrants me in
stating that I am competent to work in a manner that
will materially add to the prosperity of and stability of our
society, and I respectfully claim your generous support
and interest. I am, Ladies and Gentlemen,
Your faithful servant,
GEORGE MAIDEN,
of Goldsbrough, Mort, and Co., Limited.
Circular Quay, Sydney, Ist May, 1903.

ASTHMA
At Poverty Point.
The Hibernian Catholic Benefit Society objects, and
with justice, to the “ stage Irishman.” Still the stage
Scotchman generally has red whiskers and is very drunk
on whisky; the stage Frenchman is either a grotesque
buffoon or a villainous Count; the stage Australian, a
never-before-seen alleged boundary - rider ; the stage
foreigner in general is a bad character almost all the
time ; and the stage mother-in-law is a howling blas-
phemy. And all these people put up with it.
Truth lately resurrected the silly old fiction that Sims
Reeves was a drunkard whose frequent failures to keep
his concert engagements were due to the bottle. Reeves
was a strictly abstemious man, troubled all his life with
a bronchial affection, which sometimes seized him after
his first song and prevented him appearing in the
•econd half of the programme. At 80 years of age, or
thereabouts, the well-preserved veteran found a young
lady willing to marry him. '
“ F.” : Be the marriage of the Qearl of Yarmouth to a
’Murkan millionaire’s daughter. Melbourne papers speak
of Yarmouth as being remembered only for his skirt
dancing at the local Bijou. The absurd young man
touched his highest point of unconscious humor as a
vocalist. Attired in a pink silk coat, with knee-breeches
and stockings to match, his youthful Nibs warbled
" Knocked ’em in the Old Kent Road.” He didn’t even
attempt to give the coster ditty in coster accent or
manner. He just “ Knocked ’em ” as though he were
striking them with a feather —the funniest travesty of a
coster that ever was.
Dan Barry, when last heard of, was way up in Mackay
telling fairy tales to the local press. One of Dan’s
dramas—“ Run to Earth ’’ —was alleged to have run for
four consecutive months in Melbourne last year, also it
had the alleged distinction of being the only drama wit-
nessed by Melba in Victoria. The narrative went on
gravely to state that Melba wrote a letter to Dan, telling
him that she had seen the same drama played in London,
but was not so impressed as when she beheld it in
Melbourne under Dan’s supervision.
Died, in Melbourne, the other week, Miss Belle Russell
(wife of Jack Parsons, the cyclist), who played child
parts under many Australian managements in the 80’s.
She was remembered as the Prince Edward in Geo.
Miln’s successful production of “ Richard the Third.”
Previously, Belle Russell had been the interesting small
child of numerous melodramas, and her local claim to
the character of Little Eva or Little Willie held good
until she outgrew her usefulness for theatrical purposes.
Then she played the part of Mrs. Parsons in the best pos-
sible way.
The dainty young daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Court-
neidge is making her appearance as one of the four fairy-
lings in “ Midsummer Night’s Dream.” Little Cicely
Courtneidge was born’in Australia just 10 years ago, so
if she should grow to be another Ellen Terry—which is a
promising possibility—this country will claim her for a
native actress by birth and by Shakspearian baptism.
“N.”: Rudinoff, the latest and hitherto perhaps the
most expensive of Rickards’ investments, is an amiable
giant of Polish extraction —painter, singer, siffleur,
shadowgraphist, and showman. Off the stage he is a
painter, and deprecates any allusion to his music-hall
performances. He is engaged in making a competence
that will enable him to work unfettered by the necessity
of pleasing the dealer and the American millionaire.
The Jap. is identifying himself resolutely with his
English ally. A Japanned version of Othello is now on
the boards of some of the Jap. theatres. The scene is
laid in Formosa. Othello becomes Lieut.-General Muro ;
Cassio, Major Katsu-Yoshio ; the unfortunate Desdemona,
Tomone; and lago, Lieut. Iya Gozo ; and Muro chops up
Tomone with his sword instead of smothering her ; and is
allowed to commit seppuku instead of simply stabbing
himself, as does Othello.
“ Alex M.”: I first heard Edward Lloyd lift up his voice
30 years ago. Also Charles Santley. Found Santley a
decided “ gOnoph ” when he sang in Sydney, and expected
—despite local press praise—to find Lloyd also a back-
number. Discovered, however, that he is still in the
present tense ; with much of his old power and brilliancy
in such items as “ Lend Me Your Aid,” and very nearly
all his aforetime sweetness and grace in “Sally in Our
Alley ” and the like. Where Time appears to have most
touched the athletically cherubic Ned’s organ is, strange
to say, at the bottom ; not at the summit, for he can still
hurl off his burly chest as stupendous a high-0 as ever.
And, as was always his way, he stands on the platform
like a man —straight, strong, and easy—and sings as if he
were having a chat with the audience ; an example much
to be commended to the great tribe of dolly, die-away
tenor-warblers.
Alfred Bucklaw, returning to Australia after an ab-
sence of 13 years, finds himself almost forgotten. And
yet he was the Julius Knight of the olden time. He played
the Pointsman and Silver King, and the nautical hero of
“ Harbor Lights,” a part which Charles Knight filled the
last time the drama was revived. There must be a few
seasoned spinsters in the amphitheatre at Melbourne
Princess’s who heave a sigh when Bucklaw, their idol of
1889-90, comes along as the Duke of Athens. He reminds
’em of bygone Midsummer Nights’ Dreams that had no-
thing to do with Shakspeare’s comedy. They loved their
Bucklaw with a love they transferred in due course to
Kyrle Bellew and Julius Knight. - Loved him best in
“ The Bells of Haslemere,” where he wore a dandy white
linen suit, and used to sit down to slow music and say
“ Now, to read Evelyn’s letter.” Hundreds of girls
wished they were Evelyn.
“T.D.” : A well-known Melbourne singer, whose name
is constantly on programmes, tells me that out of con-
certs in the metropolis last year he made. £4O, the rest,
being all charity and advertisement. His income, outside
teaching, came almost exclusively from the country dis-
tricts, which are remarkably liberal to visiting profes-
sionals, considering the size of some of the towns, and
their inability to cram more than a couple of hundred
people into a hall. Also, at a country concert, the local
populace is almost ready to kiss the hem of the singer’s
garment, and certainly buys him numerous drinks,
whereas the Melbourne'advertising dame, whose charity
consists principally of getting an underpaid professional
to “ give his services,” doesn’t even know him again when
she meets him in the street.
The late Chas. Cogill was a good subject for mimicry.
Young Hawthorne of yore, and Albert Whelan used to
reproduce his voice and chuckle to the life.

THERE is nothing gained in employing an un-
practical person to do an expert’s task. Nine
times in ten, EAILURE follows. The average
physician is oftentimes an amateur in the treat-
ment of grave nerve diseases. His time is so fully
occupied in treating a full assortment of human ills
that he cannot devote special study to any particular
branch of practice. Some diseases are sufficiently
simple as to require only the general knowledge
obtained in colleges and general practice.
The More Complex and Perplexing Disorders
are not always favored by the family doctor. He pre-
fers to be relieved of tasks which call for special know-
ledge and special skill.
Dr. RICHARD WALLACE,
Physician-in-Chief.
The true SPECIALIST is a physician who is absolutely expert in the treatment of a few diseases. He knows all
about the complaints he treats, and he supervises difficult cases with the same certainty with which the general practitioner
treats a common ailment.
For over 30 Years we have been treating 1 Serious Diseases.
We began our special work after thorough training in some of the best known colleges abroad. We have cured more
men of their troubles than any association of men in Australia. You can come to us feeling certain that we know just
what to do, that you will be free from malpractice and certain of a cure if one remains for you.
WEAKNESS.

Sound medical treatment under the

or Belt, which pours a current of vital energy
into the weak parts, giving them new life and
strength. Over ninety per cent, of our cases

This “INVIGORATOR,” the best in Aus-
tralia, is ONE POUND ONLY. It is a

in Australian medical practice locally certifica-
ted. Duty is paid free to all Australian States.
VARICOCELE.

Varicocele, can come to us with com-

We will cure you quickly, thoroughly, and
without the need of hospital care.
BLOOD POISON.
There are many phases of this complaint
and its origin is sometimes obscure. No

“ Jaybell ” uses language :
“ L.W.,” in 8., 18/4/’O3, talks about the “ microbe of
credit.” Quite evident he never lived next door to a
family which was in an advanced stage of the disease.
Microbe ? Bosh ! It’s a tapeworm. Am living next door
to one such now, and m.v missus is just worn to the bone
passing borrowed provender, furniture, jewellery, litera-
ture, crockery, and every other conceivable household
requisite over the fence, in the fond belief of having them
returned “ to-morrow.” I’ve rampaged about, in efforts
to stop the domestic leakage, till the neighborhood
rushed its front verandah thinking the Townsville
cyclone was working south. Did I stop the leak ? I was
less than half-way through the row when these plausible
friends came to me and borrowed ss. and our terra-cotta
bust of Baden Powell. And now an apparently sane
individual arises to proclaim that a mere microbe is the
root of the evil. Microbe ! It’s an elephant.

Melb. Argus having’ stupidly let itself be drawn
into a controversy with Tom Mann on the subject
of Socialism, the hopeless paper went blundering
along like an elephant in a bog. Its chiefest
exhibition of absurdity and greed was over the
question of the distribution of profits. It instanced
the case of an inventor, who made a machine that
enabled one employee to do five times as much
work as was formerly possible, and asked: “Is
that increase also to be accredited to labor, and
appropriated by it ?” It put this question in the
tone of one who knocked the problem out in a
single act. And yet there seems very little reason
for the Argus’s wildly triumphal attitude. When
a valuable invention comes to light the inventor
gets something, and he deserves to, but as a rule
he doesn’t get so very much. The rest of the
profit realised must go either to the emplovee
who works his invention or the employer who
exploits it, neither of whom has any relation to
the invention itself. As a general thing, the
employer takes the whole lot of it—in fact’ it is
difficult to remember when he didn’t take the
whole lot of it. And the Argus asks in the
shocked tone of one who bangs the door on all
further controversy, if it is actually suggested that
the employee should get any of it. Would any-
thing break if he did ?

What the Farmer and Miner Gain by
Protection.
In what way do the farmer and miner benefit by
Protection f Extract from letter addressed to Thb
Bulletin from Ballarat.
The above question is asked so often, and asked
in such a supercilious and BRUCK-SMiTH-like tone,
by the Importing Party, that it seems the oft-
repeated answer might be repeated again with
advantage.
(1) The farmer profits by Protection because it raises a
large manufacturing population to buy his produce. He
can’t eat all his produce himself. He can’t sell all the
surplus to importers’ clerks, for there are not enough of
them. He must sell it to the people who make his boots,
hats and clothes, and his wife’s and daughters’ boots,
hats and clothes, also to the people who make his imple-
ments, furniture, whisky, etc. If these people are at his
own door he sells them his potatoes at 6s. per cwt. If
they are in England he can send his potatoes to them
there, and after paying freights, insurance, and middle-
men’s charges, and allowing for depreciation en route, he
gets about 4s. per cwt.
(2) The reason why farmers are comparatively poor in
Eastern Gippsland, and many other places, is that there
are no large manufacturing towns thereabouts to buy
their produce. Protection will supply such towns in
time.
(3) Protection benefits the farmer by supplying him
with locally-produced implements at a cheaper rate than
the importer supplied them. Under Freetrade the im-
porters’ ring charges extortionate prices while there is no
local competition. When local competition starts it cuts
down prices till the local manufacturer is killed off.
Then it starts to be extortionate again.
(4) The farmer benefits by Protection because it creates
manufacturing industries in which some of his sons can
find employment. Everybody can’t be “on the land,”
and if everybody tries to be on the land the competition
for land runs up prices and rents to a hideous extent.
Rack-rented Ireland has long been an example of the
fearful prices people will pay for the use of a few barren
acres when they and their families must go on the land
for want of other occupations.
(5) The fanner benefits by Protection because it pre-
vents other countries, when they have a specially
large harvest, putting their surplus on his market to sell
for what it will fetch and reducing prices to starvation
level.
(6) The miner benefits by Protection because it supplies
him with cheap tools, as mentioned in paragraph 3.
(7) He benefits because it creates manufacturing
industries which employ thousands of people who would
otherwise be glad to take his job at half-rates rather
than starve. And these same manufactures supply work
for his sons when the mines give out.
(8) He profits because adequate Protection would create
ironworks here, and then many hundreds of miners
would find work digging out iron ore. He profits because
if our manufacturing is done here instead of in
Europe and America, then the factories would use
Australian instead of foreign coal, and that would
furnish employment for hundreds of extra coal-miners.
He profits because if our brass-works, and other
metal works, were here instead of in Europe and
America, the metal would be used here; the saving
of freight and charges would increase profits; and
that would allow of better wages. Also, many mines
that could be worked at a small profit if the product was
used here can’t be worked at all when the product has to
pay freight to England or Germany—the freight item
beina-sufficient to turn what would otherwise be a small
profit into a loss.
(9) The miner and the farmer, in short, profit by Pro-
tection because it creates manufactures which supply
them with cheap implements, which buy their products,
which save them freight charges, and which reduce the
competition in their industries by finding work for people
who would otherwise have to compete with them or else
starve. Every toiler profits by the creation of new in-
dustries. If by abolishing Protection you close up an
industry in Melbourne which supports 1000 men, there
are 1000 men to gradually disperse themselves over Aus-
tralia, and compete for everybody’s job, and offer to cut
under everybody’s wages, till even the man who drives a
cow at Port Darwin feels the consequences.
The people who ask what benefit the miner and
farmer receive from Protection shouldn’t narrow
themselves down to that one question. They
might as well ask what benefit the miner and
farmer gain by naval defence seeing that they
mostly he remote from the sea; or by a University
seeing that it is generally far away in the capital;
or by a Government and Parliament as they don’t
sit in the farming districts or on the goldfields.
Protection benefits the farmer and the miner just
as much as it does anybody else. If Australia
made one year’s trial of real Freetrade there
would be suoh a general closing up of manu-
facturing industries, and such desperate compe-
tition, as a consequence, in the remaining indus-
tries, that the Kalgoorlie gold-miner would have
cause to be glad if his wages didn’t fall below £1
a week.
At the conference of pedagogues held at Mel-
bourne in Easter week one J. T. Raw (may his
somewhat uncouth name be held in reverence)
raised the question of the cocky’s slave children—
the fagged-out mites who get up with the first
gleam of light to pursue and milk the cows, and
do all manner of “chores” about the house, a’hd
then trudge long distances to school, where they
try vainly to keep awake, and come back to pur-
sue and milk the cows once more, and do more
“chores” till far into the night, and do their
lessons in between times when they can. And
then another teacher raised his voice to this
effect:—
Mr. S. J. Mbad said tha.t some of these children never
knew what it was to be children. They worked from the
time they were children until they were old men—that
is, if they had the luck to become old men. (Hear, hear).
Some of the men in the country had less consideration
for their children than for their horses.
And yet there is a lower depth than to be a
cocky’s child, and that is to be a State child
“boarded out” to the cocky by a stepmotherly
Government. On this subject a correspondent
writes: —
Let us raise the cry of the waifs and strays, the orphans,
the tiny workers who are boarded out to certain people
by the Govt., which pays so much a week for the keep of
the unfortunates. These little mites are taken, in nine
cases out of 10, with only one object: to be made to work.
I have only known three poor little things of this class,
and these were boarded-out to women with families in the
country. Instead of paying these children for their toil —
the drudgery of the household, scrubbing, message-run-
ning, kid-comforting till all hours of the night—their task-
mistresses (what a driver the average woman is when she
has the whip-hand !) were actually being paid for their
keep. What worried, nervous little midgets they were
too! and how they sobbed themselves into incoherence
when the local vigilance committee, composed of hard,
grim, economical females, just like their mistresses, and
mostly cronies of the mistresses, looked in once a month
to lift their petticoats to see that they were clean, and
questioned them tartly to know if they were quite happy !
Of course, they were quite happy 1 I don’t know what
kind of spanking they would have got if they had been
otherwise. Compulsory happiness is the lot of the State’s
boarded-out child.

Tom Mann, at the unveiling of the Eight Hours?
Memorial, in Melbourne, told the assembled
workers that “one step towards solving the diffi-
culty of new conditions would be to reduce the
hours of labor to six per day.” Is there no
sense left in the land ? Australia imports more
goods for its own consumption, in proportion to
population, than any other country on earth,
because it doesn’t produce enough to supply
itself. It borrows about £5,000,000 or £6,000,000
a year from foreign countries, because it doesn’t
produce enough to pay for its imports. It makes
no attempt to pay off its debts, because it doesn’t
produce enough oo pay them with. And Tom
Mann’s remedy is to produce less.
LIVER.
KIDNEYS.
The Cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago,
Sciatica, Neuralgia, Blood Disorders,
Gravel, Bladder Disorders, Indigestion,
Biliousness, Sick Headache, Anaemia.
HOW TO EFFECT A PERMANENT CURE.
The Kidneys. —Blood is conveyed from the heart to the kidneys through arteries, and it is the
function of the kidneys to filter the blood, and otherwise separate from it water and various im-
purities. These impurities are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter. When the kidneys have. done
their work of purification, the blood enters the veins and traverses the body, accumulating, in its
course, a fresh supply of effete matter produced by the wasting of the tissues. The heart again
delivers the blood to the kidneys, and the process is repeated. Night and day the work of the
kidneys goes on without ceasing.
When the kidneys, through weakness or disease, are unable to perform their duty efficiently,
the blood retains the uric acid and urea which would otherwise be removed, and becomes con-
taminated by these urinary poisons. Nature's method of letting us know that the kidneys are
working indifferently is to make us conscious of the presence of these poisons by causing us to
suffer from Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica, Neuralgia, Blood Disorders,
Gravel, Stone or Bladder Troubles. The commonest first symptom is a
PAIN IN THE BACK.
but it is not necessary to wait for this acute signal. If some urine, passed the first thing in the
morning, is placed in a covered glass and allowed to stand for twenty-four hours and is then cloudy,
shows a sediment like brick dust, is an unnatural colour, or has particles floating about in it, then the
kidneys are weak or diseased, and immediate steps must be taken to restore them to health and
activity.
The Liver. —The health of the liver and of the kidneys is closely connected. It is almost impossible
for the kidneys to be affected and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa. The liver is an automatic
chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or
three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood, con-
verts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood, gradually,
as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is
completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles, which have lived their life and
are useful no longer.
When the liver is inactive or diseased, we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia,
Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders, because the blood is laden with urinary and biliary
poisons to such an extent that it cannot absorb nutriment from the food we eat, red corpuscles are no
longer formed, and it is these red corpuscles which nourish the nerves. In other words the blood is
vitiated and starved.
The importance of keeping the kidneys and liver in vigorous health will therefore be realised,
and an explanation is afforded why
Warner’s Safe Cure
possesses such wonderful power in curing so many seemingly different disorders as those mentioned.
Warner’s Safe Cure possesses the remarkable property of being able to restore the kidneys and liver
to health and activity. All urinary and biliary poisons are then duly removed by the simple process
of Nature and all suffering due to the presence of these poisons in the system necessarily ceases.
Mr. R. H. Blackman, 32 Greek Street, Glebe, Sydney, says About seven years ago I was
suffering from Gout, and was suddenly seized by Sciatica, which attacked me as I was driving, and
the pain became so bad that I had to be carried home. I was advised the next morning to take

Like a Fan in the Autumn-
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the Judge called to see Margaret one Sep-
tember morning, he did so with the full intention
of asking her to be his wife ; but directly he saw
her sitting idly by the open window, her white
hands folded listlessly in her lap, he divined that
the time was singularly ill-chosen for such an im-
portant step. A great measure of his successes
where women were concerned was due to the fact
that he.considered their moods and not his own.
He did not wish to be refused even once, for he
knew that Margaret was a creature of habit, and
would possibly go on refusing him if she once
began. And that one quick glance at her and the
flower-decked room had told him all he wanted to
know.
For one thing, the flowers were wild ones, save
for a bowl of early violets. There were trails of
starry traveller’s-joy and jars of brown-stemmed
fluffy yellow wattle that filled the whole place
with a suggestion of Spring ; and the girl was one
with the flowers in humor, and wanted nothing
that savored of the sere and yellow leaf. The
Judge was a fine-looking man, with level black
brows above the handsomest eyes in the world,
but his dark hair and beard were thickly sprinkled
with grey, and there were tell-tale lines about the
eyes which gave his five-and-forty years away.
And Margaret was very young—a tall, graceful-
looking girl; small-boned and fair of hair and
skin. Not strikingly pretty, perhaps, but with
an air of breeding which the Judge fully appre-
ciated. As the girl turned to greet him he smiled
to think how disappointed his first wife’s sister
would be when he returned home that evening
with no definite news to tell her. For Mr 3. Ford-
ham was set on her brother-in-law’s re-marriage ;
being convinced that marriage even to a girl of
Margaret's tastes and bringing-up would be better
for his income than his present bachelor days.
“I’ve come to say good-bye,” he said. “I
suppose you still intend going to-morrow ? ”
“Of course I do —and, oh! howl am looking
forward to it! We shall not be able to get letters
even. Imagine the perfect peace of it all.”
“Imagine the utter boredom of it,” said the
Judge. “You will be tired of it all in a week,
unless ”
“Continue,” said Margaret. “Unless?”
The Judge shrugged his shoulders. “You may
fill the blank when you return. Your father will
add materially to his collection of beetles while in
the Bunyas. Perhaps you may find something
worth collecting there, also.”
“Perhaps,” said Margaret, her hazel eyes
bright w.th anew light of expectation; “but I
sincerely hope we shall not see a soul. I want to
get away from everyone and everything—l’m so
tired.”
“Brain-tired and body-tired,” said the Judge.
“Well, you will be able to tell us all about the
rest cure when you return.”
“Perhaps I never shall return.”
“Perhaps not—it is quite possible. But if you
do return I shall call —I mean to ask you to marry
me then.”
Margaret frowned and abruptly changed the
conversation. “It was good of you to give so nice
a farewell last night,” she said. “I enjoyed it
all.” . v
“I hope you noticed the crape on the box and
the mourning note in the table decorations.”
“Oh! was that what the violets and camellias
meant —they were very pretty at any rate, and I
liked ‘San Toy’—it was bright and sparkling.”
“ Both adjectives appropriate —they accentuate
another equally appropriate one you haven’t used.
You should say, ‘So bright, so sparkling, so
pathetic.’ ”
•“ Pathetic ! Search it all through and you will
not find one pathetic part.”
“ Do you remember the comical Chinese student
in the bee-hive hat who makes futile love to San
Toy?”
“ Yes ; but he was only comic.”
“But his speech was pathetic. * You shall not
cast me aside like a fan in the autumn.’ There
are all sorts of possibilities of passion in that little
sentence, even if the speaker did look fairly
comical.”
“ How cold you are!” said Margaret, raising her
eyebrows; and then she blushed suddenly and
swiftly, so that the Judge was fain to reassure
her.
“ I wasn’t thinking of myself, Margaret. I here
are some fans one keeps just for the value of the
gilding and the ivory, even after one has no
pressing need for them. ’’
Margaret looked slightly puzzled. “ Of course,
one only throws away the cheap fans,” she said
vaguely.
“Of course,” assented the Judge.
• *
For the first week of tent-life under the shadow
of Moherland, 4000 ft. above sea-level, in the heart
of the Bunya forests, Margaret was very happy.
She was thoroughly tired out by a season of
unusual dissipation during a royal visit to Poin-
settiatown ; and to strolF about from one fern-
gully to another, and rest on a sofa of fern-leaves
when she was tired, was delightful. Her father -
an indefinite-looking, sandy-haired man of unex-
ceptionable birth and a small independent income
—had an all-absorbing passion for Coleoptera, and
was wont to go hunting for specimens in all parts
of Queensland ; but this was the first tune his
daughter had ever shown a wish to accompany
him. , . ,
Doubtless the Spring weather had got into her
blood, or the opportunity had come just as she
was in one of her “romantic” moods. At any
rate, here she was—far from the haunts of man,
and apparently quite contented to be so.
However, after the first week she grew strangely
restless. She was craving for the atmosphere of
male adulation in which she had basked since her
miss-in-the-teens days, and she missed the thou-
sand and one little attentions the Judge had
given her so naturally and so unobtrusively. In
the tops of the tall bunyas the scrub-doves were
cooing and calling to each other, and the restless-
ness of the waking year was over everything.
The glory of expectation animated every breaking
bud and uncurling leaf. And so, one evening
when a man walked out of the mystery of the
dark bunya-stems into the circle of light round
the brushwood fire —the girl felt as if she had
known long before of his coming. He was a good-
looking young man, with a shy, reserved manner,
and showed flashing white teeth when he smiled,
which wasn’t often ; and he was dressed in that
most becoming of masculine garbs—moleskins,
gaiters, crimeau shirt, and cabbage-tree hat. His
appearance fitted in with the sylvan scene, and
the girl shuddered to remember that but half-an-
hour before his advent she had caught herself
longing to hear the Judge’s firm footstep. She
could never imagine the Judge in any guise except
that pertaining to the silk-hatted, frock-coated
man of fashion who would be so out of place in
the wilderness.
The man who had suddenly stepped into her
life —and, to tell truth, into her heart as well
—was a romantic-loooking person. He was
camping the other side of Moherland; had lost
his way while out shooting, and had made for the
light of the fire. He volunteered to show the girl,
next day, a trellis-work house, formed of matted
liana-creepers round the crumbling bole of a
forest giant; and that was the only one of many
excursions. The beetle-hunting father never
meddled with anyone, least of all with his own
daughter; and the cook and the black boy who
shot for the pot had possibly neither of them ever
heard of Mrs. Grundy. As for the man and
Margaret, it was simply a case of youth calling to
youth—with the girl’s romantic mood to shed a
glamor everything. The man was slow of
speech, and had a slightly Scotch accent. He told
Margaret that he was in business in Poinsettia,
and had a fortnight’s holiday—the first for years.
She stopped further confidence, for it suited her
“ romance ” to take him just as he was. In the
back of her mind was a vague conviction that he
worked in a bank or on some accountant’s busi-
ness, and she quite imagined how her friends (in-
cluding the Judge) would pity her for becoming
engaged to a poor man. For very soon her
apparent admiration called out the passion which
lay deep, though dormant, in his breast; and, know-
ing her position in the world was the upper one,
she stooped most sweetly to conquer, and broke
through the lad’s Scotch reserve just when she
chose to. Then, one day in the tree-fern gully,
where a stream ran noisily, yet unseen because of
the tangled mass of maiden-hair above it, he
kissed her; and no doubt in that supreme
moment both imagined that Heaven was reigning
upon earth at last.
That very night, people on the moonlit plains
below the Bunya Mountain saw the white clouds
lying like snow on the topmost crests, and pre-
dicted “ weather ” ; and, soon afterwards, Mar-
garet and her father were obliged to shift camp
hurriedly, and come down to the sawmills to
get a trap to take them to the railway-station,
many miles away. Margaret’s lover had left the
evening before, and it was arranged that he should
come to see her in Poinsettia the following Satur-
day.
The Judge called soon after her return. He
was gravely amusing, and told her many odds
and ends of interest about what had taken place
during her absence. He also brought her the
latest book, and did not discuss it too learnedly.
Margaret was not clever, and the Judge never
bored her with a show of his keen criticism. He
was intellectual giant enough to be able to suit
himself to the veriest dwarf in that line, and he
loved the girl none the less for her limited mental
powers. But Margaret—with her heart far off in
a fern-lined valley, living over again a certain
incident —was rather distant; and, when the
Judge called again, she was “out.” Really out,
too; for she was restless and excited, and had
suddenly decided that, as her life was to run on
simpler lines in future, she must learn to do her
own shopping, and take an active interest in the
price of groceries. Instead of being chatelaine of
the Judge’s charming mansion, she would reign
supreme in a pretty rose-covered cottage, and
would dispense tea and cake to her friends, and
show them how happy life could be on £5OO a
year—or probably three.
“ You need not give the order, John —I’m going
in myself,” she said, as her smart little chariot
drew-up at the Co-operative Stores in Alexander-
street. “Very good, miss,” said John, showing
nothing of the surprise he felt. Margaret rustled
into the store and up to the grocery-counter; and
there —clad in a coarse apron, his shirt sleeves
rolled up, and a long knife in his hand—was her
lover of the Bunya forests, slicing rashers from a
side of bacon under the supervision of a keen-eyed
German woman.
He smiled his quick, joyful, dazzling smile, and
finished with the customer before he came to the
girl. “I thought you’d find me out,” he whispered.
“Oh, but the time has been long without you,
my wee girlie—knowing I’d not be free before
Saturday. I dreamed all day that you would
come.”
Margaret felt herself turning cold to the tips of
her fingers.
“ Yes ! yes !—of course,” she murmured. “I
called to make sure that you would come. I—l’m
husy now —very busy ’’ —and she gathered up her
skirts and fled with burning cheeks and a hideous
conviction in her mind that the young grocer’s
assistant had followed her to the carriage-door
and patted her gloved hand, or raised it to his
lips, or done something equally dreadful before
the all-seeing eyes of John.
How she got through the day she never knew —
she wrote letter after letter, and tore them up.
Then came Saturday evening, and her lover,
looking decidedly unpolished in his suit of ill-
fitting Sunday clothes. She wondered now, in
the sudden revulsion of feeling, how she had ever
cared for him—it all seemed so utterly impossible.
And, whether it was the large and beautifully-
appointed house which brought things into focus
for him, or something indefinable and chilling in
the girl’s manner, certain it is that the truth
dawned upon the young Scotchman, and he gave
Margaret very little trouble in the order of his
going. He was a man of few words, and Margaret
cried herself to sleep that night when she thought
of the dull misery on his face as he went out of
the flower-scented room into which he had come
so full of hope. She could not even console her-
self with the idea that he had tried to deceive her ;
to his simple mind class-difference mattered
little—to her so much. It was out of the question
—so utterly, hopelessly impossible. He would
never understand—never ! She loved him no
longer ; and that was all he knew.
The Judge called again, but not until a week
had passed ; and, when he left the house, it was
as Margaret’s affianced husband.
A dark-faced man was leaning against the
railings, heedless of the passers-by, his eyes fixed
oil the windows of Margaret’s sitting-room. Every
afternoon as the western sunbeams touched her
casement-garden Margaret was wont to lean across
the sill and move the plants into shadow.
The dark-faced man watched her for a while —
then he turned and walked swiftly away, and the
Judge paused on the topmost step and watched
him go.
“ * Like a fan in the autumn,’ ” he quoted softly.
Then he stopped again to extract a cigarette from
his case and added, “ How long would she keep
the other, I wonder, if it were not fashioned of
ivory and inlaid with gold.”
From the beginning of things reason has -ver
been but handmaid to desire; and doubtless the
Judge had no inclination to solve the riddle he had
himself propounded.
ETHEL MILLS.
What has China got against Ireland ? If
you want to disturb John’s placidity call him
“Paddy,” and you have a dancing, infuriated
demon whirling a bamboo, and spluttering un-
known curses with the velocity of a steampipe.
“G.A.P.”: Just struck something new,
or, rather, it struck me. Last Monday, the wife
being out, I answered a door-knock and discovered
a patriarchal-looking bloke weeping on the mat.
As he seemed too agitated to speak, and the
gathering crowd began to suspect me of something
horrible, I invited his nibs in to continue his
weeping on the hearth rug. Between weeps I
elicited that his wife had just died and left him
with 11 kids—the four youngest being twins. He
was collecting money for the funeral, and had I
any old clothes, &c. Gave him two bob and went
upstairs to look for duds. When I returned the
old and affected one was gone, and after consider-
ing the number of things that were gone also (I
have already missed 47 articles), I reckon he must
have had eleven similar persons weeping round
the corner and waiting to assist him, or else he
was a single-handed house remover. I reckon the
first theory is the correct one. Has anyone seen a
drove of 12 old men weeping in the street lately ?
A philosopher has to learn what a fool
knows by instinot.
“ J.F.D.” : Re the disadvantages of being
big. Was in King-street one day during the last
test match. My companion stood 6ft. 2in. and
weighed eighteen stone and because he happened
to obstruct the view of a venomous little sawed-off
atom who stood behind, the little man started to
say things. This amused the crowd which backed
him up for his supposed “ grit. ” Finding himself
strongly supported he got worse and in sheer
desperation the big man hit him a tiny little
hit and the four-footer rolled into the gutter.
How did that crowd hoot? “Why don tyer ’it
someone yer own size?” “Git the little ’un a
step ladder to git up to ’im, ” “ Stand’im on
somebody’s shoulders so he can get a chance ” etc.
And the cocksparrow pranced around well out of
reach of the big man’s right, and used horrible
language, and the crowd roared for him. We go*i
away as soon as a chance offered and my com-
panion said ‘ ‘ that’s the disadvantage of being big;
the crowd’s always against you. If I hadn't hit
him they would call me a cur, and when I did they
called me a bully. Wish to the Lord I was only
s£ft.”

NFLUHNZA only seizes upon those whose systems have become
run down and weakened. Those who keep in the pink of con-
dition snap their fingers at it. Liver chill, colds, attacks of
shivering, and similar ailments have one common origin, namely,
a debilitated condition of the body. When the supply of energy
is adequate, the pulse vigorous, the digestion good, colds and
chills cannot get a hold. Once the vitality becomes lessened
the evils just named creep in. BILE BEANS will keep the
body in the “pink of condition.” They act directly upon the
liver, and end that cause of so many ailments—Constipation.
They stimulate the circulation, improve the digestion, and
increase the energy of the whole system. Just as one illustration
take the following case :
“ I was laid up a few months ago for about three weeks, with a severe
attack of Influenza,” says Mr. W. Tindale, of 28 Little West Street, Darlinghurst,
Sydney. “My body ached all over, especially in the back and head. I lost my appetite
entirely, was very feverish and slightly delirious, and sleep was out of the question. I
tried several remedies, including the usual household treatment, and also consulted a
doctor, but made very slow progress towards recovery. I was advised by a friend who had
used your medicine to give Bile Beans a trial. I acted on this advice, and have much
pleasure in stating that, after taking a few doses, I gained immediate relief, and, continuing
with them, I was quickly restored to my usual health. I can strongly recommend Bile
Beans to anyone suffering from a similar complaint.”
BILE BEANS have now a world-wide reputation for curing Biliousness, Headache,

Two Shoes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You are standing on my heart,
Two shoes;
With your tiny toes apart,
Two shoes.
I am thinking of the girl
On whose feet you used to twirl
In the dance’s dreamy whirl,
Two shoes.
I am thinking of the days,
Two shoes,
When we walked Love’s shining ways,
Two shoes.
I am thinking of the feet
And the ankles, small and neat—
Oh ! my thoughts are sad and sweet,
Two shoes.
I am thinking of the stars,
Two shoes,
That are shining on our bars.
Two shoes;
They are shining brightly yet,
And my eyes are dim and wet—
There are things we can’t forget,
Two shoes.
There are nights upon the sand,
Two shoes ;
Happy claspings hand in hand,
Two shoes ;
There are kisses by the sea
That come misting back to me
As I sit here silently,
Two shoeg.
Through the smoke-wreaths from my pipe,
Two shoes,
T can see two lips a-ripe,
Two shoes;
I can see a curving breast
And a head all sheeny .tressed—
'Comes a star-sweet mouth a-quest,
Two shoes !
Like a cool wind on my brow,
Two shoes,
Blows the'memory of her now—
Two shoes.
I am muttering her name—
O, I christened her that same !
In our happiness’ sweet flame—
“ Two Shoes.”
She is dear and dead and gone,
Two shoes ;
She I used to put you on s
Two shoes ;
And I sit a man alone
On a memory-jewelled throne,
While my heart plays telephone,
Two shoes.
Words of hers still heart ward roam,
Two shoes;
Down the stream of thought they foam,
Two shoes!
Dear dead lips and mine are met,
And my eyes are dim and wet—
O ! I love you— love you yet,
“ Two Shoes ” !
W.A.
GRANT HERVEY.
The Mailman’s Ghost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I belave,” said Lanigan, “in your white-
sheeted, pumpkin-headed ghosts about as much
as I do in yire dthrawing-room inthroductions,
an’ that’s moighty little. But I recognise th’
existence of a shupernatral a-agency, which, I
presume, is entoirely th’ property av th’ Almighty.
An example of this came undther my observation
a few years since ; though little use it is spalting
of th’ shuperior forces to onbelaving blaguards
like yez.
“ It’s a mather of twinty years ago since Larry
Cahoun carr-yed th’ mail betune Johnson’s Flat
and Wall Town. I remimber th’ ould fellow
Well, an’ his pony, Daniel O’Connell, a bay wid a
peculiar white marruk down his near shouldther
—a marruk ye wouldn’t aisly forgit, d’ye moind
me now. What Larry didn’t know Daniel
G’Connell did, an’ th’ rest didn’t matther
much. Larry was more thrusted than the
bank, and better known than the pra-aste.
an’ God forgive ’em, more welcome. Whin Larry
eonthradicted the pa-apers, iviry mahn on th’ flat
who could hould a pen sint worrd to tlfl editors
that they were rank obstructors of th’ truth, an’
their pa-apers a fraud an’ a delusion. An’ whin
Larry agreed wid the press, folks ordthered an
exthra copy an’ paid a week’s score off their
account.
“ Wan year a moighty big flood arose, an’ the
creek extinded for miles on aither bank. But
divil a flood or fire kept Larry off juty. He rode
airly in th’ mornin’ from Johnson’s with th’ Wall-
town mails —an’ he niver rode back ! Nayther
him nor the pony were iver seen again. I con-
sidther they were carr-yed down to th’ big river,
and swept out to say. Indade, it was a terrible
calamity to th’ counthry. Johnston’s wint into
mourning for three da-ays, an’ whin it was over
there wasn’t enough liquor left on th’ Flat to float
a corruk.
“D’ye moind me now: Tin years afther the
big flood Johnston’s Flat, which had been re-
baptised Alexandthrew, or some other atrocity, by
a disreputable politician, was thrown into great
consthernation by a matter which I am about to
communicate to yez.
“There was a young gur-rul on th’ Flat named
Katie Shehan, th’ bonniest female in th’ dis-
thrict. She took up wid a harum-scarum black-
guard called Jack Bolton, an’ what was the worse
for Katie’s chance o’ matrimony, a Prodesthant;
for, av course, her parents were against the union.
Bolton threatened this and that, an’ Katie nearly
cried her eyes out till old Shehan, and an uncle of
Jack's, planked down twinty sovereigns each to
take him up north to try his luck on th’ mines.
To give the boy credit, he correspondid wid Katie
wid th’ grea-atest regularity th’ furrust year ;
thin his letthers suddenly shtopped. For months
an’ months that poor little thing wa-aited, an’
hoped, an’ pra-ayed, but never a worrud oame to
comfort her from th’ north.
} “Well, wan winther’s afternoon a hawker
(found Katie lyin’ unconscious on th’ road betune
j Johnston’s an’ th’ creek. A Walltown docther
brought her round for a quarter of an hour before
she expired in her mother’s arrums.
“From her disjynted narrative they discovered
that a horrusman overtuk her. He had a bag
rowled up in front of th’ saddle.
“ ‘ Are ye Katie Shehan ? ’ he says.
I “‘I am that same,’ sez she.
, “‘I have a letther here,’ he says, ‘for Miss
,Katie Shehan,’ he sez, ‘ which I will deliver forth-
with,’ he sez, ‘ aga-ainst th’ rules though it be,’ he
sez. Jist that, an’ no more. Thin he gives
Katie th’ letther into her own hands an’ disappears
round a turrn in th’ road. But whin they ques-
tioned Katie as to the con-tents of the letther,
which, d’ye moind me, had entoirely dishappeared,
she was so overcome that they were forced to
desist. But jist before Katie closed her oyes on
this worruld, ould Shehan approached her bed-
side. She was his wan choild, an’ afther they laid
her away in th’ cemetery he niver held his head up
straight, they do say.
“‘Katie,’ he sez, ‘tell me, fwhat sorrut of a
florrus,’ he sez, ‘ was it that mahn rode,’ he sez.
“ ‘I think,’ she sez faintly, ‘it was a bay, but
there was something else about it, ’ sez she, ‘ that
I shall niver forget,’ she sez.
“‘An’ fwhat was that, ma honey?’ sez ould
Shehan.
“ ‘ There was a white marruk,’ she says, ‘ on its
shouldther,’ she sez, ‘an’ it was the shouldther
next to ,’ and wid that th’ death trimble tuk
her.
“ ‘ In th’ na-ame av th’ Grea-ate God,’ sez ould
Shehan, ‘ It was the wraith av Larry Cahoun.’
“ A few days afther they buried Katie a letther
arrived addressed to Miss Katie Shehan. It was
from a mate o’ Bolton’s telling how Jack had bin
shpeared in a scrimmage wid th’ blacks. It also
contained a lock of Jack’s hair an’ a farewell
worrud fer Katie.
“Now, if any of ye blaguards don’t belave
fwhat I’ve tould yez, go an’ say that sa-ame up at
Johnston’s Flat, an’ the bhoys ’ll drum ye from
there to Wall Town wid clubs —d’ye moind me?”
Then Lanigan gathered up his six-and-a-half
feet of mostly bone and prepared for bunk.
C. H. READE.
Explanation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Fitz ! ”
Barbara’s voice gives notice, and quits her
suddenly. Then she remembers that she is never
going to speak to John Fitzgerald again. She
takes a weak step past him.
“ Barbara!”
There is one man in Melbourne who says
“ Barbara !” in that tone of voice; “ Barbara ! ”
—one man in the world.
A cloud of dust envelopes them. The sky is
growing saffron. School-boys make shields of
their hats and stagger hidden-faced against the
fence. They enter the gardens with a crisp swirl
of elm leaves at their feet.
“ Well! ” says Fitz.
It is not well. It is ill. The wind blows the
tails of Barbara’s sable to the back of her neck.
It makes a wilderness of her curls. Her skirts
are driven against her limbs. Her umbrella has
evident intentions of turning inside out.
Under the circumstances, it is difficult to pre-
serve a strictly cold and dignified exterior.
“ Well ? ” retorts Barbara.
Now that they have exchanged names and
“ wells,” there appears little to be said. These
flashes of silence threaten to become painful.
Fitz. has treated her shamefully. Two years—
and not a word. And he hasn’t the grace to
appear ashamed of himself.
‘‘ I expect you had a good time, ” volunteers
Babs, stiltedly.
“Very.”
Her heart sinks. Barbara wishes she could hate
Fitz. She studies him furtively through a dust
cloud, admiring his walk and profile. Even his
abominable air of nonchalance. Fitz catches her
in the act and smiles.
‘ ‘ Missed me ? ” he queries.
Barbara is afflicted with sudden deafness.
Fitz pitches his voice in a higher key, and re-
peats —
“Missed me?”
She is so taken aback that she stammers —
“Yes !” Fitzgerald’s smile broadens.
“I missed Babs,” he tells her.
To Barbara’s horror she blushes. What a tame
cat he will think her ! Where is her pride—her
dignity, her—her—maidenhood—
“ Awfully ,” he continues plaintively.
“ Perhaps that’s why you wrote so often.”
Thank goodness her voice sounds spiteful. She
feels more full of tears than spite. Why on earth
does she love Fitz? Why on earth does Fitz
make a catspaw of her ?
“ Glad you liked my letters,” he replies,
through the dust.
What a fool she is ! Now Fitz will never ex-
plain. He will persist that he did write letters—
often !
“Oh ! don’t be horrid ! ” Babs falters.
“ Am I horrid ? ” asks Fitz.
The sky is white now. The baoks of the elm
leaves are white. The whirling dust clouds are
white with scraps of paper.
“Ami?”
The gardens are empty.
He draws very close. Babs feels his arm against
her arm. Her skirts against his knees .. . And
he is asking her if he is horrid !
A dust cloud intervenes.
“What ‘lost-to-sight-to-memory-dear ’ sort of
people we are ? ” she laughs, temporisingly.
“Am I horrid ! ” Fitz repeats.
“ You’ve been cruel,” she whispers.
“Yes?”
“Oh Fitz ! You’ve hurt me. . . . You hurt
me ! ”
“Don’t say that! ”
“ Why did you ? ”
‘ 1 Did I ? —why am I horrid ? ”
They pause for breath behind a statue and a
rose bush.
“ Why horrid ?” says Fitz for the fourth time.
“You know you don’t think I am ! ”
That’s the worst of it! Babs doesn’t. And he
knows it!
So she just sobs.
The sky becomes a scheme of orange pink. She
gets a mouthful of dust and —a kiss sub. rosa._ r
Not till she gets home does she
Fitz explain ? ” '
She thinks he must have. She ia-sA very happy.
And she laughs.
And Fitz in the dust storm laughs too !
Did he explain ?

Of interest to the local oritic person is the cabled item
that 'London Daily Express has been ordered, by an
English Court, to pay Dramatist Ganthony £2OO for sug-
gesting that Actor-dramatist Charles Hawtrey had re-
written a “ Message from Mars.” More or less, every
modern and ancient play is padded, re-written, or con-
siderably pruned by the various theatre authorities to
suit themselves (not always to the play’s advantage), but
the Daily Express was probably foolish enough to also
“ suggest ” that Ganthony’s play was improved by such
doctoring.
“ 5.T.8.”: My innocent remark that Nellie Farren
must have been meant, when Kate Vaughan was credited
with playing street-arab parts, has brought down two
distinct contradictions in B. 11/4/’O3. “ J.A.C.” repeats
that it was really K.V. that he meant, and that he often
Baw them both “back in the ’7o’s.” Well, so did 1, a
little later ; saw K.V. in all sorts of parts, from a slave
(with dance) to an old English comedy heroine (with
minuet), but cannot imagine her as a street boy. How-
ever, one can’t prove a negative, whereas “ J.A.C.” has
only to name the plays in which she took that kind of
part. “ E.F.” apparently agrees with me as to Kate
Vaughan, hut won’t have Nellie Farren as a street boy
either i I really did not mean Fanny Leslie or Jennie
Lee, as he suggests. N.F., as shown by her “ Nan, the
Good-for-Nothing,” was just the person to play (as far as
any woman can) the kind of part. What I take my stand
on, and believed the original par. to refer to, was her
“Aladdin,” about 1883. The street boy’s song (“Please
to hold your nag, sir. Take your little bag, sir”) was
simply the talk of London. She didn’t dress the part.

What diplomatists are these concert tenors—some of
them 1 In the art of getting a benefit your tenor—veil,
e tak ze cake. Now that Walter Kirby has at last packed
up the proceeds of his ben. and gone to Europe, there is
a whisper in Melbourne that Napoleon Boffard ze irr-re-
pressible Boffard—will arrange a farewell complimentary
concert unto himself in June, by way of breaking the
news that he has a subsequent departure in contempla-
tion. Should Boffard carry out the second part of this
fell intention, surely darkness will fall upon Melbourne
Block, and the thresholds of the music-shops will be rent
in twain.
Basso Peterkin, of the Lloyd company, who is a very
fine singer indeed, marvels at the rush of would-be stars
from these parts to London, and laments the inevitable
fate of most of them. He has been singing over ten years
in London, “ and I’m just beginning to get known.” Yet
he was singing in Scotland before that, and probably
knew most of his business before ever he tackled the
Great Smoke. Lloyd himself was for several years glad
to pick up fees of a guinea a time. The people who go
from here, after being used to their three guineas or so,
turn up their noses at the smaller dole when it first
comes their way in London. Often a time comes when
they are only too glad to be afforded a show to sing for
nothing.
